THE RED PAGE
THE BEGGAR WHO CHOSE.
Your mind is like an April dawn,
As clear, and clean, and cool, and sweet,
And all your lines are thoroughbred
From forehead smooth to narrow feet.
Dear lady of the high-poised head
And laughing eyes that veil their pride,
My life would be a perfect thing
If one like you were by my side.
.But since I have not much to give,
And since to beg I am—unfit,
Our lives will never touch, and you
Will never know I thought of it.
And token you can't afford such things
It's useless to regret them —
None hut the hrave deserve the fair;
None but the rich men get them!
N.S.W.
DOROTHEA MACKELLAR.
Illusions About War
Since the fall of Napoleon, Britain has
not been seriously afraid of armed invasion.
For a hundred years she has been undis-
puted mistress of the seas, with a navy equal
to the combined fleets of any two other
powers. To-day the position is different. The
German navy, which began only a few years
ago, has grown rapidly, and keeps on grow-
ing. Soon it will be equal in size to the
navy of Britain. The British statesman
and the man in the street regard this new
fleet with apprehension. All the signs and
tokens seem to indicate that it can have only
one objective—an attack on Britain. Du
Maurier’s play, Blatchford’s pamphlet, and
a large part of the press have shocked the
British into a state of alarm. Peace advo-
cates and fierce opponents of military esti-
mates have been converted. In three years
a Liberal Government nas increased
the enormous naval vote by more than
£12,000,000.
Into that country on the verge of panic
at the end of 1909 there dropped a booklet
called Europe s Optical Illusion, by Norman
Angell. The author’s name (now stated to
be Ralph Norman Angell Lane, of the Paris
Daily Mail) was unknown to the public
Yet no publication of recent years has had
such an important effect in so short a time.
Norman Angell wrote about war, and par-
ticularly about the consequences of a wax
between Britain and Germany. Many strong
words have been uttered against war;
many writers have stripped off the romantic
tinsel and shown war in all its naked hor-
ror; but, as Oscar Wilde said in one of his
essays, “so long as war is regarded as
wicked, it will have its fascinations—when
it is looked upon as vulgar it will cease to
be popular.” Could anything be more vulgar
to the Britisher than a business that doesn’t
pay? War, according to Norman Angell,
does not pay the conqueror, and his forcible
argument has convinced many who sit in
high places, and even the militant Attorney-
General of Australia.
The remarkable pamphlet has been ex-
panded somewhat, and is now published as
a book —The Great Illusion —and sold for
2s. 6d. net. It has been translated into
various languages, favorably received by
most newspapers, and an enthusiast has
given a large sum to increase its circulation.
It is a book which everyone should read. It
is an argument that every sane man would
wish to find true.
The ordinary peace advocates, like most
idealists, set up a moral law against the
natural law, and offer a counsel of perfec-
tion. Angell brushes them aside, for they,
with the belligerents, assume that war is
profitable to the conqueror. He finds the
same assumption implicit in all the maxims
of modern statecraft. He claims that this
is “a gross and desperately dangerous mis-
conception, partaking at times of the nature
of an optical illusion, at times of the nature
of a superstition.” In brief, his case is
that: —
No nation can destroy or greatly damage
the trade of another, since trade depends
upon the existence of natural wealth and
a population capable of working it.... An
invader could only destroy trade by destroy-
mg the population (which is impossible),
and if he could destroy the population he
would destroy his own market (which would
be commercially suicidal).
If an invasion by Germany did involve
\ the collapse, of the British Empire, German
capital w r ould also disappear in large part
and 'German credit also collapse—because
ot the delicate interdependence of credit-
built finance and industry.
For allied reasons, the exaction of tribute
from a conquered people has now become
an economic impossibility, the exaction of a
large indemnity of doubtful benefit to the
nation receiving it, the capture of the carry-
ing trade of another nation impossible, and
the conquest of the British colonies would
be ot no advantage to the conqueror.
Angell brings evidence to show that in re-
cent wmrs. the winner is really the loser,
b ranee is in a better financial position than
Germany; the Japs are poorer, not richer,
for their war; since the defeat or Spam by
America, Spanish securities have doubled
in value; and, since England added the
goldfields of the world” to her “possessions,”
British consols have dropped 20 points.
The colonies, such as Australia, are not
“possessions” of material advantage to the
nominal owner; they must be left alone, as
G. P. Lucas said, “whether they are rigl
or wrong—still more perhaps when they ar
wrong.” If Germany took over Britain
position and continued the same policy, sh
would not be commercially better off tha
she is now.
All this, however, would not dispose
the justification for war or prove that Ger
many would not gain in ways which wouli
compensate for material loss if she con
quered Britain. Angel! therefore attack
the psychological case for war. He show
that human nature is not unchanging, but i
steadily changing in those characteristic
which made man a fighting animal; h
brings to bear* proof that the warlike na
tions do not inherit the earth, and that th
struggle for survival is not of man wit!
man, but of mankind with the universe. In
this struggle, man is moving away from th
resort to physical force, and tending more
and more to eo-operation; thereby extending
the international community of interes
which, in the case of the financial world
had been advanced at the beginning of the
book as upsetting all old ideas about the
practical advantages of war.
The second part of Angell’s book is closely
reasoned, and carries conviction. His case
however, depends upon the first part—upon
the statement that the financial interests of
the great nations are so closely interwoven
that war is as damaging to the winner as
to the loser. It is undoubtedly true that
the growth of international investments has
brought about a condition in which a finan-
cial crisis in one country is felt in another;
and a severe blow to British credit would
spell disaster to many people in France,
Germany, America, and other places. Yet one
can imagine that the destruction of the Chan-
nel' fleet and the occupation of London
would secure all that the Germans might
desire without shattering British credit and
reacting dangerously on Germany. The cash
indemnity received by Germany from France
may have, in the long run, injured German
trade; but the individuals through whose
sticky fingers the boodle passed would have
no objection to indemnities. Nor would the
sons, cousins and nephews who received
official positions in the captured provinces
complain about the spoils of war.
All that Angell has said in a late chapter
to prove that a State is not a person, but a
collection of individuals, seems to have been
overlooked by him in the earlier chapters.
AH the people, or the nation as an entity,
might not gain by conquest; but some of the
people would. Most wars have been brought
about by a few of the people even when the
nation as a whole has been “fanned into
an hysteria,” as Masefield put it. Many be-
sides the standing armies have a vested in-
terest in war—the makers of guns, ships and
ammunition, the contractors, speculators and
adventurers—a host in themselves. Whether
the nation win or lose, they generally stand
to gain.
But even if a war between Germany and
Britain be unprofitable, Angell’s argument
does not apply to war between a great na-
tion and a small one, to the annexation of
Bosnia by Austria or to a fight for a pro-
vince like India. In disposing of the sup-
position that a foreign nation would gain by
the acquisition of Britain’s colonies, he ig-
nored India, which is not in the same politi-
cal position as Australia or Canada. That
brightest jewel of Britain’s crown” has
yielded an enormous tribute to some Brit-
ishers, who do not seem to have violently
objected to it; her 200 millions of people
afford a market for British produce, and
support an army of British civil servants.
Any power capturing India and occupying
the same place, as Britain, would be pre-
pared to receive these advantages, and
chance the consequences..
Coming to the position of Australia in
relation to such a power as Japan, Angell’s
Great Illusion gives us no hope of security.
It is quite conceivable that it would pay
Japan to annex Australia, or part of it. Our
territory would be of use to her surplus pop-
ulation ; our wealth, developed and latent
would be serviceable to many of the Japs’
and, if we had to pay an indemnity, it could
be used to relieve the Japs from oppressive
taxation without dislocating their trade.
In fact, Angell’s generalisation will not
stand much strain. It is one of those things
Sfcrlf/n; good *° be true ' Nevertheless
1 he Great Illusion is a very valuable book
and by tar the most notable contribution of
lecent years to the anti-war propaganda.
It clears away many of the mists which have
gathered around the subject of war, and em-
phasises the movement of humanity away
from the physical-force argument. Angell
does not advocate disarmament. There is
no roya! road to peace; the shortest cut
is that which makes for greater enlighten-
cTumrTiuf the ®" e
county will come to co-operate with like-
mmbrdh People . m other countries. War
might become impossible by a plan also ad
yanced by Maurice Hewlett some“imeL c k'
t-e., an international strike of the peonle
w ° < ’ tbi " s *° aad everything to
lose by being nsed as pawns in the game of
a fat and greedy minority.
Some Minor Verse
'The Humbler Pods, a collection of ver
extracted from periodicals and obsci
books, mostly American, was published i
years ago by McClurg and Go., of Chicaj
It filled a gap, and 11 editions have be<
printed since then. The same publish?.:
now issue a continuation — The Humbl
Poets — Second Series. It is not edited
the same hand as the first; its scope is wic
and its quality more uneven. The edito
reached over to Britain and grabbed bits o:
Tennyson, Davidson, Masefield, A. E. Hoi
man, Dowson, Owen Seaman and others,
mix with specimens of humble poetry re
cued from the Milwaukee Morning Glo
and similar places. They also raked ov
Australia, and captured Lawson’s “The Sli
rails and the Spur,” G. G. MeCrae’s “Eorb
Sutherland,” Mary Colborne-Veel’s “Satu
day Night,” and verses by F. Tyrrell Gi
W. F. Chambers.... and Sarah Welch!
was an eccentric rake that caught Sara
Welch in Sladen’s garden, and* missed
many other —well, brighter blossoms. Bu
ev n if it were any honor to be represented
with Phineas K. Buggins, Tennyson anc
other humbler poets, Australia does not ge
any credit, as the editors have not given the
authors a place label. However, it is
higgledy-piggledy with some good tiling
which might otherwise have wasted all thei
sweetness on the desert air of the back num
bers.
There is a rough arrangement of the con
tents according to subject matter. Mase
field’s “Cargoes” is included in a sectio
headed “In Lighter Vein,” with many thing
worse thkn this neat epitaph by R. K. Mun
kittrick :r
Beneath this quiet, turfy
And flower-scented green
Lies Arabella Murphy ;
As usual—kerosene.
In another compartment you find this perfec
little thing by Ernest Dowson (Eng.), who
is, perhaps, not so well known as he ough
to be: —
They are not long—the weeping and the
laughter,
Love, and desire and hate:
I think they have no portion in us after
We pass the gate.
They are not long, the days of wine anc
roses:
Out of a misty dream
Our path emerges for a while, then closes
Within a dream.
Margaret Veley’s “Japanese Fan,” which
originally appeared in Cornhill Magazine
1876, is attributed to “Anon.” Saintsbury
in his History of Prosody, was enthusiastic
over this trifle, and pointed out the difference
in modulation between it and Browning’s
“Love Among the Ruins,” owing to the
shortening of the odd lines. It is worth
reprinting:—
Though to talk too much of Heaven
Is not well,
Though agreeable people never
Mention Hell;
Yet the woman who betrayed me,
Whom I kissed,
In that bygone summer, taught me
Both exist.
I was ardent, she was always
Wisely cool;
So my lady played the iraitor—
-1 the fool.
Oh ! your pardon! but remember,
If you please.
I’m translating; this is only
Japanese.

WEARYIN’ FOR YOU.
Jos' a-wearyin’ for you—
All the time a-feelin’ blue;
Wishin’ for you—wonderin’ when
You’ll be cornin’ home agen.
Restless—don’t know what to do. *
Jes’ a-wearyin’ for you!
Comes the wind, with soft caress,
Like the rustlin’ of your dress;
Blossoms failin’ to the ground
Softly, like your footsteps sound ;
violets like your eyes so blue —
Jes’ a-wearyin’ for you !
Mornin’ comes; the birds awake;
Use to sing us for your sake!
But there’s sadness in the notes
Ihat come trillin’ from their throats;
Seem to feel your absence, too—
Jes a-wearyin’ for you !
Evenin’ comes; I miss you more
When the dark glooms in the door-
Seems jes’ like you orter be
lhere to open it for me!
Latch goes tinklin’; thrills me through
Sets me wearyin’ for you ! ’
Jes’ a-wearyin’ for you—
All the time a-feelin’ blue;
r i s Jfi n ’ f°i‘ you—wonderin’ when
You 11 be comin home agen.
Restless —-don’t know what to do —
Jes a-wearyin’ for you !

THE SUM OF LIFE.
Nothing! to do but work,
Nothing to eat but food,
Nothing to wear but clothes
To keep one from going nude.
Nothing to breathe but air,
Quick as a flash ’tis gone •
Nowhere to fall but off,
Nowhere to stand but on.
Nothing to comb but hair,
Nowhere to sleep but in bed,
Nothing to weep but tears,
Nothing to bury but dead.
Nothing to sing but sougs,
Ah, well, alas! alack!
Nowhere to go but out,
Nowhere to come but back.
Nothing to see but sights,
Nothing to quench but thirst.
Nothing to have but what we’ve got •
Thus through life we are cursld. ’
Nothing to strike but a gait-
Everything moves that goes
Nothing at'all but common
Can ever withstand those wpjs.
BEN KING.
A WAR ECHO.
Wake up early, ehillun!
Day is long and bright;
Sun is workin’ overtime
To give us lots o’ light.
So’jers is a fightin’
An’ we mustn’t stop to 'play,
Ev’ry minute’s precious,
’Ca’se we got dat tax to pay.
Bees is makin’ honey,
An’ de hoss he pull de plough.
De corn’s a-raisin’ tassels
Jes’ as fast as it knows how.
De pigs is eatin’ faster,
An’ de hens is cacklin’ gay,
Ain’ no time foh loafin’, 1
’Ca’se we got dat tax to pay.
ANONYMOUS.
Shakespeare Bibliography
As a catalogue to a library so is a biblio
graphy to the literature of any large subject
And “Shakespeare” is so large a subjec
that the various editions of the Works and
the books of criticism, commentary, exposi
tion and what not, make a library in them
selves —a scattered library, and therefore one
most in need of a catalogue. This has been
supplied by William Jaggard of the Shak-
speare Press at Stratford-on-Avon, a de-
scendant of the Elizabethan author and
printer also named William Jaggard, who
was one of the printers and perhaps the edi-
tor of the First Folio Edition of Shake-
speare’s plays. ,
Jaggard’s Shakespeare Bibliography is not
the first of its kind, but it can be confidently
said that it is the most complete. It is a fat
quarto volume of 730 pages, and it cbntains
over 36,000 entries and references. It has
taken 22 years to compile, and is worth it,
for it is a complete guide to informatiori re-
lating to Shakespeare, and therefore indis-
pensable to every library and to any stu-
dent making an elaborate study of the sub-
ject.
Australia is represented in the list by the
names of Prof. MacCallum, Henry Gullett,
J. Le Gay Brereton, and P. Glynn. Josiah
Symon’s pamphlet and some others have
been missed. However careful, the biblio-
grapher is sure to miss some items, and Wm.
Jaggard proposes to issue supplementary
lists from time to time.
One does not expect to find any of the
compiler’s personality peeping out between
the dry bones of a bibliography ; but it is
evident from the notes to some entries that
Jaggard has his knife into that bumptious
writer, Sidney Lee. He refers to “the im-
pertinent patronage of Shakespeare by ‘Sid-
ney Lee,’ ” and indexes the latter’s books
under his real name—Solomon Lazarus Levi.
The chief cause of offence seems to be:
“ ‘Sidney Lee’s’ unmerited attacks upon
Wm. Jaggard, Shakespeare’s accredited pub-
lisher,” who has been dead 300 years. There
aie not many of the tribe of Barabbas who
are likely to find a remote descendant of the
same name keen and able to defend their re-
putation.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Mystery.
As far as a man might go»
When morning weaves
A spell o’er his soul, I went
Through £( green place of leaves.
The trees were as olden friends—
AS tall friends of old.
1 knew them, root, trunk and branch,
To their domes lit with gold.
The birds of that forest-place,
Uplifting loud joy,
1 knew theni, song, plume and flight,
From of old, as a boy.
Tet, deep in that p’laas Of leaves,
A Presence a'fcode,
Making strange as a world afef
The old ways that I strode.
I sought for that Presence fa!f
the wood),
And came, when the West was red,
Face to face ivith my Mood.
N.'S.'Wv
RODE RIO QUINN.
At Toil and at Rest.
tSil of the tender hand
f-I6‘w it bleSSeth a weary land!
flaw it &often§ the fevered bed,
Pouring balm ori the deaf one’s head !
Ho'w ft brighteifs the brow of pain,
Counting love as the Crown of gain !
How it lifteth the lamp of light
O’er the swell of the Seas at night I
How it blesseth the weafy land—
Beautiful toil of the tender hand ?
Beautiful toil of the work-worn ha fid,■
How it levels the rock-rough land !
How it giveth the dear ones rest,
How it maketh the sweet home-nest!
How it sendeth the streams of love
Rippling down from the peaks above!
How it lifts in a joyous flow
Waters glad from the caves below!
How it blesses the parched land—
Beautiful toil of the work-worn hand!
Beautiful play of the children’s hand,
Opening Heav’n for a sin-sad land !
How it ties with a tender twine
Heart to heart in a band divine!
How the palms as they clasp and press
Weave a joy in the wilderness!
How the lips with the lisp of love
Bear us back to the realms above,
Pouring joy on a joyless land—
Beautiful play of the children’s hand!
Beautiful rest of the folded hands,
Folded meet for the Silent Lands!
If we guessed at the things they know,
What to us were our weal and woe ?
Peace as pure as a paling star
Might wrap our souls in a radiance far;
Love as deep as an ocean dell
Lap us round with an endless swell!
Loveliest sight in the weary lands
Is the radiant rest of the folded hands !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
Telepathy.
How dark it was last night! One bar
Of light swept upward from the Head,
Hung, and wheeled down and round, and fled.
Insistent, steady, low, afar,
The full tide pleaded to the moon
To show her face. All down the long
Dark coast I heard the mournful song
The slow, upheaving breakers croon:
Like one who sots himself to sleep,
And dreams his love has come again,
And stretching out his arms in pain
Awakes—to see the grey dawn creep
With stealthy feet across the floor ;
And knows his utter wretchedness,
And can but kiss her empty dress,
The handkerchief that last she bore,
Half wondering in his misery
That death should be so long delayed. . . .
1 leant above the balustrade
Of the verandah suddenly,
And all my heart and soul and nv'nd
Went throbbing out across the dark,
Flashed like the telegraphic spark,
In aim your own heart’s heart to find.
Was it in vain? One instant’s fire
I seemed to feel your thought with mine.
Who knows? Does the veiled moon divine
The sea’s vague, infinite desire ?
N.S.W.
FRITZ S. BURNELL.
The Ferry Maiden.
We see her on the ferry-boat,
That daily brings us down
From suburbs tranquil and remote
Into the toilsome town.
Across her eyes soft shadow s float;
The laces of her gown
Slip sweetly sideways at her throat,
Sun-kissed and smooth and brown.
She dreams of duel and of dance,
Sword-flash and fan’s gay flirt;
She sees a cavalier of France
Clean through his ruffled shirt
Receive, from some fair lady’s glance,
A sweet and deadly hurt;
And in these visions of romance
The hero’s name is Bert.
We envy her the golden days
Wherein these songs are sung;
We know of tricks that Cupid plays
When dainty maids are young;
How, all across Life’s glittering ways,
His wanton net is flung,
And little birds, where girlhood strays,
Sing love on every tongue.
A youth approaches at a trot;
His face is fat and white;
His socks are plentiful and hot,
A most amazing sight.
But her young welcome falters not,
He is her own true wight;
In accents clear she hails him: “Wot
Cher goin’ t’ do t’-night?”

DEOPPING THE PILOT.
“Yes,” Mr. Wade said; “I have seen the various articles, and I think they fairly accurately represent what has been, and is, going on. But I have not
been consulted, or even approached. Naturally, as lam leader of the Opposition, one would expect that I should have heard something in an official manner

How to Address Letters.
CorrespoT.dents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JULY 6, 1911.
THE BOOM THAT IS GOING TO WASTE.
Australia is booming. Federation and a
long series of good seasons have combined to
give the lie to pessimists from Cape York to
the Leemvin, and to hopelessly discredit De-
cayed Fish purveyors from the Bight to the
Gulf of Carpentaria. The squatter, who,
eight years ago, had the gloom of the heavily-
mortgaged inscribed all over him, is to-day in
a position, if he feels like it, to spurn his
bank manager like the dirt beneath his feet.
The overdraft is a nightmare of the past.
The pastoralist’s main business worries are
connected with the problem of what to do
with his accumulations. Farmers, even
improvident and unskilful farmers, have
made their piles in every State. Their goon
fortune has reacted in the usual way on ihe
rest of the community. Country stores and
shops, pubs and stock and station agencies,
stand on velvet. The country doctor, lind-
ing that more and more people are able to
pay his fees, has moved into a larger and
more ornate dwelling; the way-back solici-
tor’s heaped-up Costs are met without a
curse: bagmen, book agents and other pests
get rid of their wares with the minimum ot
argument; writters are being driven by lack
of employment into more savory callings,
for everyone has cash, and feels sure there
is plenty more where it came from.
In the cities the prevailing affluence is
even more marked. People own thousand-
guinea motor-cars who, a few years ago,
scarcely dared to run a cab-account at a
livery stable. The hotels and restaurants
are crowded, and such patrons as refrain
from drinking the alleged champagne that is
supplied there, do so for reasons of hygiene
alone. The business done by theatrical
shows of every grade has not been surpassed
in the history of the stage. Bookmakers are
more numerous, fat, and satisfied with life
than ever they were; chorus-ladies and bar-
maids are lovelier and more bejewelled. A
land tax has lately been imposed which hits
the very rich to the tune of w T ell over a mil-
lion sterling per annum. Of course there
have been moans of protest; but the uproar
was a perfunctory, unconvincing affair at
best. The fact is, Australia’s rich men are
so very rich that the so-called confiscatory
tax of the Fisher Government means no-
thing to them. It is impossible to keep up a
convincing display of grief concerning the
loss of twopence, when one’s pockets are
bursting with ten-pound notes. A cry that
has done sterling Anti-Labor service since
the beginning of things—the crusted howl
that unless the status quo is maintained, and
progress is held up indefinitely, Capital will
be driven out of the country—has completely
lost its sting. Actually the spectacle is of-
fered of the thoughtful business-man speed-
ing the parting idol of other days with
cheers of relief. As a Sydney Share-Market
Circular put it the other week :
If it be true, as alleged, that money is being with-
drawn from Australia, it is a matter of great satis-
faction. There have been times when this country
required all the money that could be obtained from
any source whatever, but that is not the case now.
Never before was there such an abundance of capital.
But the question remains—Why should a
right-thinking sharebroker yearn for that to
befall his native land which has been held
up for years as the blackest of calamities?
* * * *
One explanation is that the present Boom
has many of the features that precede a
Burst. Land-jobbing was never more rife.
In every suburb for miles around every State
capital, large, shiny and prosperous citizens,
likewise small and frowsy ones, are engaged
“cutting up estates,” and unloading them on
the public. It is not only in the immediate
neighborhood of the cities that this sort of
thing is going on. Arid land, which is hours
by buggy, tram or rail from any G.P.0., is
selling at 10 and 15 and in some cases 30
times the figure it would have fetched in
(say) 1903. The papers print the vast, and
frequently mendacious, advertisements of
these enterprises, and keep silent as to their
commercial unsoundness. Chiefly because
there is a shortage of attractive investments
in Australia to-day, the frugal man is dis-
posed to put his savings, or at any rate some
of them, into allotments. Failing that, he
may invest in a cinematograph co., or in a
new motor-garage, or in the latest theatre for
the perpetration of melodrama, or in a sea-
side hotel or restaurant, or vaudeville con-
cern. The prime danger of all this lies in
the fact that the public is dashing into these
things on the assumption, seemingly, that
the present fat times are never going to fine
down. And yet it is as certain as death
that our present prosperity must end some
time ; and it is as likely as disease that it will
end very soon. The first direction in which
the public economises when the pinch comes
is in its amusements. People start staying
away from theatres and picture shows. They
no longer buy or hire motor-cars. They
put up at hash-houses instead of hotels, and
have high teas at home instead of cham-
pagne meals at expensive restaurants. And
this is likewise the time when the bottom
falls through the land boom; and the specu-
lator’s victim finds that his £2OO block is un-
saleable at a fiver, and the bank manager
who has allowed overdrafts on the security
of land valued at boom prices repents the
transactions with profane language. The
pastoralists who have been wont to splash
round the cities after the fashion of Corin-
thians, stow their glad clothes where the
moths may not devour them, and get back
to their holdings and the gloomy old round
of skinning starved sheep and hand-feeding
live ones. Presently they find their way to
the back-parlor where the overdraft lies in
hiding; and the sickening arrogance of the
bank manager is resumed. The jumped-up
theatrical shows begin to burst. The white
ants invade the fences that encompass the
“desirable allotments.” Traders learn that
the golden epoch has closed. The country set-
tles grimly down to a further spell in the
financial shadows.
* * * *
The economic rottenness of this business,
which has been recurring in more or less
regular cycles for as long as the oldest in-
habitant can remember, is plain. The rea-
son why the Australian has little choice be-
tween putting his savings into more or less
ephemeral concerns which do widespread
damage when they burst, and sending it out
of the country for investment abroad, may
be exnressed in a brief phrase—an ineffective
tariff. Australia has every asset requisite to
make it a great manufacturing nation. It
has declared, at election after election, for
Protection. Yet, according to Knibbs’s
Official Year Book for this year, this
country imported in 1909, from the United
States alone, £1,732,853 worth of machinery.
In the same year it imported three millions
sterling worth of apparel, and £1,196,942
worth of woollen piece goods; also £418,716
worth of tinned and preserved fish, nearly a
quarter of a million pounds’ worth of furni-
ture, and £713,390 worth of bicycles and mo-
tors. And this is a mere fraction of the
total imports. The most furious of foreign
traders will admit that it is better business
for a country to invest its money in fish
canneries and furniture manufactories than
in picture shows. Iron works and woollen
mills have a habit of remaining na-
tional assets long after seaside pubs
have lost their licenses and gay restaur-
ants have filed their schedules. It is in-
finitely more profitable for the State to
make motor-cars than to stable them. But
in a new country a condition precedent to
such things is a tariff that really protects.
That Australia’s existing tariff fails miser-
ably to do the work it was built for is as
obvious as a light on a dark night. It is
not Labor legislation which is causing capital
to be withdrawn from the Commonwealth,
any more than it is Labor legislation which
is driving capital into nationally useless con-
cerns which must wilt at the first breath of
a drought. Capital is behaving in this
fashion because it can’t help itself. Some
day an Australian Government will material-
ise which is not dominated by the importing
interests of Melbourne and Sydney, and
which will accordingly have the nerve to
dower Australia with the Protec-
tion which has made America and Germany
great. And then capital will not have to
depart when a boom arrives, and the Aus-
tralian with money to invest locally will be
offered the chance of putting it into stable
concerns like woollen mills, steel works and
ship-building yards, instead of into enter-
prises whose existence largely depends on
the state of the weather. It will be a black
hour for land-speculators and shady company
promoters, but it will be a supremely fortu-
nate day for Australia.
ONCE MORE THE “DEAD HAND.”
In giving judgment last week in the special
case of the Federated Enginedrivers and
Firemen’s Association v. the Broken Hill
Proprietary Co., Ltd., C. J. Griffith em-
phasised the point that the Act “was de-
signed, however it might be applied, to pro-
mote industrial peace.” But what actually
is the effect of the High Court’s amazing de-
cision that the Engine-drivers and Firemen’s
Association “is not an association of em-
ployees in or in connection with any industry
within the meaning of Section 55 (b) of the
Arbitration Act, and is therefore incapable
of registering under the Act”? Well, one
effect which will he regarded with dismay by
all lovers of industrial peace is that the
award which the Federated Engine-drivers
and Firemen achieved some time ago in the
Federal Arbitration Court —an award which
all fair-minded employers have now come to
recognise was a just one—is rendered so
much waste paper. And, since the Engine-
drivers and Firemen are illegally registered
under the Act, so also are the Amalgamated
Society of Engineers, the Federated Carters
and Drivers’ Association, the Furniture
Trades Society, the Bread Carters’ Union,
the Plumbers and Gasfitters’ Union, the Can-
dle, Starch, Soap and Soda Employees’Union,
the Federated Painters and the Federated
Carpenters and Ironworkers. Countless
workers who have known what it was, ow-
ing to the Federal Arbitration Court’s
award, to earn decent wages, are liable to
find themselves back on scratch again, and
at the mercy of the sweating employer. Inci-
dentally, the employer who wants to pay fair
wages will find himself at the mercy of his
trade rival who wants to pay as little as
ever he can. What with the newly-dis-
covered difficulty as to the meaning of the
words _ “calling,” “employment” and “ser-
vice” in the definition of an industry, and
what with the difficulty in regard to
creating that “dispute” which the Court
has declared to be essential before
any industrial wrong can be redressed,
and what with the fact that no hu-
man being can define (in a legal manner of
speaking) just what a “dispute” is, the Fed-
eral Arbitration Act has been reduced to im-
potence and absurdity. Riddled as it is by the
interpretations which the High Court has put
on its powers under the Constitution, the Act
is of rather less value as a peace-promoter
than Peter Bowling.
* * * *
The other day the Melbourne Age —one
of the journals which were most vehement
during the Referenda campaign in denoun-
cing the suggested Constitutional amend-
ments —printed the following :
The “dead hand” of the Constitution of the United
States has again reached out to nullify an admit-
tedly wise and beneficent law. A Workmen’s Com-
pensation Act, drafted by a Commission of experts
upon the subject, and, as finally adopted by the
Legislature of New York State, modelled upon
humane and modern lines, has been declared by the
Court of Appeals of that State to be unconstitu-
tional. The argument of the Court is peculiar. It
seems to point to the conclusion that while monarch-
ies are able to adopt such laws —as almost every
country of Europe has —“free” America cannot do
so because of its Constitution. “Can a free people
be free?” asks a New York paper in commenting
upon the decision, and the question is justified by
the language of the Judges in this case.
The tragedy in connection with which the
Age smugly sympathises with the United
States is a frequent horror in this country.
Under the regime of an ultra-Conservative
Bench, the “dead hand” of our Constitution
is repeatedly to be seen nullifying “admittedly
wise and beneficent laws modelled upon hu-
mane and modern lines.” Britain affords a
horrible example of a modern country which
has no recourse to an Arbitration Court.
The Ton-y-Pandy strike approached the di-
mensions of a civil war a few months ago;
and at the time of writing, according to
the cables, the trade of Hull is paralysed by a
vast shipping strike, the districts round about
are “suffering from a scarcity of supplies”
(i.e., are starving), 10,000 rioters are peram-
bulating the city, and the police are “charg-
ing the mob and trampling women and chil-
dren under foot.” It was with the express
object of averting this sort of calamity that
the Australian Parliament passed the Fed-
eral Arbitration Act. The measure was in-
tended to right industrial wrongs, extending
over more tfian one State, wherever it found
them ; and the more wrongs it redressed, the
better it would be, in the opinion of both
Parliament and people. For many years
judges, lawyers, and the public generally
have proceeded placidly on the assumption
that the phrase '“any association of not less
than 100 employees in any industry.. .may
be registered (under the Act) as an Associa-
tion,” meant what it appears to any intelli-
gent layman to mean. In last week’s
test case the dissenting judgment of Justice
O’Connor (who has never been accused of
ultra-Democratic leanings) well expresses
the popular view
In construing a statute it must be conceded that
prima facie full value should always be given to the
language of the Legislature, and that where it is
sought in an enabling and remedial Act, such as
that now under consideration, to give to the words
which the Legislature had used a meaning more re-
stricted than that which they ordinarily bear, and
so narrow the operation of the enactment, some good
ground must be shown for such an interpretation.
In my opinion no good ground has been put forward
which could justify the narrow interpretation of the
sections in controversy, upon which the respondents
in this case are insisting. It is impossible, in my
opinion, to hold on any ground of reason, that an
interpretation which would lead to so restricted
an operation of the Act could carry out the will of
the Legislature as it is expressed on the face of the
enactment.
* * * *
An effect of what Justice O’Connor calls
the “narrow interpretation” put on the sec-
tion in question by a majority of the High
Court Judges is that large bodies of workers
finding that they are denied the legal relief
which they thought they had a right to and
which Parliament certainly intended them
to have, are talking strike. It is possible
that commonsense and good citizenship will
prevail, that employers will not seize the
chance that has been presented to them to
cause trouble, and that the community mav
be spared the horrors that the North of Eng-
land is going through. But, if that is so it
won’t in any sense be the fault of the ma-
jority of the members of that Court which
claims to have a yearning for industrial
peace. In the Engine-drivers and Firemen’s
case, as in so many others in the same juris-
diction, the “dead hand” of the Australian
Constitution has been employed by the High
Court to “nullify an admittedly wise and
beneficent law.” There is every likelihood, so
long as the Constitution of Australia ’or
that of its High Court Bench, or both, re-
mains unaltered, that this perilous state of
things will continue. What is Australia
going to do about it?
CORONATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rain squalls swept the heaths upon the
scrubby moors between Point Nepean-road
and the sea; the lines of pines blew to-
wards the Dandenongs, for behind them was
the Antarctic depression with chopped ice
in it. Depressed fowls sought cover from
the blast; their feathers blown back in
tufts and ridges, so that they became agon-
ised gooseflesh and chilblains. Sandring-
ham hasn’t made all its streets and foot-
paths yet, and diphtheria germ and the
bacillus of typhoid shivered in their wind-
swept pools—safe there because the local
Squillses said that diphtheria must be
hushed up so that real-estate values might
not suffer. A depressed man in a vegetable
garden cut cabbages by the dozen to load his
cart for the morning’s market; his hands
were blue and his nose was blue, and he
had lost his handkerchief, and unpleasant
icicles formed in the dense thicket of his
whiskers.
Everything was depressed, from the Ant-
arctic to the cabbage. The ti-tree remem-
bered a sunny day three weeks ago ; and, as
it had seen a ray for ten minutes that morn-
ing, it had allowed itself to be swindled by
the same old confidence-trick that midwinter
always plays on vegetables and the young
man with the new light suit. The ti-tree,
like the rooster greeting candlelight with the
crowing that belongs to dawn, had put forth
its buds, only to meet the Antarctic depres-
sion, and was now very sorry tnat it had
spoken.
To the swindled ti-tree came a bright-
eye'd lady, chilblained like the fowls, but en-
thusiastic of the day; so that; let the Ant-
arctic depress himself ever so lowly, he
could never dim her pride, though he might
make her nose weep. Her kindly, foolish
face shone with “loyalty”; she walked by
the ti-tree as proudly as if she were not
merely to assist in celebrating a Coronation
by proxy many times removed, but as though
the actual ju-ju business was hers —the An-
ointing, and the Genuflections, and the Giv-
ing Out of the Bible, and the Taking of the
Bible Back Again, and the Laying-on of
Hands, and all the other ritual of savagery.
She stopped at a gap in the ti-tree and
hailed the man with the cabbage knife.
“Hey, mister, ain’t you going to it?”
The blue-nosed man turned and replied
with a question: “Goin’ to what !”
“The Coronation.”
He screamed back at her: “Not me!
Cabbitches!”
“Come to school and hear all about the
Grayshers King. Mr. Dogshall is going
to speak, an’ Mr. Puffball, too—all about
the King- and the open Bible.”
“I got me cabbitches.”
She yelled again: “Open Bible, wondrous
Bible —book divine.”
“Cabbitches.”
The gale got its second wind, and the
kindly, foolish lady gave up the contest and
breasted the rise towards the school. The
sudden down-turning from the gravel to the
peat brought her head-on to the full force of
the tempest, and made her teeth ache; but
she struggled on, warm inside with the
quenchless enthusiasm over nothing. The
fact that she arrived late at the school-
house disappointed her a little ; but Mr.
Dogshall was speaking to the assembled
children —speaking at large and intermina-
bly, cursing the wearier infants with value-
less information, and blasting their intelli-
gence with his adjurations. He told them
of anointing the King with holy oil and other
savage rites —himself a savage spreading
himself to gins and piccaninnies in a wet
camp.
“The King,” concluded Mr. Dogshall,
“then comes to his Throne, and then he—ah
—is crowned —yes, crowned. The —ah —
Archbishop of Canterbury pours the —ah —
sacred oil upon his head and gives him the
—ah—Bible —the holy Bible—and then he
—ah —gives it back again. And so he is
crowned and made King—over this glorious
Empire—on which the —ah—sun never —ah
—sets, and over the glorious British navy
—and the — ah—so on. And we must never
forget—that we belong to the —ah —glori-
ous British Empire and the glorious King,
and—so on.”
Each wondering and half-frozen child had
received its little medal—the cost being
charged to “Education” for some Chinese
reason or other —and the floor became the
property of Mr. Puffball, an unnecessary
little person obsessed by his own importance
and mad on the thrilling subject of the Walls
of Derry. Peculiarly enough, these people
believe “sectarianism” spells “loyalty” to
some nebulous thing or other; they are
proud of the doubtfully safe “possession”
of India, and ashamed of the Imperial pos-
session of Ireland —or at least of those
parts of Ireland south of Black Ulster.
Mr. Puffball told the infants things which
should be forgotten utterly or utterly abol-
ished ; of how the real function of an Eng-
lish King is to “protect” one sect against
all others; of the Almighty having taken
the Hanoverians under His special guid-
ance, the importation of Dutch William
and the Guelphs apparently being made im-
perative by the fact that the British are
such a miserably poor people that they
could not grow such an exotic thing as a
King on British soil. After that he emitted
another whoop for the'openness of the Bible
—which was mere vain-glory, seeing that in
m< si British communities it is the Book


most rarely opened except in church. Also he
told them of brotherly love —brotherly love in
a mean and snobbish little community* where
the young man from the sox department in-
terdicts grocers’ daughters at the local
dance!
And then the children went out to re-
freshments and sports of the good old
humorous British-peasant type —bobbing tor

and singing “Gor’save” to the gale; all un-
derstandable enough, for the intelligence
tuat respects kings is the intelligence that
laughs at the underclothing joke and the
mother-in-law joke, and is ready always to
grin through a horse-collar. , , ,
The small boys of the Naval Brigade had
studied and digested one of the great men s
eulogy on the British Navy, and they now ap-
proached him for a subscription to the local
detachment’s funds. The great Imperialist
looked at the growing defenders ot the Em-
pire—a look full of indulgence and benevol-
ence —and their hopes rose against the tact
of his reputation for extreme carefulness.
“I carn’t to-day, lads,” he said; “the
smallest change I got is tlirippens.”
But if the National uebt could be paid
with talk, that kind of man would work tor
three shifts, or for so long as an audience
might hold out. . . ~ ,
In the chilling dusk the kindly, foolish-
faced lady walked home past the vegetable
garden, her little soul filled with the mental
equivalent of flatulence. . , , , ..
The blue-nosed man had finished loading
his cart, and was strapping its tarpaulin
down over the freight.
“Y’ didn’t see the King crowned,’ said
the half-frozen lovalist.
“Didn’t want to see nobody drowned,
said the blue-nosed man. “Near got drowned
meself. I °ot no time for Coronations —I
got me cabbitches.”

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Toryfat Has a Bad Night.
’Since the Referenda foolishness, Toryfat
has been continuallv announcing that a re-
volt against “the domination of Labor” had
set in, and that the democratic cause was
doomed. Of course, Toryfat never calls it
•“the democratic cause” ; but that is what he
meant, and hoped. In particular, Victoria,
which did such shocking things to Tory-
fat’s representatives at the last Federal
elections, was pointed to as a prodigal
who was now tearing liot-foot aw T ay from
the Labor swine-troughs. Now, on top of
all this pleasant castle-building and day-
dreaming, has come the Bendigo East elec-
tion Bendigo East only became a State
Labor seat at the last elections, when a pop-
ular and strong man in the late Tom Glass
won it by the narrow majority of a little over
200 So the Wi mmen’s Fashionable League,
and the Illiberal League, and all the other
Tory associations went gaily into the fight,
with the halo of victory already around
their heads. They were so sure that they
very nearly ran two candidates; but at the
last minute they decided to make sure, and
coaxed one to retire. The Labor party ran
a man with the handicap of no fewer than
six previous defeats; and he was neither as
strong nor as popular as Glass. Yet he
romped in by a majority of over 500 votes.
Moreover, the election was conducted on the
old roll. The new rolls, compiled in the
same manner as the Federal rolls, contain
hundreds of new names, mostly those of
young men and women and hitherto-disfran-
chised laborers, three-fourths of whom will
certainly vote Labor. So Toryfat feels as
if an iceberg had slipped down his back, and
the defeated candidate announces that
Bendigo East under present conditions seems
to be a Labor constituency. The contest is
highly significant, in view of the general
elections that are to be held at the end of
the year. Acting-Premier Watt is straining
every nerve to strengthen the Government-
position. A big attempt is being made to
win the railway service by means of a re-
classification scheme, and money is being
ladled out to pretty well any country depu-
tation that asks for it. Nevertheless, it
seems more certain than ever that the net
result of the struggle will be the loss of
several Government seats; and Toryfat,
who was beginning to feel fairly comfort-
able, is having bad dreams, and is waking
in the night with the notion that Prender-
gast has hold of his wind-pipe. So all the
old wheezy leagues are to be oiled up and
set going again, and the prospect is not
a bit encouraging.

A Proposed Shipment of School Teachers.
The N.S.W. Education Department finds it-
self short of teachers, and Minister Beeby
says he may be obliged to import a half-
hundred or so. Before doing anything of the
kind, or while he is doing it, Mr. Beeby
might very reasonably consider whether the
shortage isn’t the best proof he could possibly
have of the failure of the present system of
recruiting home-grown teachers. Down to
about six years ago the pupil-teacher plan
was in force in N.S.W.; but it was con-
tended that the teachers produced by that
method were not sufficiently well educated,
so the trainee system was adopted instead.
The change may be well enough, provided
the trainee gets a sufficient amount of prac-
tical experience in teaching; and he and she
certainly are now getting more of it than
they did at the beginning. The trouble now
is that they don’t get enough pay. In a
broad and general way professions and call-
ings may be divided into two classes. In
class 1 the person starts off with moderate
pay, and never gets much more all his or
her life. The domestic servant of 18 can
thus command almost if not quite as much
as the Mary of 48. In class 2 there is
either no pay at all for some years, or very
little; and doctors, for instance, base on
that fact. the high charges they make when
they get into full bearing, so to speak. Un-
der the pupil-teacher system the pay was
pretty good right from the start. A lad of
18 might get £6O a year. That being so,
there was some excuse for paying him a com-
paratively poor salary in after years. In
plain words, teaching was put on the basis
of an unskilled trade. The new system
sought ostensibly to remove that blot. It
therefore provided for a period of training,
running to four years, and in the fourth
year all that the trainee gets is £25 a year.
To that extent the business was put more on
all fours with other learned professions. But
when the Department cut down the pay at-
taching to the early years of service, it for-
got to tack it on to the pay attaching to the
late years; so that, except as to a half-dozen
or so special schools, the maximum salary
for a teacher in N. S. Wales is £420 a year;
and very frequently the teacher doesn’t draw
that much, £75 of it being deducted for the
rent of a school-house, which may be, and
usually is, worth very much less than that.
At (say) 32 a teacher ought to be very
nearly as good as ever he will be; yet the
average pay of a male teacher of that age
would probably be found to be not more than
£l5O a year—-less pay, for the most import-
ant work in the community, than the indiffer-
ently-skilled tradesman can command. The
great majority of teachers never get beyond
£2oo a year; the few who climb up to a
first-class school have to go on submitting
themselves to graded examinations for (say)
20 years; and the man who has the pertin-
acity to stick to the examinations, and the
ability to pass them, coupled with the special
qualifications required of a successful teacher,
cannot reasonably expect to reach the dizzy
maximum height of £350 a year, plus house
accommodation, in less than 25 years. In
most cases it is considerably more than that.
After reaching the supreme height of a first-
class school, he may get one of half a dozen
or so special appointments; if not, there is
nothing in the way of promotion but an
inspectorship, involving travel and very fre-
quent absence from home —conditions which
appeal to very few men well up in years.
The trainees may be able to climb faster;
but they cannot be at all sure of it. And,
anyway, they cannot climb higher; for the
Department offers no higher things to climb
to. Naturally, in these circumstances, the
profession is not rushed. The Department
is, in brief, trying to make it a learned pro-
fession. involving the self-denial usual in
learned professions during the years of train-
ing and probation, without offering the
prizes which the other learned professions
give in after years to their successful fol-
lowers. And, as the proof of the pudding is
in the eating, so the proof of the failure of
the system lies in the fact that there is both
a present and a prospective shortage of men
and women to man (and woman) the teach-
ing staffs.

A Wise Proposal and Some Factors That
May Wreck It.
Over a year ago the Victorian Health De-
partment instituted an investigation into the
prevalence of a certain disease in that State.
The idea was to discover from town and
country doctors the extent to which the com-
munity was ravaged by it, and then “to
cause legislative measures to be taken to ef-
fect proper control of it.” The inquiry
closed on May 31, and the Government has
by this time in its possession a record of plain
facts calculated to make its spine congeal
with horror. Unfortunately, if past form
is any indication, this is about the only effect
the movement is likely to have. Victoria
already has an Act, passed as long ago as
1890, which is capable, if firmly adminis-
tered, of doing Considerable good. It con-
tains elaborate machinery for the compul-
sory docking and overhauling of the coffin-
ship of the streets. The calamitous vessel
may—on the sworn information of any
police-sergeant, or higher officer, that she
(the vessel aforesaid) follows the oldest
trade, and needs attention —be haled before
a P.M., and committed to hospital for any
period up to three months. If Salvarsan is
half as efficacious as the medical profession
claims, a course of three months’ treatment
with it should, in a large percentage of cases,
remove the patient’s capacity to act as a
distributing-centre for disease, even if it fails
to cilte her personally. And, in many in-
stances, it is likely that she will be cured
outright. In the past the Victorian Health
Act was not given a chance to justify itself.
When the late Dr. Ckesswell was Chair-
man of the Board of Health, he tried to give
effect to its provisions; but Wowserdom
arose as one Stiggins, and snuffled him
down. Ckesswell was assured that the
time was not ripe, that all right thinkers
were revolted at his conduct, and that Public
Opinion was looking for him with an axe.
After a • while he was persuaded that the
State resented his effort to rob it of its syphi-
lis, and retired from the fray with something
of the aspect of a detected burglar. As a
matter of fact, the general body of the pub-
lic was then, as it is now, perfectly willing
to have venereal disease dealt with. The
people are, after all, mostly sane, and sane
folks are no more attached to this scourge
than they are to diphtheria or plague. But,
in the first place, there is the Wowser. It
is his hideous theory that sinners should ex-
piate their offences, not only in the next
world, but in this. That in doing so they
may ruin the lives of innumerable innocents,
and pollute the very life-blood of the nation,
concerns the militant pietist not at all. The
other foe to the health-reformer is that false
shame which is part of our British heritage.
The papers scarcely dare print a word which,
in the interests of the public, should be writ-
ten and spoken as freely as words like “con-
sumption” and “fever.” In social life a pall
of mystery is deliberately cast over a ques-
tion which should be exposed regularly to the
daylight of frank discussion. If Victoria or
any other State means to get a strangle-hold
on its syphilis, it will first need to treat the
objecting citizen exactly as it would treat one
who inveighed against plague-protection mea-
sures—-namely, as a person of unsound mind
and a public menace. And it must educate
the community into the view that this is not
a subject that needs to be muttered about in
cellars, and behind furtive doors. Failing
successful campaigns in those two directions,
fresh legislation is of about as much practi-
cal value as boots in Paradise.
The Unions and the Doctors.
“Os Jxjnominatum” puts the case for the
British Medical Association in the matter
of the old row between that body and the
N.S.W. friendly societies: —
A deputation, including medical men who don’t
belong to the 8.M.A., waited on Acting-Chief Sec.
Flowers the other day, and made statements that
were untrue and misleading. First, the Minister was
told that there were 1500 doctors practising in
N.S.W. A pure fabrication. There are about 1000.
Then the number of B.M.A. men was put down at
700, whereas it is in the region of 800. It will thus
be seen how mendacious was the impudent assertion
that the Aus. Medical Association represented 800
practitioners in N.S.W. In point of there can’t
be more than 20 of them all fd'h ... against 800 in
the reputable association, i.t, P...±.A. The latter
has its own block of building., where it meets for
the discussion of medical, 'scientific, social and
ethical matters. It conducts its own medical jour-
nal, and gives its members the latest ideas on medi-
cal subjects. Its aim is to promote fair and honor-
able practice amongst its members. Now, if a medi-
cal man is being underpaid by friendly societies, how
can he give his hordes of patients proper attention
and carry on an honorable practice? It means that
he must slur over his work, make a spot diagnosis
(as often as not wrong), and frequently give inade-
quate treatment. It is the same old curse of cheap-
ness over again, only in this case the sweating em-
ployers belong mainly to the industrial classes.
The greatest curse of all is the Institute or Dis-
pensary system, and against this the B.M.A. has
been compelled to sternly set its face. And small
wonder. Several lodges in a district combine and
agree to pay (say) two medical men a fixed sum of
(say) £3OO a year each to attend all their members.
If such an institute starts with 750 members, the
remuneration would work out at about 16s. per
member per annum. But the trouble is that there is
no provision for increase of salary with increase of
numbers—no sliding scale. Hence the numbers go
up, often as high as 1500, with no corresponding
payment, or else there may be a paltry £25 a year
increase. Thus the doctors have twice the work to
do, their expenses are increased, and they have prac-
tically no time for a private practice or self-
improvement. Then another evil crops up. Two men
are paid equal salaries, but you can never equally
divide the work. The man who is more thorough
and painstaking and the greater favorite, gets the
greater share of the work; in other words, the man
who neglects his patients and makes himself objec-
tionable to them, or who may not be relied upon to
be sober, has the better time. Surely even a pack
of lunatics would abandon such a system! And yet
the champions of this system, both the masters and
the medical serfs (who are chafing now they find
themselves tight in the shackles they put on), have
the effrontery to ask for a Commission.
What is the situation, then? A handful of non-
unionists, who have taken up appointments from
which the union men resigned, at the call of their
union, found themselves in much opprobrium, and
thought they would try to cover it by giving them-
selves a name. They did so, and the A.M.A. was
formed. But no true unionist could ever regard
them as anything but a bogus union. They are pre-
pared to accept any terms from their “masters,” the
sweating branches of the friendly societies, and to
put up with any indignity, even to the extent of
going on a deputation with their “bosses” to the
Minister. If Mr. Flowers grants a Commission, it
would be equivalent to granting one to a combined
deputation of squatters and Machine Shearer union-
ists (if that defunct union, so rightly regarded as
“bogus” by every A.W.U. man, were resurrected),
to inquire into the ordinary unionist procedure of
the A.W.U., such as refusing to work alongside a
man who had blacklegged, etc. Every union is entitled
to this refusal; then why not the B.M.A. ? Surely
Donald Macdoneli/s locum will tread a very thorny
path if he grants the request of a bogus union for a
Commission to inquire into the ordinary unionist
procedure adopted by the genuine union representing
90 per cent, of the profession.
Very good ; but, if the B.M.A. has as strong
a case as it alleges, what has it to fear from
a Commission? In fact, why doesn’t it ask
for one itself? Obviously, though it insists
that its position is the true-unionist position,
it hasn’t had the good fortune to carry con-
viction to the minds of thousands who be-
lieve they are true unionists, and who
wouldn’t hire blackleg doctors if they were
convinced that they were blacklegs. _ If the
B.M.A. men can convince the unionists who
dominate the friendly societies that the op-
position union is bogus, their troubles will
be practically over. Hence why not rush
every opportunity to do the convincing—
even to the extent of saying “Hear, hear”
to a request made by the allegedly bogus
crowd, so long as the request is only for
more light?
Hope on a Rock.
Dk. Erasmus Wilson, the grandfather of
the great biologist who gave us the theory
oJt evolution, addressed to Josiaii Wedcj-
wood some prophetic lines on ‘‘The Visit of
Hope to Sydney Cove.” The apostrophe to
Wedgwood was evoked by the modelling ot
a medallion from clay brought by Sir Jos-
eph Banks from Botany Bay, and col-
lected by the famous botanist during the
Endeavor’s stay at Ivurnell in April-May,
1770. On June 5 last the Australian official
Coronators were entertained at the potter-
ies of Stoke-on-Trent, in Staffordshire —the
county in winch old Joseph . yVedgwood,
the potter, achieved both artistic fame and
a substantial fortune. They were treated
to a luncheon at the Town Hall. The Mayor,
who presided, was another Wedgwood, a
descendant of the glorious old potter who
founded Etruria. But the gentleman seems
to be somewhat hazy concerning his family
history. When he proposed the health of
the guests, he stated that his great ancestor,
in 1789, had received from Sir Joseph
Banks some clay which Captain Cook had
obtained in Sydney Cove, and with it had
made a number of medallions, in which Hope
was represented standing on a rock. As a
matter of fact, neither Cook nor Banks ob-
tained any clay in Sydney Cove, simply be-
cause they were never there, and knew
nothing of its existence. Sydney Cove
was so named in 1788 (18 years
after Cook landed in Botany Bay)
by Governor Arthur Phillip, after
Thomas Townsiiend, Viscount Sydney,
Secretary for Home Affairs in the Pitt
Ministry; for in those days “the colonies”
were administered by the Home Depart-
ment, from a branch called the “Office for
Plantations,” which also dealt with the
affairs of Ireland. Sydney Cove is an inlet
about seven miles from the Heads; and it is
well known that Cook never even entered
the Heads, satisfying himself by naming the
harbor, as he sailed past it, in honor of Sir
George Jackson, one of the Secretaries of
the Admiralty. Mr. Mayor Wedgwood ap-
pears to know nothing, either, of the adap-
tation of the design on the medallion to the
reverse of the first Great Seal of N. S.
Wales. That design was something more,
too, than “a figure of Hope standing on a
rock.” There were the convicts landing at
Botany Bay (which, by the way, they never
did), received by Industry—surrounded by
such of her attributes as a bale of merchand-
ise, a pickaxe, and a shovel —who releases
them from their fetters and points to
ploughing oxen, and a rising town on a
neighboring hill, with a fort for its protec-
tion, while the masts of a ship are seen in the
bay. Heraldically, this is a finer coat-of-
arms for the wearing of Josiaii Wedg-
wood’s descendants than any the College
of Heralds can grant, though the good Mayor
of Stoke-on-Trent seems to know little about
it.

A gasp of pretended amazement goes up
from the Victorian Tory press, caused, by
the *following figures relating to the distribu-
tion of the Commonwealth land-tax pay-
ments :—
N. S. Wales £570,408
Victoria .. .. -. - • 518,161
S. Australia .. .. •• •• 116,833
Queensland .. .. .. . • 71,417
Westralia .. .. .. • • 30,689
Tasmania .. .. .. • • 29,372
Total .. .. •• £1,337,180
On these figures, the cry is raided in the
Argus and elsewhere that the tax is Avick-
edly disproportionate to population, and so
on. This alleged argument really amounts
to a good joke. The tax Avas designed, not
to touch up the populated lands, but. to reach
the regions where land monopoly is at its
worst. It was therefore intended that Vic-
torian squatterdom should pay heavily. But
if the squatters, wffiose friends now weep be-
cause the figures suggest that the tax prom-
ises to be partially successful, will cut up
their estates, they will speedily find their pay-
ments reduced. If they will partition them
up into small Dlocks of less than £5000 un-
imnroved value, with a cultivator’s family on
each block instead of a mere brood of sheep,
then, instead of paying £518,461, Victoria
will not nay one penny. Not one single
brown.
The British population has increased by
nearly 5,000,000 in 10 years; but before the
drum’s are banged in rejoicing, the 5.000,000
ought to be analysed The vaunted policy
of an open door —an open door for rags,
steel, third-class men, and anything else cal-
culated to shorten the rations of the de-
scendants of Vikings—is getting in more
deadly work tnan its strongest opponents
thought likely. Through that entrance pours
a stream of all the cheap and inferior human
material of Europe, with odd scraps of Asia
and Africa. When crews of Chinamen were
wanted to assist in breaking the maritime
strike, there was no need to rake Hong-
kong or the Straits Settlements for them.
They signed on at Cardiff. If that door is
allowed to remain open, the population of
England in 25 years Avill be as mixed as
that of Tnursday Island. And yet there
are people Avho would seriously try to per-
suade us that there is no need to go in for
Australian defence. With the Australian
door closed, and the English door open, the
aims and objects of the people on opposite
sides of the world must become more and
more divergent; and Sydney may still be
speaking the tongue of William Shak-
speare when St. Paul’s is a Buddhist tem-
ple, and The Times is printed in a modified
Hindustani which has learned to use the
Russian characters for the convenience of
Lithuanian readers.

A Boom in “Peace.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Japan, England and America are busy falling on
each other’s necks in the transports of a new arbi-
tration-and-general-'Tll-be-good” corroboree.
Have the nations drawn closer together, with
tears in their penitent eyes;
Are they tying themselves in a_ tether ot
peaceful complexion and size?
If they are, then prepare for the shindy,
keep your eyes most remarkably clear;
For when statesmen grow fulsome aud windy,
then war and disturbance are near!
Yea, they talk with one hand pawing
Heaven, and the other concealing an axe r
When they prate of all things being forgiven,
pray examine the hands at their backs.
Though the Jap and the Yank weep together,
though they shed sudden rivers of grease,
Keep your eyes firmly fixed on the weather,
and beware of the booming of “Peace ;
For we’ve witnessed these fervid proceedings
on sundry occasions before ; '
And when .Peace forms the substance ot
pleadings, it is mostly a prelude of war:
“By your leave,” says the Jap to the Rus-
sian, “we’ll agree to forget Liao-yang :
Says the Frenchman likewise to the Prus-
sian, “We’ll let Alsace go cheerfully
hang!”
And the Yank sort of smiles rather frantic.
and observes to his relative Bull :
“Let us shake o’er the Gard-dam Atlantic,
for my hands have grown suddenly full.
But the Jap and the Russ and the Prussian
from their launching of ships never
cease —
When the Yank and the Jap get to gushin’,
then beware of the fragments of
“Peace”!
Keep an eye, if you like, on the cables, and
expand, if you please, with delight;
But as cats lift their yowls on the gables, so
the nations are spoiling for fight.
And the Yank and the Jap and the Prussian,
being old at the game, sort of smile ;
And the Frenchman and Bull and the Rus-
sian keep their guns loaded up all the
while! , . „ ,
They are friends, if vou please, whilst you
listen, unto Gkey and our friend Vvilly
Taft; ..
But their eyeballs at times seem to glisten,
not with joy, but with cunning and
craft!
And as cats swiftly leap into battle, so the
nations their war-dogs release —
Yea, the shells and the shrapnel’s loud rattle
often spoil these orations of “Peace .
So, my friends of this small, sucking Nation,
get a large, solid bolt for your door;
Also hustle with White Immigration, and pre-
pare for red ruin and war!
For the land that is fooled into dreaming,
whilst the statesmen perfervidly swell,
Often wakes with a start to the screaming
and the swift ricochet of the shell!
When the nations draw closely together,
there are saurian tears in their eyes;
They are sparring for breath, and a tether
is tied with their mutual lies.
“We’ll be good,” chant the nations in chorus,
“let’s grow friendly and calm and
obese”*
But — the guns with their anthem, sonorous
play a dirge o'er the carcase of “Peace” !

Twaddell, the brave man who made the
furious attack on Justice Higgins from his
coward’s castle in the N.S.W. Assembly last

MR. R. P. CROUDACE (of Oroudace Bros.)
writes :—“The 25ft. x 6ft. 6in. Launch you
built, and installed a 5-h.p. Engine in, has
to date run 14,400 miles in 2160 hours, at a cost of
1056 shillings for oils, and £1 17s. 6d. for repairs.
During the two years I have never had a stop;
always working smoothly and very easy to manage.
I had an expert to look over her last week, and he
reported that she was in as good a condition to-day
as the day she was installed. I think the above
figures are recommendation enough for anyone, so
no need for me to say any more about her.”

urged repeatedly to put through amending
Acts that will protect the public; but no-
thing has been done. Probably the Labor
Government will see that it is not much use
struggling to win for the workers an extra
few shillings a day of the wealth they pro-
duce if those extra few shillings are prompt-
ly stolen by dishonest traders.
Mills, of Milwaukee, who is touring
Maoriland under engagement to the M.L.
Trades Council Federation and Labor party,
has been dropping a sizable piece of ice down
the backs of his red-rag comrades in the
Isles of Borrow. The red-rag comrades have
worked themselves up to explosion point on
the subject of military training, and are in
the habit of frantically howling: “Down
with conscription! We stand for Universal
Brotherhood, and the yellow brother will
not hurt us!” Hearing that Mills had
been American delegate to the Peace Confer-
ence in London, one brother at Masterton
jumped up and asked Mills what his opinion
of compulsory military training was. By
the time the opinion had been given, the
brother was feebly wondering what had hit
him. “My opinion,” drawled Mills, “is
that, while militarism lasts —and it will last
for some time yet—you had better arm every
citizen, establish a gun and ammunition fac-
tory, _ and let every man keep his gun and
cartridges, at home. The voluntary system
is a farce; only men who are drunk or can-
not battle for a job enlist, and they are the
most dangerous class to trust in time of in-
dustrial trouble. If every man is armed and
trained, and has a gun, there will be no use
for troops during a strike—and if you take
my advice, you’ll arbitrate and not strike —-
and then also, if (as is possible) interna-
tional complications arise, Maorilanders will
be prepared.” Years ago the M.L. Trades
Councils decided for compulsory as against
volunteer service ; and though the local Labor
party has international arbitration as a
plank, it isn’t fool enough to want to sit
unarmed and address words of welcome and
comradeship to the armed invader, who pro-
bably wouldn’t understand “colonial Eng-
lish,” anyway.

The Parliament of N. S. Wales is to tackle
a problem of the ages in an effort to regu-
late and improve the condition of genius
and its little brother, literary and artistic
talent. A denutation of the representa-
tives of the craft to which King David,
Phil May, Sophocles, Martin Tupper,
Horace, and W. Shakspeare belonged—
not to mention Alfred Alstin, “Belle-
rive,” and Rudyard Kipling in our own
time —called upon Mr. Beeby last week to
ask that they might be definitely included
among the “industrial workers” for whose
benefit Wages Boards and Arbitration
Courts are designed. The Minister re-
ceived tljiem like long-lost brothers; and, in-
terrupting the first speaker, folded them to
his sympathetic heart. He declared that he
had heard their wailing among the seats of
the mighty, and would give them an oppor-
tunity to wail before a just tribunal set up
to examine their claims. The Writers and
Artists’ Union will thus be placed in the
position of being able to try to do things.
How long these things are or how numerous
remains to be seen. Until occult science or
some _ kindly branch of learning evolves a
machine by which to measure with exacti-
tude the merit of a poem, drawing or storjq
it would seem that that old machine —the
editor —must still fill a large place in the
literary or artistic scheme of things. The
Writers and Artists’ Union recognises that;
yet it sees no reason why it shouldn’t get
whatever lift the new machine can give,
even though the lift may not carry it very
far. Besides, with even a little lift, the
craft may have more strength to battle on
its own account for the other things that
matter.

“O.K” on the abolition of class distinc-
tions :
For Some weeks I have been chewing the political
doctrine of Josiah Thomas, of the Federal Labor
Ministry, as expressed in his lecture on “The Fall of
Empires,” and I can’t swallow it. When Thomas
states that the gospel of Christ means the abolition
of class distinctions, I admit the fact, but I am re-
minded that Christ, anyhow, stood in a class by
Himself. He had followers who grovelled to Him all
the time, and when He chose 12 disciples He created
an aristocracy of holiness right away. No; cant as
we may, we won’t dodge class-distinctions in this
world. We start with them at school, where the boy
and girl masses are divided into classes, and the
teachers are lords or ladies over all. Later on we
find that Josiah Thomas, P.M.G., is not in the same
.class in the Government service as the letter-sorter.
In fact, Josiah is a Government boss and not a ser-
vant at all, and his salary will always keep him off
the level occupied by the struggling toilers who try
to take him seriously. When he states that people
■can't have “Christian brotherhood” whilst they
■“stand to each other in the relation of employer and
•employed,” I agree with him again, and I want to
know how he would run the Post Office in a Christian
manner? If the discharging of an employee is the
sinful exercise of “class,” what would Josiah do
with a grasping Judas letter-carrier who habitually
robbed the mailbag? Thus far I have agreed with
Josiah’s assertions, but I differ from him when he
says that the possibility of a beautiful brotherhood
of man is proved by “the fact that so many men
and women are ready to renounce the joys of family
life in order that they may more efficiently min-
ister to the wants of others.” As a matter of fact,
these professing pietists constitute themselves into a
class of the “cloth,” which demands reverence from
non-wearers, and has been ranged on the side of the
high authorities ever since class-distinctions were
established.

“The Gay Grisette,” the new musical piece
at the Criterion, is a winner in one respect:
nothing so frankly imbecile has been pre-
sented to Sydney leg-show patrons for years.
It is of the old order of musical extrava-
ganza, and the trail of the early ’9o’s is all
over it. As a fair average type of
song-and-dance farce of French origin
or inspiration (it is a blood-relation
of such concoctions as “Morocco Bound,”
“The Lady Slavey,” “The Shop Girl,”
etc.), “The Gay Grisette” contrasts in-
terestingly with the Austrian-built article
which has such a vogue nowadays. It im-
presses on one that the old-time adaptation
from, or imitation of, the Palais Royal farce
was much more boisterous and much less
sentimental than the modern product of
Vienna. Also it was less suggestive, and,
withal, more spicy. As a mild smoking-
room yarn is to “Three Weeks,” so “The
Gay Grisette” is to “The Merry Widow.”
The present-day show, as witness “The Waltz
Dream” and “The Balkan Princess,” makes
a definite effort to tell some sort of story; a
characteristic of its forerunners was to let
coherence slide, and encourage the performers
to get out of the picture as often and as
completely as they wished. Taking one
thing with another, the summing-up of the
Court is strongly in favor ®f the newer
fashion in musical-comedy form.
* * * *
As regards characterisation, “The Gay
Grisette” contains all the old identities that
we knew in earliest youth. Captain Jack
Canary is a sprightly, raffish hero, after the
manner of Seymour Plicks. He pervades
the European Continent with a weirdly im-
probable man-servant, and a quartette of
show-girls who are seeking legal or other re-
dress from a military person who promised
to marry and then deserted them. An Afri-
can princess—a cannibal, and a flamingly
over-dressed one at that —arrives, hot on the
trail of a one-time victim to her charms.
There is a Sultan, anxious to make his score
of 99 wives the round century; and an Irish
solicitor hurries feverishly round beneath a
gleaming tall hat, in quest of the heir to
an earldom and £lO,OOO per annum. They
foregather in the grounds of a French girls’
school. Eroticism is the dominant note of
this establishment. The elderly principal
worships the male sex, and so do all her
charges. When the maidens desire a visit
from the neighboring garrison they hoist the
Union Jack —and it may be added that the
grand old flag is in pretty constant requisi-

Comedy Lion and Comedy Mouse. The crusty
colonel (Percy Clifton) and the mild curate
