tion. Particularly enthusiastic where the
men are concerned is the flapper-heroine.
Babette. As her young friends put it in a
stirring chorus, “The Gay Grisette is a
Great Coquette”—which, if anything, under-
states the case. A parson, in appearance
and habits the immemorial Wowser, is one
of the few characters who chases not, but,
instead, is chased. He and the Hibernian
attorney aforesaid are about the only folks
present who appear in any way fitted to lead
the celibate life. After all hands have collec-
tively and individually comported themselves
like highly-trained lunatics for a period of 2J
hours or thereabouts, the solicitor suddenly
saddles Canary with the earldom, whereupon
the latter marries Babette and the uproar
terminates.
* * * . *
Miss Florence Imeson isn’t a grisette, and
she doesn’t resemble a schoolgirl, but in every
other respect her impersonation of Babette
is unimpeachable. Her plaintive refrain, “I
Wonder if You Miss Me Sometimes” (echoed
by the military without, in a thunderous
howl, suggestive of an army chanting a
battle hymn), is the last note in allurement:
and her dancing is excellent. Frank Lin-
coln, as Canary, does all the things that are
expected of that type of character, and does
them well. Tom Graves’s cleric is alone
worth the entrance-money. The humor of
Percy Clifton (Colonel Pom Pom) is over-
done in spots; but he has one good song and
brings down the house with it. The minor
rQles are as well handled as they deserve, and
the scenery is attractive.

“Hamlet” has been playing to great houses
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) for the last 10
nights. Some meritorious features that were
crowded out of this paper’s notice last week
are the Ophelia of Miss Dorothea Baird (an
impersonation full of grace and pathos), the
King of Frank Tyars, and the excellent
comedy of Tom Reynolds as the First Grave-
digger. On Friday week “Hamlet” retires,
giving place to “The Lyons Mail.”
** * *
There are some newcomers in the cast of
“The Balkan Princess” at Sydney Theatre
Royal. Miss Dorothy Brunton (a daughter
of scenic-artist Brunton, deceased) now plays
the part of henchwoman or female A.D.C. to
the Princess with infinite grace and charm.
Also, the ranks of the show-ladies have re-
cently been augmented by two sisters who
don’t need to utter a word to simply pulverise
the arguments of those who contend that
Australia doesn’t produce beautiful girls.
** * *
Sydney Adelphi is still “Driving a Girl
to Destruction” amidst loud applause. It
is estimated that the second villain has
already thrown the cripple boy’s loaf of
bread down 871 miles of alley, and that the
cripple boy himself has climbed 40,000 ft.
through fanlights, windows, etc. Some re-
cords in these athletic byways will be broken
before George Marlow produces from his
sleeve the next theatrical trump with the
combined drawing-power of an ocean tug
and a blazing warehouse.
** * *
F. S. Kelly, the Australian pianist who
scored a sensational success at Sydney Town
Hall some weeks ago, was in the act of giv-
ing his first recital at St. James’s Hall when
this paper hurried to press. He will give
another at the same place next Saturday
night.
* * * *■
Spissell Brothers and Mack, who fall
about humorously for a living, and Alf.
Holt, who travels in mimicry and whistling,
are the most recent guests to hang up their
advertising banners on the turrets of Sydney
Tivoli, where Baron Rickards receives tri-
bute from his serfs and vassals, and nightly
causes to be performed the ancient rite
known as “the first part.” Both new turns
help to put a head on a bill which was not
by any means flat before they arrived. The
other headliners are Francis and Alfred
(comedy jugglers), comedian Ted Kalman,
Arthur Croxon, the pair of dancing Lillies,
Iveldine and Kliino, and Fanny Powers,
whose study of Julian Rose is an admirable
addition to her “imitations of artists I have
seen and heard.”
** * *
Miss Toby Claude arrived at the National
Amphitheatre on Saturday. She is as cute
a circumstance in her line as any local
music-hall has starred for a long time back.
She has some diverting songs, and one of
them enables a considerable expanse or tract
of back—that back which has won eulogy in
three continents —-to be exhibited to dazzled
audiences. Other noteworthy lures at this
show are Anita Diaz and her troupe of
monkeys, and the four Musical Gardiners.
** * *
Sousa and his band will be back in Syd-
ney next Monday to inquire about a popular
party named Kelly, who appears to be still
in hiding. These positively-last inquiries
will extend over a week.
** * *
Spencer’s picture show at Sydney Ly-
ceum, after doing excellent business with
“The Lyons Mail” and the English Derby of
1911—the former ranking among the best
of the drama pictures yet shown—has got
into a new goldfield that offers wonderfully
fine opportunities. “The Crusaders” is
the name of the new film; and the title alone
suggests the possibilities of the new and
hitherto strangely-neglected patch. The
series will begin to unwind its 4000 ft. next
Monday night.
** * *
Gregan McMahon’s team of repertory
players gave their first exhibition in improv-
ing drama at the Melbourne Turn Verein
Hall early last week. The entertainment,
which included a bright performance of the
second act of “The Critic,” was mostly de-
voted to the production of “The Two Mr.
Wetherbys,” Hankin’s amiable essay in do-
mestic comedy. In this play Hankin in-
dulges in mild and persuasive iconoclasms.
He batters down a few conventions, and
leaves James Wetherby, a household “idol,”
stripped of his fictitious reputation as a pat-
tern saint. Gregan McMahon, in the part
of Richard Wetherby, strongly assisted in
the process of reducing James in his moral
status; while G. H. Crosby and the Misses
Smith and Hungerford employed their obvi-
ous abilities in portraying other characters
in the cast. On Thursday night another
party of McMahon’s amateurs made a much
more ambitious appearance in “John Gabriel
Borkman,” a study of a poet-financier. Crav-
ing for authority and boundless power, he
sacrifices love and the homelier virtues to
his enormous and admittedly noble ambi-
tions. Soon he involves himself in hopeless
ruin, and at the beginning of the play is
found in the gallery of his house mainly in
the company of idealistic dreams. The spec-
tacle of this broken mystic thinking the
world well lost for his faded illusions, is
more moving than the tragical motifs in the
drama. Borkman is poignantly interest-
ing, and F. Norris played the character with
strength and force. Ella Rentheim received
sympathetic treatment from Miss Maud Jol-
liffe, and Miss Rene Tanswell gave good ex-
pression to the cold egomania of Mrs. Bork-
man. J. Fowler was oddly attractive as
Vilhelm Foldal ; and, concerning Miss Moore
as Mrs. Wilton, it is a sort of compliment
to say that the difference between her read-
ing of the part and the part itself was pos-
sibly all the difference between a lady and a
female.
** * *
“A Fool There Was” might have done
much better with more time; but it hap-
pened in a brief interval between the de-
parture of a success and the arrival of a suc-
cessor. Porter Brown wrote this melodrama
with the deliberate intention of giving the
public what it likes; but, like managers who
start out with the same laudable intention,
he bases his high resolve on a limited defini-
tion. He fixes in his own mind on a certain
level of intelligence, a certain degree of
taste, and thinks he has a compendium of
popular fancy; but you can’t make certain
of getting the public that way, any
more than you can rely upon drawing ail
sorts and conditions of people to a drama
composed of “Hamlet,” “The Doll’s House,”
“The Silver King,” and “The Face at the
Window.” However, Brown, in the course
of his experiment, appears to have struck a
definite strata of public partiality, and his
worst woman is a good thing financially.
On Saturday night next Brown is to be dis-
placed by Mason, whose “The Witness for
the Defence” introduces Miss Ethel Irving,
of London, Eng., a lady who lately was only
second in importance to the Coronation,
judging by Melbourne newspapers, and now
beats that exploded function hollow.
** * *
“Our Miss Gibbs” is happening at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) every evening at 7.50 with
the regularity of an astronomical movement,
and would appear to have become a. habit
with a large number of middle-aged citizens,
who give the stalls a certain appearance of
bald monotony to those moving in higher
circles. “The Dance Du Vaurien” is as
familiar as the boarding-house procession
to breakfast, and that other foreign item,
“Yip-I-addy-I-ay,” is scarcely more exciting
than grace before meat by reason of its un-
reasonable frequence, the epidemic having
spread to the suburbs, and broken out in
10,000 upright pianos. Still, profitable audi-
ences are always in attendance. Melbourne’s
floating population drifts into Exhibition-
street in response to a sort of natural law
that has nothing to do with gravity.
** * *

sin that lay dormant for 20 years, and then
exploded with a loud report, and blew two
families into a state of domestic chaos, does
not retire in favor of Clyde Fitch’s rural
entertainment, “Lovers’ Lane,” till after
Friday night’s performance. On Saturday,

** * *
“The Favorite,” at Melbourne King’s, is
now introducing Miss E. Duggan in the role
of a negligent owner of horses. The lady
pursues her hobby without forgetting that
the gown of a duchess is the most appropri-
ate costume for the most menial occupation.
She wears several bright frocks, which
serve to remove one’s attention from the fact
that she is repeating her set of six postures
and three kinds of smile. The next charac-
ter in point of importance is the villain,
Rutland Beckett, who tries “to nobble the
favorite” and murder Miss Duggan on the
top of a house. Knowing that to be intelli-
gent is to gain a reputation for eccentricity,
Beckett follows the safe plan of committing
silly crimes for much more silly reasons.
Two grotesques assist him in his amazing
deeds, and these persons perform their duties
with an excessive display of the comic
spirit. The parts are represented by Bailey
and Harrison. Roy Redgrave pictures the
character of the Hon. Harry Stirling with
pleasant sincerity; while Ned Duggan’s
hearty portrayal of Joseph Silver is a fine
demonstration of eyebrows and well-booted
feet.
** * *
Of the present Opera House co., “Mar-
guerite Ismay and Girls” call for the earli-
est consideration. The young ladies indulge
in a form of wooden-mat dancing, and ex-
pend a heap of energy without any remark-
able artistic result. Among other perform-
ers, Miss Charmeroy is fingering her piano,
and Lamberti continues his imitations. Also
there are Fred Bluett, Reichen’s good dogs,
the two Pi’ices, and the generally effusive
Cadwall and Verne.
** * *
The Vic. Proiessional Orchestra, which
has the best wishes of musical free-
thinkers, and the bitter enmity of the
worshippers of Marshall Hall, burst into
evidence again at Melbourne Town Hall the
other Tuesday afternoon. The audience
might have been much larger, but the roar of
applause at the end of the third section of
Tschaikowsky’s “Pathetic” Symphony must
have put the players on good terms with
themselves —barring only the trombones, who
committed one or two sad indiscretions dur-
ing the afternoon. Bizet’s “L’Arleslienne”
suite and the overture to “Flying Dutch-
man” were in the second half of a programme
that rather exceeded the 90 minutes allotted
to it. Zelman conducted this first concert
and will shake the stick again sometimes
hereafter; but the idea of the V.P.O. is to
have a variety of talent at the desk, includ-
ing brothers Slap, and Joseph Bradley, from

Sydney. Eight concerts in all are projected,
to be given always on a Tuesday, commenc-
ing at the unusual hour of 4 o’clock. It would
probably be a better arrangement to strike
up at 3.45 p.m., and make the first half of
the programme a curtain raiser to the piece
de resistance. It seems clear enough that
even the best of Melbourne orchestras cannot
thrive at present on the support of genuine
musical maniacs, the demented ones being
insufficiently numerous. Therefore, any de-
vice for capturing the moderately musical
crowd is worth experimenting with.
** * *
A male pianist who proclaims himself
English, and wears his hair moderately
short, and doesn’t prolong his recital with
“encore” pieces, has captured the affections
of all those Melbourne people who rallied
to the Carreno performances of yore. Leon-
ard Borwick is an artist of the unaggressive
order, with a particular talent for touching
the piano lightly and getting a singing tone
from the notes. He puts “soul” into his
reading of the music of many masters, and
is, in short, an interesting entertainer who
makes no attempt to present outward and
visible symptoms of genius. Thus far, he
has given three recitals at the Town
Hall, and one at the Athenaeum, and
next Saturday afternoon he will play
some more in the bigger building.
On this occasion he will clash with the
final matinge of Sousa’s Band at the Exhibi-
tion Building; but Borwick appeals to a
public which has already begun to reprove
itself for its weakness as regards the band.
In the most intensely refined circle the band
is now spoken of as a popular intoxicant,
suitable only for the beer-drinking classes.
And the multitude with the popular thirst is
certainly rushing to Sousa’s bars of music.
** * *
West (Melbourne), who has been making
people better acquainted with “Our Navy,”
has also been unrolling “Woodcutters in the
Tyrol,” which furnishes glimpses of Tyrolese
peasants, pastimes and sports. Other pic-
tures recently screened included “Tontolini
and the Straw Hat,” a wild comic film ; “La
Favorita,” a pictorial adaptation of Doni-
zetti’s opera; and “Her Child’s Honor,” an
odd domestic drama with an unimpeachable
moral.
*# * *
At the Prahran Lyric (Vic.) recent audi-
ences have been watching the birth and de-
velopment of a mushroom, besides getting-
many views of birds and beasts at the Lon-
don Zoo. People have also taken interest in
the tragedy of a clown, and have noted the
capers of a reverend gent who borrowed his
adventures from “What Happened to Jones.”
Incidentally, much admiration has been
lately excited by a brave Red Indian who
rescues a che-ild from bold, bad whites. At
the Lyric all Red Indians are noble, though
in real life they are often strangers alike to
sentiment, scruples and soap.
* * * *■
The Adelaide Tivoli still has a strong co.,
headed by R. A. Roberts, whose time in Aus-
tralia is coming to an end. Peggy Pryde
makes a welcome reappearance; and among
the others are Eunice and Zena, finished
musicians; Sydney Bach, a cheery soul from
Wales; and Scot Gibson, from Scotland,
equally cheery though more sophisticated.
Also Irving Sayles galvanises and holds to-
gether the first part as no other man can.
Sousa and his band have moved on, after
filling the Exhibition, day and night, fox-
most of a week. Sousa’s manager is Nigel
Bi-ock, an old Adelaide amateur. The Royal’s
week of “Charley’s Aunt” showed R. C.
Tearle as a pleasantly easy and humorous
Aunt, supported by local amateurs under
assumed names. These young people,
trained on Galsworthy and Shaw by the Lit-
erary Theatre, sailed briskly and effectively
through the old farce. (Time affects these
things in odd ways. The fizz of a syphon,
heard “off,” and telling that father is having
a stiff b. and s. before proposing to the
widow, is a mild substitute for the pop of a
sodawater-cork which used to announce the
same.) The Marlow co. has now come in
with “A Girl’s Temptation,” a subject on
which Adelaide takes up the pose of knowing
nothing, though willing to learn. West’s
pictures are specialising in the army and
navy: the Wondergraph at the Town Hall
has a French rendering of Irving’s “Lyons
Mail” ; while the Lyceum and the Vitascope,
having thoughtfully had an operator and a
camera present at the Deluge and at the
great nail-driving contest, Jael v. Sisera, are
now reaping the reward of their enterprise.
The servant trouble is spreading in the
Commonwealth Ministry. Kink O’Malley
has now announced that he is going to dis-
continue the use of formal endings to his
correspondence, and from this out isn’t going
to have the honor to be anybody’s “obedient
servant.”
Vic. Attorney-General J. Dry Brown ob-
served the other day :
I regard it as a most hopeful sign, that whilst the
population is steadily rising, the number of prisoners
in our gaols is gradually decreasing.
VV ell, Dry Brown has the keys; so what is
to stop him making the place a perfect
paradise by liberating all the prisoners?
As a start, he recently, by virtue of his
powers under an amending Crimes Act, re-
leased seven perjurers who had served about
a third of their two years’ term; and when
men who pollute the fountain of justice
are freed, almost any other sort of prisoner
who is kept a prisoner has good reason to
complain of the injurious differentiation, as
B. Smith would say.
“Melb’s” send-off to his old cook: —
I wave adieu to Estevan Parer, better known by
his English first name of Stephen. There were four
Parer brothers: Frank, Steve, Philly, and Johnny.
Frank came to Melbourne first, from Spain; but
Steve proved the masterful man. He could not
speak English, yet in two years he had got along
well enough to partner with Martin Arenas in the
Spanish Restaurant, Bourke-street, now Cole’s Book
Arcade. Next the Parers were heard of at the Duke
de la Victoria Restaurant, the time-honored “Duke.”
Steve had imported brothers Phil and John. When
they had made enough, all three returned to .their
native Spanish town of Alella, where the three were
married to their old sweethearts on the same day.
It was characteristic of the sturdy Parers, and of
their donnas, this waiting. They all three came
back to Australia, and now there is a goodly colony.
Send us such! But such are not sent —they have
the pluck to come. The Parers were lucky in coming
before the State stopped issuing hotel licenses to
restaurants; but, though they could thus fix you up
with the drink, the eating has always been the lead-
ing article in their faith. In the boom they put
the profit on many a thousand meal into land, and
got badly knocked about; but their ship righted.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Lud” : Sketch of the career of Irving, sen. Pupil
of Hoskins, 1854-5. First stage appearance in Sun-
derland, 1856. Became a stock actor in Edinburgh,
supporting Barry Sullivan and other stars. Acted
elsewhere with Brooke, after that actor’s return
from Australia. First London appearance, 1859 ;
small part in “Ivy Hall.” Immediately returned to
the provinces. Joined with Maccabe in exposing the
Davenport Brothers at Liverpool. Became leading
man at Manchester, following Neville and preceding
Dampier. Was the original Redburn in “The Lan-
cashire Lass” at Liverpool. Made a hit in Manches-
ter in Boueicault’s “Hunted Down.” Boucicault
procured his re-appearance in London, after seven
years, as Doricourt, in “The Belle’s Stratagem.”
Engaged at the .Queen’s, with Toole. Did well in
sundry plays. After seeing him as Sikes, Dickens
said he would be a great actor. Succeeded in “The
Two Roses,” at the Vaudeville, 1870. Engaged by Bate-
man for the Lyceum. Failed as Landry in “Fan-
chon.” Did well as Jingle in “Pickwick.” Then
his memorable hit in “The Bells,” 1871. Thence-
forward plain sailing. “The Bells,” 150 nights;
“Charles the First,” 120; “Richelieu,” 80. Also
performed “Eugene Aram” before his Hamlet triumph
of 200 nights. His management, with Ellen Terry
engaged, began 1878. Chief productions: “Hamlet,”
“Othello” (with Booth), “Merchant of Venice,”
“Much Ado About Nothing,” “Faust,” (most suc-
cessful of all), “Romeo and Juliet,” “Twelfth
Night,” “Macbeth,” “Richard III.,” “King Lear,”
“Henry VIII.,” “Becket,” “Cymbeline,” “Corio-
lanus.”
“L.H.P.”: “Hamlet” as an opera seems absurd, but
Ambroise Thomas, whose “Mignon” is well known to
Australians, furnished one which was produced in
Paris in 1868. Barbier and Carre, the librettists,
had but one object—to make Ophelia the heroine.
Thomas also wished to provide a role for Christine
Nilsson, then the idol of Paris. With the aid of
some charming Scandinavian melodies, he fitted
Nilsson with a character in which she was exquisite.
The following year saw the production of the
piece at Covent Garden with the original
Ophelia, and Santley as the Dane. In the
Covent Garden season of 1890, our own
Madame Melba appeared as Ophelia to the Hamlet
of Lassalle. Of later years, a tenor, Declery, has
been seen as the operatic Hamlet. Like the knighted
Tree, he makes the Dane wear a beard. Dramati-
cally speaking, the opera is no doubt full of
“bloomers,” the burlesque play scene and the Spring
ballet suggesting nothing but a grotesque travesty
of the tragic play. The German stage has a more
workmanlike edition of the opera in five acts.
“Polonius” : Irving’s “Hamlet” seems to me a very
fine performance; but why cut the play to pieces ? , and
probably other old-stagers, went to listen to the
music of the fine speeches, and found several of
them slaughtered. The king’s abortive prayer, which
gives the note of remorse to his villainy, and al-
most redeems the “lecherous, treacherous, remorseless,
kindless villain,” is cut away. Then the advice to
the players, which is strong evidence that Hamlet’s
madness was feigned, is knifed; while that beautiful
speech beginning—-
“Horatio, thou art e’en as just a man
As e’er my conversation cop’d withal,”
and containing the finest tribute to a friend ever
written, has been blue-pencilled. The lines—
“ Since my dear soul was mistress of her choice,
And could of men distinguish, her election
Hath sealed thee for herself; for thou hast been
As one, in suffering all, that suffers nothing; . . .
A man, that fortune’s buffets and rewards
Has ta’en with equal thanks” —
are worth a lot of the rest of the play. Part of the Grave-
diggers’ dialogue was cut, too. If I went to “Faust”
and found the “Salve Dimora,” the “Jewel Song”
and the “Soldiers’ Chorus” cut, or heard “Carmen”
without the “Toreador” and the “Flower” songs, or
went to “Tannhauser” and discovered that the
“Venusburg” music was left out, I should feel as
indignant as I was when I felt the gaps in this
“Hamlet.”
“Spotlight”: One thing I think should be pointed
out regarding The Bulletin’s criticism of H. B.
Irving’s “Hamlet.” The critic treats it as a replica
of Sir Henry Irving’s performance. I have H. B.’s
assurance that he never saw his father in the part;
consequently it is an achievement on his own basis.
The personal likeness of father and son is, of course,
striking, and heredity has gone farther: there is the
same “feeling” towards the part. But where the
elder Irving was difficult to follow, by reason of his
methods being obscure, H. B. has the educated intel-
ligence to make his meaning hit the audience the
whole time. An English critic summed up Sir Henry
absolutely on this point. “In his creations,” he
wrote, “there was always something esoteric and
essential, which eluded and defied analysis; he sur-
prised and captured strange beauties; within him
there was always sequestered and withdrawn an
idiosyncratic and inscrutable soul which flowered
into fantastic and gargoylish tricks of speech and
looks and gesture, but never expressed the whole of
its intention.” H. B. Irving’s acting is from with-
out, inwards; Sir Henry worked from inward, with-
out. There is in H. B. Irving’s reading “a burning
lucidity” ; his Hamlet is wary and purposeful. His
soliloquies are remarkable ; he makes “Hamlet” think
aloud.
When H. B. Irving told a send-off supper party that
he and his brother had often wished they were related
to Ethel Irving, he might just as well have deplored
his bad luck in not having Washington Irving for a
deceased uncle. H. B.’s name is actually Brodribb
by birth registration, as his father’s was. But Ethel
Irving acts under the name she was born to, and
now that she has made it a big one, her theatrical
namesakes stand rather in the light of her reputation.
Probably she wishes the famous Henry Irving and
his sons had gone into the “profesh” under the name
they were born to.
“Blue Mask”: It will probably be found true that
H. B. Irving, like his father, is the servant of his
own personality, and cannot make it properly nt any
but histrionic affinities. The man whose mind natur-
ally turns to the sinister side of study, as shown in
his “Life of Judge Jeffreys,” and “French Criminals
of the Nineteenth Century,” will not _ catch on to
comedy and romantic drama as he will to tragedy.
For myself I’d like to see him play Bill Sikes, the
character of which his father made so terrible and
ferocious a picture; but I suppose it is impossible.
Actor “Jimmy” Atholwood has rearranged “Ham-
let” so as to make a connected story with a suc-
cession of tableaux. He has added nothing, but has
re-arranged and dovetailed scenes, cutting out lines,
or transposing them to suit his plan. James _B. is
confident the public will like Shakswood far better
than it ever liked Shakspeare—if it ever gets a
chance of judging.
The stage-struck sisterhood of “Melbourne Gal-
lery Girls” seems to have saved up a burst of adula-
tion for Ethel Irving, judging from the fact that an
M.G.G. “deputation” presented her with a bouquet
as soon as she arrived. Mummer-worship had
slackened in Melbourne after the Asches were dis-
posed of, and the fanatics were feeling the want of
a new idol when Miss Irving came to hand.
From London: Laurence Irving’s dramatised ver-
sion of the story of Margaret Catchpole, at London
Duke of York’s, has been rather roughly handled by
the critics. It is too crudely melodramatic and old-
fashioned for their tastes. Irving plays the villain
with what the Times critic calls “hereditary snarls
and grunts,” a not very polite allusion to some of his
famous father’s mannerisms. His wife (Mabel Hack-
ing) impersonates Madcap Peggy; but whether it is
really she or a double that shins up the 40ft. wall of
Ipswich Gaol, while her lover or another double is
playing the fiddle on the other side, is a point on
which opinions differ. The probabilities are in favor
of a masculine double. Peggy’s famous ride from
Ipswich to London is shown on a cinematograph
screen. A naval spectacle of the Nelson era is intro-
duced ; also a troupe of dancers, who enliven the
piece and get most of the praise. The Australian
scenery in the last act is voted good by Australians
—and they ought to know best.
Ramaciotti rounded off his highly profitable con-
nection with J. O. Williamson, Ltd., by buying for
£25,000 the freehold of one of the J.O.W. theatres
—the Sydney Royal—along with the inevitable pub
and the accessories. It looks dirt cheap; yet on
June 26, 1861, Fitzgerald (with whose trustees the
deal was made) bought the lot, including the ruins
of the recently-burned Prince of Wales Theatre,
which stood on the site, for £3250.
Touching Melba’s first re-appearance at Oovent
Garden this season, in “La Bohfeme,” London
Referee said: “Mimi is merely a simple-minded
workgirl, with a tender heart and jealous tempera-
ment ; but it is probable that Melba has done more
to increase her popularity as an artist by the per-
sistency with which she has embodied the character
than by the brilliancy of her singing in other roles.. .
She sang with fascinating purity of tone and ex-
quisite finish, and also acted with a simple direct-
ness and earnestness in the third and fourth acts that
intensified the pathos of the scenes.” Who was it
said that Melba had no “soul”? After witnessing
her emotional acting in “La Traviata,” Hugh Ward
protested years ago that she was the best Camille
he had seen, bar Sarah Bernhardt.
“O’Kasion”: A few days ago Conway Wingfield
sailed from Sydney for New York. He has been
many things in his time. In Fleet-street he was on
the staff of a religious weekly, after turning his back
on the allurements of a Nonconformist pastorate.
Then he did a lot of acting in England and South
Africa before he tired of it about six years ago, and
came to Australia to try dairy-farming. Tiring also
of the cow, he joined Meynell and Gunn to produce
a pretty bad failure, “The Rake’s Wife.” He was
responsible for an excellent Irish musician in that
lurid horror, "A Warning to Women,” which Maud
Hilyard perpetrated; and he showed to advantage
as the cheated American lover in “Jim the Pen-
man,” when Meynell and Gunn revived it, with
Madge Mclntosh and Beatty. When Nellie Stewart
did “Sweet Kitty Bellairs,” a couple of years ago,
Wingfield got a good chance as Captain O’Hara, and
in Brisbane he went on at a few hours’ notice to
play the King in “Sweet Nell.” But, generally,
Wingfield was better than the work he found to do
here, and it shouldn’t be hard for him to strike
better luck somewhere else.

“Bookware”: Ben Jonson’s masque, “The Vision of
Delight,” was introduced in the Coronation gala per-
formance at Tree’s Theatre, in order to cram in all
the most beautiful women on the London stage. The
masque opens with something like the transformation
scene in a Christmas pantomime—a bevy of classical
dames, suggestive of Venuses, but here described as
Grace, Love, Harmony, Revel, Sport, Laughter and
the like. Wonder gazes on them, and delivers a
swathe of poetry. Mrs. Langtry is mentioned first
in the cable, so perhaps she took this part. A Sea
Monster is delivered of six Harlequins and six Colum-
bines. (Dancing here.) Then Night appears in her
chariot, bespangled with stars. She speaks a piece.
Fantasy appears from the clouds, and has a turn.
(More dancing.) The Hours all pass by. Next is
the Bower of Zephyrs, with elocution by Peace. Ar-
rive the Glories of Spring. (Singing here.) The
whole winds up with the appearance of Aurora in her
car. All the language is in the exuberant classic
vein in which Jonson was inimitable.
“Schupin”: The easy, casual way in which pianist
Leonard Borwick grips his audience reminds Mel-
bourne’s older stagers of one Friedenthal, a German
pianist of the sweet and gentle school, who used to
give recitals in the Athenaeum Hall. Borwick is a
finer executant than Friedenthal was, but not more
likeable. For a pianist of his type the Melbourne
Town Hall seems absurdly big, ugly and unsociable.
His warmest admirers are the people who don’t sit
at the far end of the building, whereas a Mark I-lam-
bourg punches the keyboard with special designs on
the way-back portion of his audience. The same
instrument serves a double purpose. A piano player
delights; a forte player excites.
“Star”: A profusely be-whiskered person, in big
boots, loomed before the circle doorkeeper in a
metropolitan theatre one evening recently. He was
backed by a portly wife, and behind her was a string
of three —two freckled daughters and a very large
son of 13, jutting out of a very small, tight suit.
The man with the whiskers wanted to get in “on the
gun,” and the noise he made when the check-taker
raised technicalities disturbed the whole audience, and
threw the players out of step. The man said he would
go in, his wife seconded the motibn and the children
supported it. The representative in front was has-
tily summoned to quell the insurrection, and for his
enlightenment the visitor from Tinbilly produced
an ancient document which proved to be an unre-
ceipted bill for three weeks’ board against the gifted
“sole lessee and manager.” The bill was 17 years
old. The holder was going to “take it out,” he
said. Although far beyond the statute of limitations,
the bill (a relic of unhappier days) was honorably
met.
A gruesome playlet, with a earning moral, was
produced in Paris lately. A young actress is pho-
tographed on her death-bed by her husband, after the
doctor, hurriedly called in, has given a certificate of
death. When the photo, is developed the eyes of
the “deceased” are seen to be wide open. There-
upon the coffin, now two days buried, is dug up and
opened. The corpse is found to have broken a blood-
vessel in screaming, presumably to make herself
heard! Moral: Never get buried until your post-
mortem photo, has been developed.
THE LEGS OF MARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some poets sing of arms that cling,
And hair black, brown or brindle;
For Cis and Kate still celebrate
The old, time-honored swindle;
With hair that’s dyed, and teeth supplied
Per dentist-cove, they amble;
They play their cards with simple bards
Who always lose the gamble.
False teeth deceive, and man may grieve
That form and tint should vary;
I rise just here to hymn with cheer
The lovely legs of Mary!
Let Jess and Sue have eyes of blue,
Let Jane have lips of coral;
Spring poets all are free to squall—-
With these I shall not quarrel!
I do not care whence Celia’s hair
Is bought or lent or rented;
And Julia’s chest may stand the test
Or not—I’m quite contented !
I simply beg to state that leg,
When false, deceiveth nary;
And oh! I do admire the two
Most scrumptious legs of Mary!
In Mary’s praise this psalm I’d raise
With fervor truly loyal;
No falsehood here, so let us cheer—
I know her legs are royal;
We know that Eve doth oft deceive,
She’s full of tricks and cunning;
The leg that’s false, it cannot waltz—
It’s also poor for running!
But Mary mine, she’s superfine—-
Ah, she’s no sylph-like fairy;
A bouncing miss, who’s good to kiss,
God save the legs of Mary!

A GENUINE HAIRGROWER.
We have received the following recipe from
a correspondent who has found it to be of
great value. Our correspondent says that
this mixture is a hair tonic which, if rubbed
briskly into the scalp night and morning with
the finger tips, will not fail to promote the
growth of hair, to remedy baldness, to re-
store grey hair to its natural color, and to
destroy dandruff.
Take | dram of Menthol Crystals and put
them into a bottle containing 3oz. of Bay
Rum and see that they are entirely dis-
solved, then add loz. of Lavona de Compos6e,
and if desired one teaspoonful of To-lvalon
perfume. Shake well and let it stand for
half an hour, when it will be ready for use.
Other readers will doubtless be glad to try
this I’emedy which our correspondent _ has
sent. We understand that the ingredients
may be obtained from any chemist.
CAUTION.—As this is doubtless a re-
markable hair grower, it should be applied
only, where hair is desired.*

THE WIFE : “ I knew it! He tried to get up

The case of Polley, whom a Melbourne
jury recently acquitted on a charge of shoot-
ing one of B. Smith’s colored Mends at
Warburton (Vic.), let loose a small pre-
cedent that ought to grow into a fine large
animal. When Justice a’Beckett arrived
in court he found all the paraphernalia pre-
sent except the accused’s counsel, and he
declined to empanel the jury until Horse-
whiskers turned up. When he did turn up
Justice a’Beckett smote him with a fine of
£5 for “contempt” in that he had kept the
whole legal outfit waiting. Maybe the pre-
cedent —that the learned person in horsehair
can’t dodge his hired service any more than
can the illiterate person in moleskin —will
grow, and then the learned spieler who ac-
cepts fees for cases in which he never ap-
pears (being “engaged in another court, y’r
Honor”) will become less frequent than he
is now.
“Adelaide”: So tramcars in Sydney have
open doors facing the other line, and people
riding on the inside footboards cannon into
and kill each other (B. 22/6/’ll) ! And
“if the footboards were hinged the one on
the closed side could be put out of action” !
They could, and they are —in Adelaide. In
fact, as I have often said about so many
other things (land tax, absentee tax, absence
of slums, prevention of expectoration, street-
cleaning, totalisators, and good Australian
fruit-vendors are some of them), send over
to so-called sleepy Adelaide if you want to
see how tilings can be done. You can t get
out of a car on the wrong or dangerous
side in this State; even the old liorse-cars
saw to that. Well, you can; but it means
ducking under a bar and then getting down
without a step. I have done it to
save trouble in a croivded car. The first
time, a passing bobby told me earnestly
what would happen if I did it again. Ihe
second time, the driver was down as soon as
I was, took me by the collar, and firmly
helped me back by the unlawful way 1 had
come. There has not been any third time.
Mount Donna Buang, 40S9ft. high, is a
new beauty resort now being opened by
the Victorian Government. It is 48 miles
from Melbourne, and at present wears a
thick cap of snow. Last week a Ministerial
party went thither, accompanied by 150
non-official picnickers. Acting-Minister for
Works Edgar was in the lead, and,
at a little gathering after the journey, some-
one with no sense of poetry or humor sug-
gested that the name should be changed to
“Mount Edgar.” This suggestion was agreed
to by the crowd, and is to be submitted to
the Lands Department. If the Lands De-
partment has any sense of the fitness or
things it will kick the idea so hard
that it won’t be seen again. “Donna” is a
name that has been given by the blacks to a
number of Gippsland streams and moun-
tains. Old aborigines say it means “a big
woman.” The theory of experts is that the
name got into the language of the blacks a
century or two ago, when Spaniards visited
the South Gippsland coast, where some ot
them were shipwrecked. The words “donna
and “Madonna” were frequently on the lips
of these pious freebooters, and probably
were among the little things thev left be-
hind them. Anyhow, “Donna Buang” seems
to mean “Lady of the Waters,” or “Queen ot
the Waters,” and is interesting if only oil
account of its supposed origin, whereas Ed-
gar” stands merely for the Wowsensm o.:
the soon-to-be-forgotten Weary-Willie Gov-
ernment.

“Gwy”: “Hamer” and “Pera” needn’t
wrangle over whether the station book-keep-
er’s hard case should go to a Wages Board
or not: it has already got there. One of
this underpaid individual’s manifold activi-
ties lies in the station “store.” A Wages
Board is on the eve of pronouncing an
award for country shop assistants ; but be-
fore the chairman closed his notebook the
storekeeping employers reminded him that
some of the stations, not content to
merely supply permanent hands and shear-
ers. also served the sparse population of the
locality as well. The emp'overs reckoned it
wasn’t a fair thing that the squatters
should sell goods to all and sundry in compe-
tition with regular storekeepers who might
have to pay a minimum wage of 505., per-
haps more. And the union hacked them up.
Inasmuch, however, as the station book-
keeper-CKm-tutor-CMm-storekeeper might die
of shock if he were handed a weekly 505., the
big pastoral cos. will, from humane motives,
agree to restrict their retailing of plum jam
and plug tobacco to constant hands, pro-
vided they are exempted from the award.
At least that is what the barrister they
rushed along to the Board suggested.
OLD SAWS RESET.

“Akenehi” fires at a 5000-mile range:—
I wish you would hurry up the Transcontinental
Railway. Letters I posted at Port Darwin on April
26, for Sydney, cannot leave there until May 8. We
are now within 12 blistering hours of Manilla, and
our captain and hope tell a flattering tale that the
steamer Empire will pass us on her way south this
afternoon, and that she may stop and add these re-
marks to her ballast. It is for her the letters wait
at Port Darwin. For her and the Bth of May, and
your winter will be almost at its noon when they
reach Sydney!
Brisbane, Rockhampton, Thursday Island and
Cairns left me clammy and unimpressed, but
Darwin dried me, exhilarated, and impressed me
as (in spots) the most beautiful place I have seen
since Java. It was hot, but with the heat with which
I should imagine Hichens fires his Egyptian stories.
A clear, windless, amber heat, and pungent with the
scent of some fragrant weed. A heat that makes you
feel you have wings, not leaden pellets, on your
shoes. You recall Shelley’s phrase, the “painted air,”
in “The Sensitive Plant?” Well, Darwin is the
first clime I have come to in which the air has the
suggestion of being painted.
In the garden of the Residency, giant seed-pods
falling from their stems felt hard and hot as warmed
gun-metal, and the bursting of pods that had not
fallen were as the reports of fairy artillery. The
only sounds they were that broke the windless silence.
Our hosts were the Resident (Mr. Justice Mitchell)
and Miss Mitchell, and our fellow guests the Bishop
of Carpentaria (Gilbert White) and his sister. That
I should live to chronicle a Chinaman “amongst
those present.” Chickweed (the nearest I can get
to his name) has been at the Residency through
three or four Administrations, and. is a Chinese Cap-
tain Wallington, though he is head only of the domes-
tic staff. His manner of standing before the guests
he has just served with tea and squashes, and ex-
plaining the flora in the big, dim rooms, and the
agreeable coolness now apparent in the night-air, has
a quite royal aloofness and condescension.
The beauty drive of Darwin is through the Botanic
Gardens to East Point. Mr. N. Holtze, who is Cura-
tor of the Gardens, as well as Government Secretary
and Sheriff, and a few etceteras., was our driver. A
gang of native prisoners, working between the glit-
tering sea on our left and a wall of tropical vegeta-
tion on our right, was chaperoned by a bulky white
in khaki, shading himself with a big umbrella.
“Pardon me,” said the lesser authority , to the higher
as he stepped forward, raising his hand and lowering
his sunshade, “but four of my ‘boys’ have bolted into
that.” He indicated the towering wall of jungle that
looked bolt-proof except to a snake or a lizard.
“Who are they?” asked the higher authority.
“Nipper and Wally and Nickie and Towie. All
short-sentence chaps, too,” he added, in an injured
tone.
“Oh, well, take your men back and get out the
trackers,” and on dashed our ponies, and the Sheriff
was again the Curator, pointing out the numerous
fibre plants that will be such additions to the resources
of this great country. On our way back we met a
mounted constable going out to the scene of the van-
ishing trick. A handsome youngster, lean and long,
cold of eye, fine of jaw, thin of lip. And riding like
—like an Australian.
You can touch almost any Victorian Judge
in a sore place now, for the whole tribe of
them is said to be riled because Brown, the
Attorney-General, who of recent years prac-
tised mine-management, but never law, has
“reviewed” so many sentences. In private
places the Judge will tell you that he doesn’t
object to appeals—though the Appeal Court
is ’ generally wrong, of course, if it upends
the thing. What he does object to is that so
many fellows should stay in gaol because
they haven’t got influential friends to go
worrying an Attorney-General to let them
out. That is the curse of a system that
doesn’t include a criminal court of appeal :
it takes influence to start the semi-political
Attorney-General machinery. And then, as
likely as not, the machinery has not been
tested, and can’t do its work properly.
“The Religious Editor” : Last week Mel-
bourne and Auckland simultaneously dis-
covered that the kitchens of some of their
public fooderies were places to shudder over.
Melbourne has not yet plugged its nostrils
and investigated fully; Auckland, _on the
contrary, has ; through its chief sanitary in-
spector, a fair idea of the awfulness which
may lie behind the polite waiter. There will
probably be a boom in lunch-baskets when
the news gets round. Years ago I worked next
to a Melbourne oyster saloon. In front it was
polished cedar, marble, nickelplate and
rose-tinted electric lights; behind, there was
little else but dust, sodden bags, putrid
oysters, rats and manure. The cook smoked
continuously, and looked as if he had given
up washing in early youth; his assistant
looked as if he had never washed in his life.
The yard smelt to Heaven and all the inter-
vening planets; but the boss didn’t worry
about it so long as it didn’t smell to the cus-
tomers. Now and then, when there had been
too many deaths in the oyster family, we
used to ring up the Nuisance Inspector and
draw his nose to the scene. He never seemed
to be able to discover the outrages for him-
self. Possibly his nose had been rendered
smell-blind through overwork. Some day
the restaurant proprietor who neglects to in-
vite his patrons to inspect his cook and his
kitchen will be suspected and avoided like the
man who refuses to allow an intending pur-
chaser to examine his books.

Interesting extract from the report of the
Kalgoorlie (W.A.) health inspector on a
local Jap laundry: “The bedroom, kitchen
and laundry are combined, and there is no
closet.” And the Society maidens and the
Society mannikins hug to their bosoms the
linen laundered in a hovel which is bedroom,
kitchen and laundry—and other things—
combined, and “sprinkled” probably with
water squirted through the nice heathen's
teeth.
“Vagabond”: Apropos the struggle, most
marked in South Australia, to get the
rifles, etc., that were taken from the Boers
exhibited in pride and in public places.
Were you ever at Teneriffe? If you were
you probably felt for a few minutes like the
Boer must feel when we throw these things
in his face. Hanging on the walls of the
cathedral there is a British flag which the
Dagoes collared at the time Nelson paid
them a call, and found them very much at
home —so much so that he had to
hustle back to the boats, and be thankful for
small mercies when he got there. The Span-
ish guide who shows the visitors round
never fails to point out this interesting
relic, and he usually adds that if Nelson
had been a real gent, and done the square
thing, he would have sent them the empty
sleeve he had no use for afterwards, just to
hang up alongside as a climax to the story.
You’ll usually hear some humiliated Brit,
trying to explain to the guide that this was
the only defeat that Nelson ever, ever, ever
had to swallow; but the guide only smiles,
and remarks that he never heard what Nel-
son did in other places.
IN 1915?
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The ancient painter painted up the road a yard or
two,
And painted down again, a lovely, pale, transparent
blue;
And as he painted, slowly, with a calculating eye,
Towards his victim stealthily the canvasser drew
nigh.
“Goo’ morn’,” the canvasser remarked, and “Morn’ ”
the old man said —
And painted up a yard or two towards the buggy-
shed,
And painted back again, the while the canvasser dis-
played
A wealth of tailor-patterns, and persuaded him to
trade.
The township slumbered; slowly did the old man
wipe his fist
Upon his apron; slowly then the patterns did he
twist
And turn and thumb, and stared at them, it seemed
regretfully.
“We tailor cheap,” the traveller said —-“with extra
trousers free !”
“Well, / dunno!” The painter heaved a sigh and
took his brush
And painted on. The canvasser, with language on
the rush,
Did everything to snare his prey an honest man could
do —-
And still the painter painted yards of pale, trans-
parent blue.
“The fact is—no!” the old man said, and painted
up and down,
And once more wiped his hands, and took the pat-
terns, with a frown.
A shade of sadness dwelt awhile upon his counten-
ance,
As though some old-time might-have-been he pon-
dered. Then —“No chance,”
He muttered ; “no, it ain’t no good yer talkin’,” —
and began
Once more to streak pale paint. “Y’ see, I ain’t a
married man!”
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
A VAIN APPEAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sleek Reform-apostle spoke
A piece about the “grasping” grafter—
And, as at some new, splendid joke,
The crowd broke forth in roars of laughter.
“I do believe,” he beat the air,
And cackled, for “fair play” appealing—
“That most Australians, everywhere,
Are gentlemen ! ” He spoke with feeling.
“You’re wrong!” a voice derisive rose,
The while the audience laughed like Hades —
“Why, Kink O’Malley’s census shows
That more’n half of ’em are ladies!”

June’s expiring kick in Sydney took the
form of a record fog on the Harbor, with
disastrous results to traffic. Two of the
ferry steamers had a head-on collision, while
a Manly steamer butted into a collier which
had thoughtfully anchored in the fairway.
A third accident helped to show that the
over-the-harbor resident gambles when he
sallies forth on a foggy morning. A Bal-
main ferry steamer was nosing her way
along, when suddenly a steam lighter with a
punt alongside loomed out of the fog. Both
craft went full speed astern, but couldn’t get
clear. The iron bow end of the punt struck
the ferry steamer on the quarter, jumped the
protecting sponson and caught the flimsy
ladies’ cabin —fortunately empty. The wood-
work and glass collapsed like cardboard, and
the side of the structure was demolished.
Unfortunately for the Evening Blood-
stain, the Lady Rawson was bound from Syd-
ney to Balmain. Had she been en route to
the metropolis with a load of city workers,
there must have been a tale to tell Of a
catastrophe more horrible than that of Rich-
mond. The incident should be a solemn
warning to the Harbor Trust. It is, of
course, impossible to build the superstruc-
ture of a ferry boat so that it won’t be
affected when it crashes into an iron punt.
Therefore to protect the ferry steamers,
there should be special fog traffic-regula-
tions, one of which would prohibit the cross-
ing of ferry routes by punts, barges, light-
ers, etc.
At Mount Mary (S.A.), the other day, a
married couple drove off after the wedding
with the object of striding the nearest town ;
but they lost their way, and wandered
round in the scrub all night. In honor of
their introduction to married life, they in-
tend to christen their eldest son Mallee.

* * * *
The crowning of George recalls that im-
mediately after he had finished the corona-
tion of Edward, the old Archbish. of Can-
terbury fell to the floor unconscious. He
may have been overheated by the “fierce
light that beats upon a throne” ; or it may
have been the unaccustomed congregation
after being used to addressing two or three
reluctant sleepers; or, again, it may have
been simply horror on reflecting that the
alleged church of Christ has just been con-
verted into a temple for the performance of
pagan rites.

TERMS OF SURRENDER.
“ Very well , I know I was wrong .?”
"I know that , dear—but do you know I was
right?”
Reading of the kisses that were show-
ered upon His Gracious apropos of the Cor-
onation, one is reminded of the Adelaide
alderman who insisted on kissing George’s
hand when he held a reception, as Duke of
York, in the Holy City. George was giving
the levee his royal forefinger to shake, but
the civic father couldn’t content himself
with that. He grasped the hand and bent
reverently towards it. The Duke tried to
free himself, and blushed like a lobster —he
was the reddest Heir Apparent in Christen-
dom. But the bending alderman, without
raising his eyes to the royal countenance,
hung on to the royal hand until he had
smacked it with his lips. After which he
slowly retired backwards, feeling every inch
a courtier. In revenge, they made him —a
C.M.G.!

“Kaitangata”: All over America, as well
as in other countries, the use of stone and
steel for important buildings is nowadays
almost universal. In San Francisco tower-
ing edifices of these materials stood not
only the fire, but the earthquake itself. Here
in Sydney the building that it is most import-
ant should be fireproof, the Registrar-Gen-
eral’s offices, is so filled with timber inter-
nally as to make the new and handsome-
looking pile, on its lofty site, suitable for a
stupendous bonfire, especially as the slates
are laid on a network of “kindling” in the
form of narrow rafters. Why, Macquarie,
a century ago, builded with more foresight!
“Chasuble”: Archbishop Clarke,
of Melbourne, has another rose-thorn
in his softest flesh. Ridley College,
established near the Uni., pushes
ahead ; while Trinity stagnates. The
latter —High Church, was amazingly
successful in its early years, turning
out Bishop Stretch, of Newcastle;
Bishop Green, of Ballarat; Bishop
Armstrong, of Wangaratta: Dean
Stephen, of Melbourne; and Vicar
Hughes, of St. Peter’s, Melbourne.
Since then it has gone right off,
though it remains under the same
admirable Dr. Deeper. The trouble
is there is no straw for the bricks.
Ridley Colleee is Evangelical, ar-
dently supported by Bishop Langley,
of Bendigo (formerly of Sydney) ;
Bishop Pain, of Gippsland (also for-
merly of N.S.W.) ; and Archdeacon
Nash, of Gippslend. Rich w rt men
have been pouring money into it, and
now it goes in for an expensive
building.

Most people will remember the
steamer Papanui, and the fuss that
was made when her owners kicked
at paying the price of her repairs in
Australia, and quietly slid her off
to sea in spite of official objections.
They preferred to chance her bust
and battered carcase to the mercy of
the ocean between here and Japan,
where they could get the repairs done
cheap. Her luck held her up, and
she made her port all right, and was
repaired and put into service again.
But the chean Jap. repairs are al-
ready hinting that they may be nas!y
as well as cheap, for the vessel is
reported to have staggered into
Perim, in the Red Sea, in a leaking
condition. It may be only a slight leak, of
course ; but, again, it may be that her engi-
neers and officers are sleeping with the life-
belts handy and the boats kept in a state of
unusual readiness for a prompt exit. Ban-
zai !
A Melbourne Society lady,
once an actress, who pos-
sesses a Past, was being
fitted at the milliner’s. The
gear was poised upon her
head, and the milliner step-
ped back to admire it.
“Beautiful,” she murmured
ecstatically. “Perhaps it
is,” said the owner of the
Past. “But a don’t care a
(naughty word) about the
front. They all wait till I
get past, and then turn and
stare. So fix me up beauti-
ful at the back.’
The pumpkin country showed its undying-
loyalty in its own fashion. Taree, one of
the N.S.W. North Coast towns, broke out
into a queer mixture of “religious” demon-
stration, procession and sports. The lead
was taken by an individual, who repre-
sented Mephistopheles, with crimson robes,
cloven feet and horns, and a toasting-fork
for a sceptre. Other characters “much ap-
preciated” were bridal couples, the brides
wearing harem skirts, and carrying bouquets
of cabbage leaves. Taree was giddily ex-
cited over this outburst of loyal and reli-
gious fervor; the only wet blanket was the
Salv’army captain, who called out his “sol-
diers,” and declined to follow “the Devil”
under anv circumstances. It was a quaint
way of celebrating the crowning of a mon-
arch, however you look at it; and the only
reasonable excuse offered to date is that the
proceedings were no more futile than those
which occurred at Westminster.
The demand for another residence for
Victoria’s sub-Excellency seems in no im-
mediate danger of being satisfied. Several
houses have been considered; but, though
they are sufficiently commodious, they are
not charming enough in other respects. Some
are too far from Melbourne; some have too
little “grounds”; others are deficient in
“style.” So the Government will have to
hunt further. Probably it now occurs to
the Government that, when it already pos-
sessed a mansion, it should have found a
Governor to suit the dwelling. That looks
like the most reasonable procedure when
the Excellency really doesn’t matter.

Fires everywhere fringed the coast, and there were

And doubtless the battle-fleet of Switzer-

“Boswell”: Though in the case alluded to
in B. 8/6/T1 the money-lender lost both
principal and interest because he had neg-
lected his triennial registration under the
N.S.W. law, there need be no commotion
among the casual sharks that haunt public
offices and other places, and oblige hard-ups
with a “tenner” at 120 per cent, or so. The
Act of 1905 defines a “moneylender” :
The expression “moneylender” in this Act shall in-
clude every person or company whose business is
that of moneylending, or who advertises or announces
himself, or holds himself out in any way as carrying
on that business, but shall not include (a) .. . (b)
. . . (c) . . . (d) any person or company bona fide
carrying on the business of banking or insurance, or
bona fide carrying on any business no t having for its
'primary object the lending of money in the course of
which and for the purpose whereof money is lent.
Thus it points the loaded barrel straight at
the professional, but doesn’t even reserve a
blank cartridge for the casual or amateur
shark.
CONCEALED IMPUDENCE.
NEWSBOY: “So long.”
The S. M. Herald, speaking about Coron-
ation “honors,” quite forgot what it is there
for: —
There may be instances where a title is meaning-
less ; where it has been bestowed upon a person
merely because he achieved an office, and managed
to hang on for several terms.
Yah, Pro-Boer ! Disloyalist! But what
sort of an “honor” does the Herald think a
title is when it can ever be handed out in
the way the Herald mentions?
IN MELLOW MOOD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In mellow mood of morning-tide
I sang her eyes of blue;
And found, as on 1 versified,
She poetastered too.
While I her azure orbs would hymn,
She trilled of eyes of brown;
And when I praised her ankles trim
Or gushed about her gown,
She lilted then of symmetry,
Of wealth of glossy hair,
Of step as light as step could be,
Of beauty lithe and fair.
I warbled then of constancy,
Of hearts both leal and true;
And, strange to say, my lovely She’
That theme was singing, too.
And as I ran, in frenzy fine,
Her charms enumerating,
I found the Sweet Thing Feminine
My verse was emulating.
Our rhyming tasks at last complete,
With all their sweet allusion,
We read in turn —as was but meet—
But, Oil I to my confusion,
I found while I in tuneful maze
Had sung her like a noodle,
In just my turn of word and phrase
She’d gushed about her poodle.

Extract of a letter from
the late Manager, “ Motor
Transit Company of Aus-
tralia,’’Glen Innes: “ During
the time I was managing
‘The Motor Transit Co. of
Australia,’ we had plying , , . , , , , ,
between Glen Innes and Inverell an 11-16 Clement-Bayard Oar, which we purchased as second-hand. We
did about 60,000 miles with her up to the time of my leaving (she is still going well). Nearly all the running
was done between Glen Innes and Inverell, over the famous Waterloo Range, some of the grades of which
are one in six. The Car was invariably overloaded, and was given very little care ; but in the face of the bad
usage she received at our hands she stood up wonderfully well, and the only repairs that were effected were
two ball races for road wheels and a set of new valves.” . , , .
Call and allow us to explain the reason why our prices for Cars generally, Commercial Lorries and High-
wheel Buggies will be found to be 25 per cent, lower than the usual Sydney quotations.

* * * *
“Geofred”: The evidence recently given
in the Federal Arbitration Court as to one shearer
impersonating another by arrangement for pecuniary
benefit or convenience, reminds me of Jones, who
made it worth Smith’s while to hand him his name
arid Contract to shear at Ruthen (Q.). There was a
new boss, and all went well with Jones, alias Smith,
until black sister Maggie visited the shed. Hearing
Jones addressed as Smith, she chipped in, “That
pfoiler not Smith!” The ensuing argument con-
cluded, for the time, by Jones declaring that “the
gin was off her rocker.” Next day, at s’moke-o,
Maggie turned up, nursing a half-caste boy, arid sat
down, grinning beside Jones. “ ’Ere,” said he,
wot’s your game? Git!” “Oh, I dunno,” replied
the lady, “last shearing time this piccaninny be-
longa Smith. You gib it the money, mine tink.”
That night Jones camped 10 miles from Ruthen.

“J.G.”: “Alba Stralia” (B. 15/6/’ll) is
quite right in saying that snakes won’t crawl over
bright white objects. That is, of course, if they are
dead snakes. The other sort will. One time, when
camped at Bungaree (Vic.), I merely laid my tent on
top of my doss to ward off the dew, as I was too
tired, or too lazy, or too something to fix up the
canvas. Towards morning I woke up with the feel-
ing that something was waltzing on top of the
clothes. On hastily striking a match I was para-
lysed to see a big black snake, which looked about
12 feet long, sitting up on top of my counterpane.
When I looked again, with the aid of a second
match, I saw that the big snake was really two.
The pair had evidently been canoodling on top of my
white covering.
“Ixion”: “Yalgun” (B- 22/6/T1), who
takes up the cudgels for the Cow Cocky, is a pur-
blind ass, and so, I suspect, were his ancestors for
about eight generations back. He says the average State
school-boy is seldom worth his breakfast. Wrong,
nearly every time! The Cow country boys may be
dull, but it is owing to Cow, and to nothing else;
and, as to their breakfast- —well, they often go with-
out it on account of the horned quadruped. With
regard to “Border View, K.C.” (B. 22/6/’ll), and
his remarks re the Cow Cocky’s regard for his
women folk, I knew a family in the Otway Forest
(Vic.) whose cows were milked by ma and the
girls, until one fine day pa bought some milking
machines. These acted splendidly, and pa and the
boys worked them for a while; but in a little time
the novelty wore off, and the men folk discovered
that milking was “too much fag,” whereupon they
hung up the machines for the moth and the rust to
corrupt, and once more ma and the girls do all the
milking and separating, without any help whatever
from the male portion of the family. Is this
“chivalry”—or simply cussed laziness?

“The Snake”: The usually-gentle ex-
Speaker McCourt (N.S.W.) smote the Royal Agri-
cultural Society with ah axe the other evening over
a proposed grant of extra land. Contending that the
dairyman and the pastoralist did not receive one iota
of benefit from the Society’s exhibitions, he showed
that, out of a revenue of £24,000, the society spent
about £4500 altogether in prizes. McCourt’s idea
that the big Easter show is merely a sort of glorified
circus is open to long and unprofitable argument; but
he certainly makes out a case for giving greater en-
couragement to the not-so-fat country folk by reduc-
ing entrance fees and giving assistance in the costly
carriage of stock for exhibition. A wealthy body that
spends more than half the amount of its annual
Government subsidy in banquets and other gorges^ —
the cost of the annual election dinner is set down at
£lO3 —can afford to spread its bank balance a bit
for the further benefit of the real object of its
existence.

“0.L.”: Perhaps I can give the reason
that “Gip” (B. 18/5/’ll) did not supply when she
remarked on the increasing quantity of Brer
Fox in the Monaro (N.S.W.). In my part of the
country (Limekilns) the increase is due to the
friendliness of some of the squattdrs. one who
permitted rabbit-trapping on his ground only on
condition that all foxes caught should be let go;
and he is not the only one who acts that way. The
argument of these short-sighted people is that the
fox is known to kill rabbits all the year round,
whereas there is no proof that he has ever killed a
sheep.
The most amazing epistle which the Vic-
torian Intelligence Bureau has yet received from any
of their assisted innocents came from one who de-
sired to know how he could obtain an injunction to
prevent a neighbor shooting rabbits upon his bit of
land!

“Mr. Ocky”: Came across it lately— a
place where there is continuous rain. At Barra-
munga, near Forrest (Vic.), the oldest inhabitants
speak fondly of a certain far-back time when the
weather struck for one day; and the younger genera-
tions are incessantly calling on History to repeat
itself. That seems to be their eternal hope. What
a paradise for Kink O’Malley 1
“Panhandle Pete”: In Wonthaggi, the
Victorian coal and publess town, five shillings will
keep the sparrer in beer, lashings of it, for 12
months. The cadgus operandi is as follows: Having
joined one of the local clubs (ss. will do the trick),
the sparrer accosts the thirsty passer-by who is not
a member. Would he like a drink? Nine times
out of ten he would. “Right; give’s sixpence, and
I’ll take you into the club and shout.” And, as
beers are thrippence a pot, his word is as good as his
deed.
• * * *
“Gip”: The best man I ever saw hand-
ling wild horses was a Monaro (N.S.W.) native, a
lean, soft-voiced six-footer, who - could gallop . a
brumby where the ordinary rider would have to lead
him. This expert was as gentle with the animals
as he might be with a child, but he could do just
as he liked with them. One Saturday I saw him
bring in a rolling-eyed, thick-set mare that he had
caught on the previous day, On Monday she got
tangled in a barb-wire fence and badly cut, and her
capturer, after clearing her from the wire, tethered
her with an ordinary halter, bathed the wounds and
sewed them up. If I went within 20 yards of that
brute the whites of her eyes invited me to come just
a little closer to be pulverised. The man seemed to
have hypnotic power, and I came to the decision
that he knew brumby talk. Tried to get some of
his experiences, but he shied badly at that for fear
it might have the appearance of “skite.”
* * * *
“9-3-11”: “E.J.C.” (B. 8/6/’ll) may rest
assured that the sickening of mullet when a rifle
bullet is fired amongst them is simply due to con-
cussion, just as with dynamite, but on a smaller
scale. The ordinary mullet weighs about 21b., and a
rifle bullet weighs about loz. Supposing “E.J.C.”
to be about lOst. in weight, he can get an idea of
the mullet’s feelings by entering comparatively shal-
low water, and having a 41b. 6oz. bullet lobbed into
his vicinity at rifle-shot Velocity.

THE BUSHMAN TO THE POET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
{oilin’ homeward past eleven,
Happened just to cock my eye
At your blanky “vault- of Heaven”—
Otherwise the iiamin’ sky.
Little lumps of cloud were thickly
Scattered far as I could see —-
[“And the old moon, lookin’ sickly,
Peeped out from her shawl at me”?)
No, she didn’t! Wait a minit!
Let me tell you what I saw !
(Nt>! there’s not a damned verse in it—
Stop that pen and hold your jaw I)
Just a thin fleece by the Sorter
Stretched across the field of blue 1
And the' stars like drops of water
Sparkled. You would call ’em dew I
Rollin’ homeward past eleven,
Stopped and scratched my blanky head.
Wonder if the Boss of Heaven
Ever looks in at the shed ?
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.
PATRIOTISM!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At So-and-Soapulos’s cafe at lunch time the diners
all stood up and sang “God Save the King,” the
music being supplied by the cafe band. —Thrilling
News Item.
I say! they’ve been, and gone, and done
A patriotic thing !
The hungry eaters rose as one
. And sang “Gor’save the King!”
The violinist gave the note
Upon his loudest string;
Each luncher promptly cleared his throat,
And howled “Gor’save the King!”
Some left their soup, and some their eggs,
Some left off bolting ling,
And rose upon their loyal legs,
And yelled “Gor’save the King!”
The chops grew cold ; so did the duck,
Likewise the chicken-wing;
The crowd was Coronation struck,
It had to save the King!
The ancient, honorable cheese,
Unnoticed, made a spring
For safety—while the devotees
Stood up to save the King!
The tea was far too weak to stand,
The cream too thin to cling;
The pancakes lay flat when the band
Struck up “Gor’save the King!”
These were disloyal, though, perforce;
But therein lay the sting,
For ’tis a most misguided course
That cannot save the King!
The music stopped, and all sat down;
The cash-till rang ting-ting I
It was the first time half a crown
Had ever saved the King!
N.S.W.

Cornish fell down the winze at the Hole
and Corner mine, and, when he. was fished out and
hauled to the surface, it was found that he had sus-
tained two broken wrists and various unimportant
bumps and bruises. A litter was extemporised out
of Hammond’s bunk, and Cornish was placed upon
it, and raised shoulder high. When the procession
started, the patient piped somewhat nervously:
“Here, hold hard ! Where are you takin’me?” “Home
to your wife,” replied Hammond. “Home to my
wife?” squealed Cornish. “Home to my wife —with
two broken arms? How d’yer think a man’s go in’
to defend himself?” They trained him to the Bal-
larat Hospital.
“Wang”: Albert Dorrington’s book, “Chil-
dren of the Cloven Hoof,” wherein he relates the story
of a family of cattle-
lifters in Queensland,
gets close up to fact.
Knew a spot there
myself, where, oft in
the stilly night, over-
landing cattle would
suddenly bolt, and, al-
though all hands anil
the cook wheeled after
them, several were al-
ways missing. Then, af-
ter a useless search, a
reward for their . return
would be announced,
and generally it would
be collected by a fam-
ily in the vicinity,
consisting mostly of
two girls and their
brother. One nignt,
during a stampede, a
watchful drover took
a pot-shot at an indis-
tin c t form flitting
about on horseback.
The form dropped, and
when gathered in was
found to be a sun-
burnt bush-girl who
had received more than
one reward from the
same party. She
wasn’t much hurt, but I never heard of cattle being
stampeded thereabouts again. Possibly this was due
to the fact that one of the drovers married her.

“Hecagus”: “Quidam’s” recipe (B.
8/6’ll) for scaring white cockatoos from the new
corn-patch is a good one; but I know a better. I
remember one season when; as fast as we hid the
seed, the ’toos unearthed it. At last we bought
some rum and steeped three bushels of grain in it,
and scattered the lot at the bottom of the paddock,
the ’toos’ usual haunt. In an hour’s time the whole
push was gloriously fighting, screeching drunk. We
bagged 200 odd.
“Tom Spargoe”: I can’t give the origin
of “running the cutter,” but I can say it is not
native Australian. Fifty years ago it was the
common phrase at a ship-building yard where I
toiled on the Clyde (Scotland). Was benefited by
it, also, in a Devonshire foundry, an iron-works on
the Thames and in a lodging-house in London,
Haven’t been introduced to it in M.L. yet; but
probably it was discarded by the inhabitants years
ago as obsolete. How to get beer quick is a fine
art in this country, and “rolling the barrel” into a
public park and scoffing it at a sitting is much more
rapid than “running the cutter.”

Another bush christening:—-
“Smith,” called Bill from the far
side of the fire, “did you notice—- —” ?
“Mai name,” broke in the new chum
loftily, “is Soldred-Smith. Give me
my full name and my hyphen, for
God’s sake! They’re about the only
things I’ve got left now!”
From his pocket he drew a creased
card and solemnly passed it over to
the recumbent Bill, who looked wild
enough to'eat him. Bill took the bit
of pasteboard, straightened it out,
and regarded it long and thought-
fully. The hyphen busied himself
with his swag.
“Handy thing a hyphen,” remarked
Bill, after a pause. “A cove might
hang his billy on it, or use it as a
blanky fowl-roost if ever his blanky
relatives sent him enough money to
build a home.”
“Look hyah!” cried Soldred-Smith
warningly, wheeling round.
“Or,” continued Bill, in a loud,
offensive tone, “if yer cut a notch in
the top yer could use it for a clothes-
prop, or beat the dog with it, or stick
it in th’ garden and tie rose bushes
to it, or give it to th’ wife for stirrin’
th’ clothes on washin’ days. If things
come to th’ worst and it was strong
enough t’ stand yer weight, yer could
sneak a bit o’ clothes-line and hang
yerself to it.”
“Look hyah, now!” gasped Soldred-
Smith, in helpless rage.
“I don’t go much on these hand-
cuffed names,” concluded Bill. “I
haven’t got time to call any man two
names—-even if he does harness ’em
together with a parrot perch. You
blokes heard me try to call this cove
Smith, and you heard him stop me.
I haven’t got time to call him' Solder
as well. I reckon I’ll call him
Roost.”
So Roost it was, until the hyphen-
owner got another job.
In the Jeparit district (Vic.) the foxes
have developed a new habit. For some time past
they have been killing lambs, so the cockies have
been hunting them persistently. As a result, the
foxes have taken to tree-climbing; anyway, a cocky
who riddled one on a pine tree the other day says
he had to climb 40ft. to get the brush.
* * * *
“Stickpicker”: Can any Abo. inform me
if the pine adder, blue-mouthed species, is venomous?
Fencing recently on the Lachlan, I happened to lay
my forefinger on one of the above-mentioned reptiles,
and on examining my hand I noticed a few strands of
a filmy substance, similar to a cobweb, lying on the
knuckle. Old bushmen have informed me that this
substance is ejected whenever the adder is attacked,
and, if lodged in the eye, it is certain blindness.
Anyhow, next morning my finger was puffed up and
painful, and remained in that state for close on
three weeks, despite different liniments and embro-
cations.
“The Tramp”: Talking about weather-
prophets, can anyone tell me how Black Brother
foretells rain, and especially big rains? There is
no doubt he can do it; and I’ve known a nigger
lift his nose, sniff deep, and say he smelt rain
coming, even when there wasn’t a cloud or a sign of
it in the vicinity. Generally his black nose was in
the right, which is more than I can say for the
white Noes.
* * * *
“L. 8.” : The man-bird has a long way to
go before he reaches the speed of the small thing
of feathers and hollow bones which he copies. Take,
for instance, the frigate bird, the beast with the
pendulous throat-bag, the exact purpose of which
is still a puzzle •to ornithologists. Aubudon, who
studied the habits of these birds for over a year on
the Florida Keys, asserted that they swept through
the air at much over 100 miles per hour. When
living in the Pacific, I once noticed several black
specks high in the air. They were visible for per-
haps two or three seconds, and then vanished. “What
were they?” I asked a native. “Katafa” (frigate
birds) the man replied. “They go from Nuitao to
Ulakita, where they breed.” “How far is that from
here?” “Fifty leagues.” “And how long will it
take them to get there?” “It is now two hours
from sunset, and they will be at Ulakita, at rest, on
the pandanus palms before it is dusk.”
* ■*■ **
“Philander F.” : Murray Island, in Torres
Strait, possesses an interesting work of some an-
tiquity. Some thousands of acres of shore have been
built in with rough stone walls designed as fis’.i-traps,
strong and enduring. Writer questioned the natives
some two years ago as to the origin of these walls,
but could glean nothing beyond a few misty tradi-
tions about giants of old. As a fish-trap the con-
struction answers successfully to this day.
“Drayman”: Another instance of the
quaint tricks of speech of our (mostly) departed
Black Brother. One of the chief black outlaws in
Tasmania in Governor Arthur’s time was a N.S.W.
aboriginal called Mosquito. He was imported as a
tracker, but afterwards joined the Tasmanian blacks,
becoming boss of a tribe and the leader in several
raids on stockkeepers. He was captured, tried for
murder, and duly strung up at Hobart Town, on
February 24, 1824. It is recorded in “Melville’s
Annual” (V.D.L. 1835) that while waiting for the
hangman, Mosquito had a conversation with Mr.
Bisdee, the gaoler, expressing the opinion that hang-
ing was “no good for blackfellows,” but “all right
for white pfeller: him used to it !” Six white bush-
rangers had to mount the gallows the same morning
as Mosquito.

“Tractor” : Wagga Bill was telling us
that bullocks ain’t what they was. Said William:
“I landed in Wagga-one day with 14 ton sewt. on
eight poddies. Their eyes was sticking out of their
heads like bullets —you could have knocked them off
with a stick. A mounted John rode up and says:
“Don’t you think you’ve got those poddies loaded
too heavy ?’ I never said a word —just backed the
leaders, brought the whip across the off-side poler
and dug the boot into the nearsider, and she rolled
along. ‘There,’ I says, ‘you concertina-booted, big-
paid loafer. What’s them other six for?’ Them was
the days when bullocks was bullocks.”

“Phil Harum” : How’s this for a strange
case of 'foster-parentage? A settler on the Bogan
(N.S.W. i has a cow which suckles three young pigs.
The porkers first acquired the habit of exploiting the
bull’s sister as she lay in the paddock. As they
grew larger they followed the lady round and tugged
away whilst she was feeding. The cow seemed to
enjoy the operation; the pigs certainly did.

THE LOYALIST: A TRAGEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
George Albert Edward Connaught Brown
Adored the Person, Throne and Crown.
A humble elerkling he; but oh !
He worshipped Royal functions so.
He loved to make the welkin ring
With yells of homage to his King'.
Such joys he never could resist:
He was, in brief, a Loyalist.
Arrived the Coronation week;
George Albert, usually meek,
Became intolerantly proud ;
His eye flashed fire, his voice grew loud ;
A Union Jack, like some live coal,
Blazed brightly in his button-hole.
His villa flew gay rags enough
To stock a; shop or house of “fluff.”
He forced his faithful wife to wear
A Royal Standard in her hair.
Their offspring’s clothing was, in hue,
Exclusively red, white and blue.
So hour by hour —as is the way
With these events —approached The Day.
George Brown, though hitherto opposed
To alcoholic drink, disclosed
A sudden yearning love for pubs
And bars and smoking-rooms of clubs.
Here all day long he might be seen
Toasting the Empire’s King and Queen.
Ilis clothes, no longer drab and dun,
Gleamed garish as the mid-day sun.
Spurning his billet’s sordid needs
He yielded him to loyal deeds.
The Coronation Day and Night
He found a dream of pure delight—•
A paradise of flags, songs, cheers,
Howls, speeches, lights and frequent beers.
For three ecstatic nights and days
George sang and spoke his Sovereign’s praise.
Then turned, with a regretful sob
Or moan, and sought his loathly job.
Amazing as the fact may seem,
His Boss informed him, with a gleam
Of hate in his disloyal face,
That he (the Boss) had filled his place.
George Albert Edward Connaught Brown
Fled shocked and horrified from town.
Arrived at home he found that it
Contained a person with a writ.
His household’s gallant flag-display
Had cost far more than he could pay.
A bill for clothes—red, white and blue—
Was thrust at him: ’twas overdue.
A set assault on Brown was made
By the Disloyalists of Trade.
You would have thought this worthy man
Was some distraught Republican.
The outlook, for the Browns, is bleak ;
He flies his schedule Friday week.
ENVOI.
Be Loyal if you must. But oh—
Recalling Brown’s grim fate —Go Slow.
