“Wally” Redin, for 23 years on Adelaide
advertiser, during most of which period
he ran the aiternoon paper, has thrown up
journalism in order to breed sheep at Kan-
kalilla, Darling Downs (Q.). He has the
double-barrelled reputation of never having
done anyone an ill turn and of being an
authority on poultry.
“E.E.” : If there is a gold medal or pen-
sion attached to the honor, or accident, of
being the first white horn in Victoria, please
enter John Melbourne Gilbert as a claimant.
His idea is that he was born somewhere
near the site of the present Customs House,
Melbourne, on December 19, 1835. His
father, a blacksmith, was one of the party
fitted out by Fawkner at Launceston and
sent across in the schooner Enterprise. John
Melbourne now lives at Ravine (N.S.W.).
Norman Jolly, of S. Aus., who goes to
Bananaland as Director of Forestry, was
the first Rhodes scholar from his State.
After finishing his course at Oxford, he
joined the Indian Forest Department, and
then came back to Australia and his beloved
gum-trees. He is a good all-round athlete,
and at one time gave promise of being some-
thing choice as a wicket-keeper; but he finds
more joy nowadays in looking after larger
sticks than he can find at the end of a
cricket pitch.
Died the other day, at the Melbourne
home of the Little Sisters of the Poor, Mick
Mahony, one of the best known of Cobb and
Co.’s men in the days when the coaches
were heavy with gold and the tracks
spiinkled with men who wanted some of it
badly. Mahony frequently met Ben Hall,
O’Malley and others of the profession, but
though he had to look down their gun-
barrels several times, he got through with a
whole skin. When the railways came,
Mahony was driven north into Queensland ;
and there he stayed on the box until the sun
and ague got too much for him.
Up to the time the addresses started in
the Coal Vend case towards the end of last
week, the coal and ship-owners had spent
£16,000 and the Commonwealth Government
£lO,OOO. As many as 12 piles of horse-hair
have been around the Bar table at the one
time —and mostly very expensive hair.
Australian pioneer type-founder H. J.
Thitchener cast his last font lately. He set
up a casting plant in Melbourne 32 years
ago, passed on to Sydney in 1885, estab-
lished the Australian Type Founding Co.,
and continued his hard fight against im-
ported goods until Death called in on him
at 70.
A bronze statue to a big man, Westralia’s
late Engineer-in-Chief O’Connor, designer of
the Fremantle harbor works and the gold-
fields’ water system, has been unveiled at
Fremantle. The statue is by Porcelli, of
Fremantle, but the casting was made in
Italy. It is the largest but one in Aus-
tralia.
Shipbuilder Henry Beattie, who died
lately, aged 83, was brought from Scotland
57 years ago by the co. which subsequently
developed into the A.S.N., and he laid down
the co.’s slip at Darling Island (Sydney).
Later on, with T. Ferguson, of Mort’s Dock,
he became a pioneer of the Australian iron
shipbuilding industry, and turned out:,
among other vessels, the Tinonee and Leich-
hardt —tw T o well-known old-time coastal
steamers. For 35 years his firm has been
at Balmain, ready to build, but mostly
hobbled by a fool tariff to mere repairing
jobs.
Dead at Clare, in S.A.’s “lower North,”
John Christison, who came from England
with cattle for J. H. Angas 30 years ago,
and had run affairs generally in that district.,
ever since. He presently found brewing pay
better than station-managing, and thus be-
came patron and president of many local
concerns. There was just one thing he could
not do —get into Parliament. Always bluff
and impatient of contradiction, he refused
to give smooth answers to awkward ques-
tions, and those answers always smothered
his supporters’ hopes.

The Victorian Premiers office has received
an intimation that King George has made a
rule not to accept any composition in M.S.,
neither will he accept the dedication of any
work unless the writer is known to him.
Randolph Bedford is expected to fed very
annoyed over this.

James M. Walker, who came level with
his 84th year-post at South Melbourne re-
cently, claims to have been among the first
to cross the Rocky Mountains. In 1847, or
thereabouts, he clambered over the Rockies
with several others who had left Chicago
for California, lie came on to Australia in
1852, and was in the Vic. Government service
for 28 years, mostly in command of dredging-
operations at Geelong, Belfast and the Gipps-
land Lakes.

William Strawbridge, who died at 68 last
week, had been S.A.’s Surveyor-General
since the retirement of Goyder in 1894; but
his health had been broken for several years
past, and he had been often out of harness.
Under his rule —and Goyder’s—the Land
Boards of S. Aus. have been absolutely
clean. There was no suggestion of undue
influence anywhere, and in Adelaide nobody
has ever thought of using “land agent” as a
reproachful epithet, any more than “doctor”
or “blacksmith.” Strawbridge was a Bristol
boy, but had been 60 years in Australia,
and close on 50 in the Government service.
He was chairman of pretty well everything
on the Crown Lands side of affairs, and in
the drought at the end of the century Par-
liament left it to him to reduce rents at his
discretion, the delicate adjustment involving
—it is said —27,000 leases. Actively reli-
gious, like so many South Australians,
he put his faith in the mystical Swed-
enborgian brand. E. M. Smith, his Deputy,
who has been in control of the Survey
Office for some months past, will presuma-
bly succeed.
According to a Yankee magazine, Judge
Douglas White, the new Chief Justice of
the U.S. Federal Supreme Court, and the
first Southerner in office since the Civil War,
is a big, genial, fat, athletic man who chews
tobacco while on the Bench, and off it is a
baseball barracker.
Oliphant Smeaton, whose name appears
on Cold-country book-lists as editor, novel-
ist, bookman, and ready writer generally,
was formerly known in these States and
Maoriland. Like a great many other liter-
ary men, he graduated from the clergy; he
wrote brilliant essays for the old Victorian
Review, edited papers in Goulburn and
Maoriland, and wound up
his Australian career as edi-
tor of the Argus at Rock-
hampton. In the city of
Sin, Sweat, Snakes and Sor-
row he was a tower of pen-
strength to the Central Sep-
arationists while they were
going strong in the late ’Bo’s,
when Rockhampton, writh-
ing in agony after the burst
of the Mount Morgan stock
boom, was seeking to wreak
vengeance on somebody.
Smeaton’s father, a famous
Edinburgh professor of “exe-
gesis philosophy” (whatever
that may be), died in the
early ’9o’s, and Oliphant
went back to the auld toun
to claim the inheritance and
settle down to literary work,
amid the familiar smells of
childhood.
Died, at Campbelltown (Tas.), John Wil-
liam Bergan, aged 77, a son of Peter Lalor's
sister. The father leic Ireland suddenly for
political reasons, and his wife —patriot
Peter’s sister —followed a year later. One
of the just-deceased’s daughters, Estelle, is
probably the best-known woman in the La-
bor movement in Tasmania.
South Aus. is losing three of its young
officials. Richardson. Assistant-Director of
Agriculture, goes to Victoria ; INorman Jolly
to Queensland, and Dr. Herbert Basedow to
the Commonwealth. The first graduated in
agriculture and science in Adelaide; the
second in science at Adelaide, and in arts
and forestry at Oxford ; Basedow won the
degrees of Doctor of Philosophy and Doctor
of Medicine at Breslau and Gottingen. The
salaries offered outside the State are much
better than those paid within it, and older
officers block the local way to promotion.
A. E. V. Richardson, who goes over to the
Victorian Agricultural Department, repre-
sents the highest type of the modern scien-
tific farmer. He is M.A. as well as B.Sc.,
and has held an English-literature scholar-
ship. What is more important, however, is
that he is an expert in experimental farming
and wheat-crossing, with a special gift for im-
parting to others what he has found out for
himself. At a recent congress, M‘Coll, of
Victoria, declared that this young S. Aus-
tralian’s exposition of dry farming was the
clearest and best he had heard outside
America. His new billet carries with it
£O5O a year for five years certain.

Uncle Sam’s Harvard University has
awarded the Ames prize (a bronze medal and
400dols.) to J. W. Salmond, Maoriland’s
Solicitor-General, for a treatise on the law of
torts.
A word from the Bar: —
N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges are not now the
impressive-looking lot they used to be. Martin,
Darley, Faucet, Innes, Windeyer, Owen —all these
had something of the “je ne sais guoi”—was it the
mutton-chop legal whiskers we miss now. To
begin with, our men are all so confoundedly young-
looking. The four newest—Street, Gordon, Fer-
guson and George Rich, J.J—are quite youngsters
from the forensic standpoint. Of the older genera-
tion, Equity Chief Simpson, J., though bearded, is
insignificant in stature (if it be not contempt of
Court to say so), and has to stand up to his work
in a little dock, which is graded to give the illusion
that he is sitting down. Pilcher, K.C., once caused
smiles (smiles only are allowed in the Equity juris-
diction ; laughter would make the startled and
shocked roof fall) by saying: “This is as certain as
that your Honor is sitting in that chair!” Sly has
a beard, which is just seen above the Bench. Cullen,
C.J., impresses more by his merit than by his ap-
pearance. Pring, J. (much improved as a Judge,
and now one of the best of them, though a little im-
patient at times), has a sombre and melancholy
manner, which gives him the appearance of a judi-
cial Hamlet, and his robes “but the trappings and
the suits of woe.” Cohen, J., who was the frockest-
coated of the Judiciary, is away in London ; the inim-
itable George Bowen “Simmy,” ablest of lawyers
and most pernickety of men, has retired to contem-
plate his own greatness. He was the last of the
great, impressive, vanished, mutton-chop whiskered
brigade. There is something in dressing (and shav-
ing) the part of a Judge, after all.
EVE IN GIPPSLAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The committee of the Leongatha (Vic.) Public
Library has by resolution removed Mark’s Twain s
“Eve’s Diary,” on account of the alleged indecency
of the illustrations. — Argos, 22/6/’ll.
Eve came wandering back to earth,
White and fragrant as a flower ;
Joyous in her second birth,
Drank the sunshine for an hour;
Then on swift feet made a sally,
Running through a Gippsland valley.
Henry Pumblechook, J.P.,
Looked amid the ferns, and saw
Dazzling limbs, hair flowing free,
Panting breast without a flaw,
Lips as bright as budding roses
Pumblechook said “Holy Moses!”
Not a thought of Eden ran
Through the mind of Pumblechook,
Though (like every pious man)
He had read the Holy Book.
Pumblechook without delay
Sent for Constable O’Shea.
Shrouded was her hair aflame,
Covered was her blinding glory ;
She must don the garb of shame
In a grim Reformatory ;
Happy Eve is now a goner
Judkins preached a sermon on her.

PASTORS AND POISONERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wowsers Judkins, Worrall, Woodful, Mauger and
co. are all strongly opposed to the State establishing
a monopoly of the drink traffic, just as they fight
against the licensing of the tote.
I walked once in the bush and found
A brown and bearded man
Stretched out at ease upon the ground.
Beside him was a can.
I asked what might his calling be.
“ ’Tis rabbit poisoning,” said he.
He smiled at me, and rubbed a sly,
Insinuating snout.
“Of course,” he said, “I really try
To wipe the rabbits out.
I spread the poison bit by bit- —
But bunnies seem to thrive on it.
“I love a simple job like this.
The pay is always there,
And if my doses sometimes miss,
I guess it’s only fair.
No blanky poisoner’s goin’ to quell
A plague on which he thrives so well.”
I drifted, and to musing took.
The parsons fat and thin
As this man looks on rabbits look
On any vulgar sin.
’Tis something giving them employ-
They must attack, but not destroy.

The Brisbane Electric Tramways Invest-
ment Co.: —

This co. doesn’t own the Brisbane trams. It owns
all but 20 or so of the shares in the co. which owns
the Brisbane trams. It was this way. The Brisbane
Tramways Act was passed in 1882, and gave a co.
14 years’ street tram rights. At that time Brisbane
was satisfied with horse traction. Then the late Mal-
colm McEacharn, a man with a keen eye for a con-
cession, came along, and on behalf of a co. offered to
give Brisbane an electric service in return for 25
years’ exclusive rights. There wasn’t much difficulty
in getting that put through in those days; and there-
upon McEacharn, whose co. hadn’t really any money,
cleared off and floated this mostly-English co. to
finance his co. But McEacharn knew that there are
always two or three or more lots of people to appeal
to —the man or the widow who wants safety above
all things, then those who are prepared to take a
slightly bigger risk for the sake of a slightly bigger
return, and finally those who are neck or nothing.
McEacharn, therefore, issued in all £450,000 of 44
p.c. debentures for the benefit of Class 1; then
75,000 in £5 5 p.c. cumulative pref. shares for
Class 2 ; then ordinaries for Class 3. When the co.
got to work, things were bad in Queensland, and
Brisbane remained stagnant for some years. The or-
dinaries naturally had a wretched time, and their
cake looked so much like dough that as late as
1906 the £5 shares were only worth 17s. 6d. Then
manager Badger began to smile. The debentures
and prefs. take the same amount of profit whether
things are good or bad; consequently when things
are bad they are likely to be very bad for the people
who practically hold a third mortgage, but when
they are good they are likely to be very good; for
then the ordinaries find that they hold more than a
third mortgage—they are entitled to all the cherry
that is left.
The Tramways Co., as distinct from the Invest-
ment Co., has had the following recent history:—

Of those reserves, £75,000 is to the credit of a Re-
newal Fund. The past year’s dividends are cut up
by the Investment Co. as follows: Divs. (as shown
above), £47,656; debenture interest, £19,069; ex-
penses (the Investment Co. doesn’t, of course, pay
any of the working exes., but only its own office
charges, etc.), £9913; and the balance was avail-
able for reserves. Hitherto the Tramways Co., be-
fore declaring a dividend, has made a big appropria-
tion to the Renewal Fund; but for last year it
didn’t —it paid over practically all its revenue in
excess of working expenses, and thus gave the In-
vestment Co. a chance to build up its own formal
Reserve Fund to a respectable figure. Probably it is
thought that the accumulated profits may attract
less attention if they are cut up in this way. Any-
how, even if £lB,OOO is written off to the credit of
Renewal Reserve, the profit for last year, after pay-
ing debenture interest and pref. div., was equal to
just on 16 p.c. on the ordinary capital. And quite
possibly it isn’t necessary to write off £lB,OOO, be-
cause manager Badger may have been paying for
a good many renewals and other things out of his
Working Account.
As mentioned above, the co.’s concession runs till
1920 —25 years from 1895. The municipality may
then take the concern over—by paying for it, but
without paying anything for goodwill. As invariably
happens when a city or anything else jobs away
rights of this kind, troubles have arisen. Brisbane
is spreading, and naturally wants more tram facili-
ties. “No,” says the co., in effect; “settle down
alongside our existing lines, pack our trams, and in-
crease our revenue without adding to our expenses.”
It is no use growling at the co. for saying that, be-
cause it is precisely what the authorities should have
expected that a dividend-making concern would say.
Also the nearer the co. gets to the end of its life the
less hope will the city have of getting extensions. A
co. isn’t going to spend a lot of money, and pay in-
terest on it during the non-productive stage, when it
will only have five or six years to make good—and
in those first five or six years almost any new line
is almost certain to pay poorly. These are things
that ought to be thought of before a concession is
granted. If they were thought of, of course no con-
cession would be granted. Which is the very ex-
cellent reason why they are carefully suppressed
until it is too late.

This co. is no doubt doing much better than it did a
while ago. It is in the land-subdivision business;
and until the present boom happened along, there was
for many years gloom in that industry. Worse still,
this co. had a very large quantity of city and other
property on hand; and though part of the property
was productive, consisting of country lands, there
was precious little left after interest had been paid
on the deposits and other debts. Better things began
to arrive after 1903 ; and this and other land cos.
started to look up. The rapid increase in the popu-
lation of suburban Sydney caused a building boom ;
and, while in any case that would have pro-
duced a land-subdivision boom, the introduc-
tion of municipal taxes on unimproved land
values, followed by the Federal land tax,
made a large number of big holders very keen to
pass on their possessions to somebody else. This
meant in most cases, of course, that extra-liberal
terms have been offered to encourage purchasers; and
that has prompted a whole lot of speculation. In
this way, probably, 10 allotments have been sold for
every one house that has been built. Whether those al-
lotments are going to be paid for is another matter ; it
depends on how long the present prosperity lasts.
Evidently this co. is backing the boom to last; any-
way, instead of reducing its holding of land, it is
actually increasing it. The following comparison
shows that and other things:—
Liabilities.
Nov., May, Nov., May.
1900. 1910. 1910. 1911.

City properties stand just where they have stood for
some time past, while “other properties”-—which in-
clude the unsold parts of the Furracabad estate, near
Glen Innes —are actually moving up. Well, if the co. is
prepared to take the risk of refusing present prices,
it is pretty sanguine. Quite likely the co. paid a
good deal more for some properties than it could get
now. For many years past the published figures
relating to the value of estates in hand have been
treated as a joke;, and though the co. professes to
have a big reserve fund, the share-market persistently
says that it hasn’t even got more than about two-
thirds of its capital intact. However, if the co. is to
be killed, it has evidently made up its mind to be
killed for a sheep and not for a lamb. This paper
therefore regards it as speculative; and, though it
may no doubt come out with flying colors, it is at
least equally likely to be again knocked back by bad
seasons to a 3 p.c. proposition. Its misfortune —or ours
—-is that we don’t know what the published balance-
sheet figures are worth ; and that is a situation in which
the outsider is at a great disadvantage compared
with the insider. It is monstrous, anyhow, that a
co. which takes deposits should be permitted by a
rotten Companies law to issue a balance-sheet claim-
ing assets of a value far in excess of what it well
knows those assets to be worth. That unquestionably
was the case with the Haymarket at one time, what-
ever it may be to-day.
John Bridge and Co., Ltd., wool and
pastoral produce salesmen, etc. (Sydney).—Though
this well and very favorably known co. is 14 years
old, it had until recently been a private concern. As
at March, 1910, the paid capital was in 22,000 6 p.c.
cumulative pref. shares and 33,007 ordinary shares,
all of £l, fully paid. During the next few months
15,333 ordinary shares were issued ; then, on decid-
ing to extend the business in Queensland, 29,660
more ordinaries were issued at a premium of £l.
Thus the paid capital was brought up to £lOO,OOO,
the full amount authorised. Instead, however, of
transferring the £29,660 of premium capital direct
to a Reserve Fund, which would have been the best
course, the amount was taken into Profit and Loss;
but the proper thing has now been done by transfer-
ring a level £30,000 to a Reserve Fund. The past
year’s history is shown hereunder: —
Dividends.

1910.. £192
Premium on new shares .. £29,660 29,852
1911.. £18,885 £1320 20 p.c. =£15,600 1,965 31,817
Revenue has been charged with bonus to staff
( £400) and depreciation (unspecified). The allow-
ance for depreciation is important, because freeholds
and leaseholds stand in the accounts at no less than
£111,333; and as leaseholds in particular are a
wasting security, it is imperative that they should
be written down adequately. Plant, furniture, sun-
dry stocks, etc., represent £12,516; and these also
may be subject to heavy depreciation. Accounts
which don’t specify the amount of depreciation in
such circumstances leave a lot to the imagination.
Further, the co. has just “purchased a valuable
block of land at Bulimba, Brisbane, and a contract
was let on Jan. 9, 1911, for the erection on this land
of what will be one of the finest wool stores in Aus-
tralia.” Australia is now either blessed or afflicted
with high prices for almost everything ; and as it is
extremely probable that more than a normal price
has been or is to be paid for both land and building,
it is very desirable that the capital value should be
promptly written down out of revenue. It is very
pleasant, no doubt, to pocket profits of 20 p.c.; but
it goes almost without saying that when a country
is so prosperous that a co. such as this can make
profits like that, money is extraordinarily plentiful
and prices extraordinarily high; and the co. which,
in view of its own big profits, launches out into
buildings, etc., must expect to pay unusually high
prices to other people too. A very substantial part
of the profits should, therefore, be set aside as a de-
preciation fund. If that is not done, it will be a
miracle if the co. is not left presently with over-
valued assets; and, with shrunken profits, it will be
then much more difficult to write the values down to
a fair thing. This applies no more to this co. than
to a hundred others. It may, of course, be that the
management of this co. has made the most abundant
provision for a shrinkage of values; on that point
nothing at all can be said, because there is absolute-
ly no information available. If such an allowance
has not been made, the additions to visible reserves
are very small indeed; out of a profit of £18,885
all but a poor little £1965 goes to the shareholders.
And when a co. with a reserve already accumulated
of only £192, apart from premium capital, pays a
20 p.c. dividend, one feels doubtful whether the
management isn’t unduly optimistic.
Apart from the freehold and leasehold properties
( £111,333), the co.’s principal asset consists of “Ad-
vances and sundry debtors, £42,547.” Its own cap-
ital and reserve being insufficient to finance those
customers who need financing, the co. itself has bor-
rowed £35,000 on mortgage. Practically without
exception these pastoral-produce cos. are shying off
advances; but if they want to keep their commission
business, the shying-off may be a more troublesome
matter by-and-bye. And then the co. may be glad of
bigger reserves—or more capital; and there won’t
be any 18 or 20 p.c. profit to be made on that capi-
tal. Further, it has to be remembered that landed
properties have been changing hands in unusual
numbers lately; and there have been fine and un-
usual profits in that, too, for cos. of this description.

“On the 17th May the special case stated by the
Arbitrators was heard before the Privy Council,
when judgment was reserved; but the directors ex-
pect to hear the result at an early date.” That is
the most important part of the present report and
accounts; and until the Privy Council’s decision is
given, the future cf the co. will remain uncertain.
The City Council offered the co. £158,866, being
the estimated value of the tangible assets. But the
co. said that its goodwill was worth a vast sum, and
the matter went to arbitration as provided by law.
The arbitrators, after inspecting the law at long
range and close quarters, said that the Council had
the right to acquire the undertaking by paying for
the works in situ; but, on the application of the
co., they stated a special ease for the Privy Council.
If that venerable body of old gentlemen who have
left off wearing horse-hair as a regular thing say
that the arbitrators are right—though there is never
any telling what the P.C. will say —-the shareholders
will go out and take just about 30s. per share with
them. That is to say, they will take out 20s. per
share of capital ( £125,000) plus the reserves, which
are equal to about half the capital. Of course,
the plant, etc., in situ may be valued at a bit more
than the hook value; in that case the shareholders
will have a bit more to divide. There appears to be
no possibility of its being valued at less. If, how-
ever, the Privy Council says that the Council must
pay for goodwill—which means if the people must
purchase their own custom —then the shareholders
may have another £1 to divide: it all depends on
what the goodwill is valued at.
The eo. supplies both gas and electric light; in
fact, though it is called a gas co., it has nearly
twice as m"ch invested in the electric light under-
taking as in the other branch; and the electric light
revenue ( £28,585) last half-year was about double
the other. The electric light also gives the better
net profit: say, 21 or 22 p.c. in the one, as Com-
pared with 15 or 16 p.c. in the other. Altogether
it has been a most prosperous concern, and the early
shareholders have done amazingly well. At one
time, while some of the shares were only partly paid,
dividends and bonuses totted up to 50 p.c. p.a. on
these latter shares. The consumers, in other words,
have provided a great part of the capital spent on
the works; and, having provided the capital, they
have been caving 20 p.c. divs, on it —to somebody
else. It has been an extraordinarily soft snap for
the somebody else.
Mount Morgan’s recent record :

Three divs. of Is. each have been paid during the
year, and a fourth is declared, making the total dis-
tribution £200,000, or £6024 in excess of the
profit. The £6024, as well as the year’s deprecia-
tion (£24,707), has to come out of the balance
brought forward from the previous term. The Con-
tingent Fund has also been drawn upon slightly
during the year, being reduced from £54,225 to
£53,397 ; but the Insurance Fund has been increased
from £40,992 to £45,558. The profit for the year,
it will be seen, was £193,976 (that is without writ-
ing off depreciation) as against £358,192 for the
previous year. Part of this big shrinkage was due
to the fall in copper. The following shows the
average prices realised for a little while past:—
Per Ton.

1911 „ .. .. .. 58 4 7
The copper output was 6973 tons; so the shrinkage
in value meant a loss of about £13,950 to the co.
That means that the bulk of the shrinkage in profit
is attributable to other causes. For one thing,
mainly owing to difficulties—mostly unexpected
—-met in connection with the introduction of Many
Peaks flux, the co. treated considerably less ore.
Thus: —
1910. 1911.
Auriferous copper . . . . 220,189 173,209
Silicious sulphide .. .. 124,122 109,371
Oxidised 52,917 Nil
On the other hand, the co. fed into the furnaces dur-
ing the past year 52,289 tons of Many Peaks metal-
bearing flux ; and the use of that fluxing ore—which
has to be purchased—only began during the past
year. The fluxing ore brought up the co mer output
pretty well—there is less than 100 ton o difference
between the two years—-but the gold output shrank
from 178,8670 z. in 1910 to 142,4490 z. during the
past year; and that is where the big slump in profits
happened. For, unfortunately, last year it cost
rather more to handle the 642,491 tons of ore (in-
cluding Many Peaks flux) than it did the 754,126
tons in 1910. Costs, however, for the past year can-
not be regarded as normal.
As to this matter of costs, etc., the directors might
be a lot more convincing than they are. “The altera-
tions-and extensions of the copper reduction works,
including the erection of one additional furnace, are
now almost completed,” they say. “The delay in
their completion has chiefly been due to difficulty in
the execution of orders for the necessary new ma-
chinery, and in obtaining sufficient labor of a suitable
kind for its installation.” Probably plant has been
delayed; but the directors might say with equal
truth and more force that the job involved in the
change-over to Many Peaks flux was too big for the
staff, which hadn’t realised the difficulties. Theoreti-
cally they may have worked everything out beauti-
fully ; but they seem to have lacked the experience
necessary to promptly overcome troubles when they
arose. This is not surprising when it is remem-
bered that from being a gold proposition, Mount
Morgan suddenly changed over to a copper proposi-
tion, and a fairly difficult one at that.
Reference was made above to a Contingent Fund,
which stands at £53,397. This reserve was pru-
dently created during the copper boom of three or
four years ago, and consisted of part of the sur-
pluses derived from the sale of .copper on a rising
market. It is preserved to protect the co. in case it
should overdraw on a falling market, and thereby
take credit for more than is actually realised on
final adjustments. As for the unsold copper remain-
ing on hand at the end of the year, the conservative
practice is to value it at only £5O per ton. The
blister (copper containing gold) on hand on May 31,
1910, was thus valued at £786,559; on May 31,
1911, at £719,979. The co. has thus had the ad-
vantage, apparently, of selling a little more copper
than it produced; and, though that doesn’t mean
much, it has helped the past year’s profits a little.
For instance, if the co. had 1000 tons on hand in
1910, and 900 tons on hand in 1911, and if the aver-
age price realised in 1911 was £5B, it would mean
that the 1911 accounts got the benefit of 100 tons
of copper at £B, being the difference between the
value at which it was taken credit for and the price
actually realised.
The financial position is strong:-
1910. 1911.

Liabilities .. .. .. 55,492 Nil
Surplus £389,496 £370,504
So the co. has paid the year’s dividends and made
and paid for additions to plant, and to do it has had
to draw on its accumulated liquid resources to the
extent of £18,992.
* * * *
The Tolwong (N.S.W.) people want an-
other chance to prove that they were not utterly and
hopelessly wrong. According to a circular issued by
the directors, dated May 11, their liabilities are:
Bank of N. S. Wales, £3565; advances by share-
holders, £5689; sundry creditors, £2614; total,
£11,868. The B.N.S.W. holds a mortgage over
everything, “and has consented to allow the present
indebtedness to be paid off by appropriation of half
the profits earned”—which is about ali it could do to
get out of an uncomfortable hole. The proposal is
to issue 10,000 pref. shares, the shareholders who
have made advances to have the right to a share
(paid to 165.) for every £1 advanced. Their present
position is, of course, worse even than the bank’s.
To get the money, it is proposed that the pref. issue
shall be entitled to a cumulative 15 p.c. p.a., and
to . then come in for any additional profits a W
with the ordinaries, pref. and ordinary share takinf
each the same-sized bite. The manager is hoDeftd
enough to estimate a profit of £126 per week. 1
This paper doesn’t say that no profits will be
made out of the Tolwong; but it does say that some!
body or other ought to go to gaol over some of the
boom statements that have hitherto been made about
the mine, and the ridiculous plant that was out on
on it, and the value of the few tons of stuff that
were produced. In fact, grossly misleading state-
ments were made almost up to the time J O Arm
strong dropped in his report. Instead of there beiim
vast quantities of rich ore exposed in the gorges and
only waiting to be blasted out, Armstrong found
things like this: “No. 8 workings.—Two short open-
ings, not extended far enough to prove anything ”
“No. 7.—Two shallow openings made, but nothing
of any value exposed.” “No. 6a workings.—Pull of
water, but said to show nothing but small irregular
stringers of ore.” “No. 6 workings.—Only string-
ers of ore; country very much broken up.” And so
on. Summing up, Armstrong said—“To prove anv-
thing as to quantities and values, Nos. 3, 4 and "6
should be carried another 100 ft. at the Very least
and then connected. So far as the workings have
gone, the ore seems to keep oh pinching and opening
out in the fissure, but more work will have to be
done before anyone will be able to arrive at anything
like a correct opinion as to the value of the pro-
perty.”
The most shameful part of the Tolwong business
was the way in which the pigeons were fooled as to
the value of the highly refractory or'e. There seem,
to be always a hew Crop of idiots who can be got to
believe that the more metais there are in an ore the
more valuable it must be. As a matter of fact, the
more metals there are the more costly it is, as a
rule, to get one or two of them ; the others are irre-
coverable. In this case Armstrong says—“ The mine
will have to be taken on its copper values, together
with any gold and silver present.” The alleged
great tin values are dismissed, and the fortune to be
made out of arsenic evaporates. Moreover, instead
of the ore standing in great faces awaiting charges
of fracteur, “mining costs will be heavy on account
of the flatness of the lode. Large pillars of ground
will have to be left to make the ground safe; timber
will not do, and filling would not be of much use.
The ore will take a lot of hand, .ng.” Finally, while
it was absurd to put up any plant at all until the
co. knew whether it bad any ore to treat, “I am
afraid the furnace would all go out of shape in a
very short time.” Practically, Armstrong didn’t say
anything at all that hadn’t been already said in The
Bulletin ; but he had facilities for close inspection,
so his report is valuable confirmation ; and it points
to the worst scandal in connection with N. S. Wales
mining for many a vear.
The prospects are very poor. In ordinary circum-
stances the thing wouldn’t be worth touching. If no
money at all had been spent on it, it wouldn’t be
worth risking any money on it. But, since a good
deal has been spent on it, it may be worth while
risking a few pounds more, purely on the back-up-or-
forfeit principle.

It won’t surprise anybody who kr ows the
man, and who also knows how the Commonwealth
Oil Corporation is being run, to learn that George
Georgeson has resigned. After much experience
with Scotch shale and oil, he was sent out to take
charge of the business side of the bloated undertak-
ing, and latterly has been acting manager. When
Georgeson has left the co.’s service and can speak, it
will probably be found that the shareholders and de-
benture-holders haven’t been told things that he thinks
they ought to know.
A tin show at Germanton (N.S.W.) has
been quietly floated (24,000 shares at £1) by a non-
mining crowd. They have 100 acres to work at, and
the apparently hopeful spec is named The Placer Tin
Mining Co. J. V. Wood, of Melbourne, is legal
manager ; and the man at the infant mine is Rudolph
Wachsmith, a German who knows the business. A
dredge is now on the ground, and a first clean-up is
promised for August. Thus far, the lowest prospect
taken averages 61b. to the yard ; and if the machine
tells anything like that tale, there are sweet divs.
ahead.
“Gwy”: The management' of the Zinc
Corporation gives shareholders only the most meagre
information concerning the details of the union with
the B. H. South Blocks. The latest development is
not, a mere amalgamation, but a proposal to wind up
the Zinc Corporation, and form a new co. in 245,69''!
pref. shares of £l, and 658,616 ordinary shares of 10s.,
such ordinary shares to have the same rights as the
others for purposes of dividends. Of these shares the
South Blocks people are to receive 66,666 preferential
and 200,000 ordinaries, which leaves the Zinc holders
precisely the same number as before. Nor does me
writing down from 16s. to 10s. affect the existing Zinc
ordinaries, as on a winding up they are to be treated
as if paid to £l, which restores them to their orig-
inal status. But, in view of the words italicised, it
may be queried whether the holders of Zinc prefs.
who are entitled to a fixed 20 p.c., and thereafter to
share pro rata, will take the new arrangement lying
down. These were drastic terms, but when they put
their money up the Corporation was, or was supposed
to be, in desperate straits; and they put it up on a
definite understanding. On the other hand, if it
should turn out that they still retain these rights, the
holders of ordinarv Zincs, who have been hanging on
patiently for years, will presently be heard asking
why the South Blocks crowd should come in on the
ground floor, and acquire preferential rights over
them. Perhaps the Melbourne office will do a little
explaining.
* * * *
When Govett, chairman of the Lake View,
told his shareholders in London that the amalgama-
tion of the Broken Hill South Blocks and the Zinc
Corporation would be a good thing for the South
Blocks, he spoke the very solid truth. In fact, the
amalgamation is going to save the Blocks from a
very violent slump. In spite of all the gaudy fore-
casts, the mine was proving itself hopeless at present
prices. Repeated attempts to give the stock a bump
up failed pretty badly. Then came a dividend which
should never have been paid.. But still the market
didn’t respond. As a matter of fact, the figures were
too plain—the co. was making no real headway worth
mentioning. The last card was the amalgamation;
and, if it comes off —and there can be scarcely any
doubt about it —Lake View can afford to shout itself
a drink over a lucky escape.

Shares in B.H. British have just doubled
their value in nine months, mainly owing to fairly
satisfactory ore-developments. But the management
has still to prove itself; it is the weakest feature of
a situation that, taken all round, isn’t nearly as
strong as the share market.
Queen Bee (N.S.W.) is crosscutting for
the southern or subsidiary ore-body that gave some
very patchy prospects at 570 ft. A winze is down a
few feet from that level, and the crosscut is ex-
pected to strike the formation in a few days. No-
thing is said about developing the main ore shoot,
which pitches rapidly north of the shaft, and should,
on the past record of the mine, be well worth pros-
pecting. The ore body south of the shaft is prac-
tically virgin, but its value is decidedly speculative.
A very promising property at Wyalong
(N.S.W.) is the Erin’s Isle, which has just handed
out £4 per share as the result of development by
tributors for the past 12 months, at the 330 ft. level.
During this time crushings, aggregating 190 tons,
averaged nearly 20oz. gold to the ton, raised from a
reef 9in. to 12in. wide. The tribute expires a few
weeks hence, and the owners will then work the pro-
perty on wages, and on a more extensive scale.

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Wilfley.” It is continuous, anil
consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in a tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported on
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior to allow the liquid to be
withdrawn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the drum the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
means of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this filter.* [2

Ironbark, Bendigo, put up a decent yield
and scrip trebled in value, mainly under the in-
fluence of the Beehive. The co., after reaching out
to the Sheepshead line at 480 ft., hit a rich patch ot
stone. Quartz was picked up between two runs ot
lava, and, as it is widening out, the problem wlncn
had to be solved was whether this meant the top ot
a reef. There were those who foretold a lodwt.
yield ; but 16 tons from the winze topped 620 z. lhis
total is small potatoes to make a big market on; but
there has been so little for Bendigo drummers to
beat on since Central Blues have settled down,
that some enthusiasm is excusable. A lot more work
is wanted to prove that the shoot of stone has any
length, and until enterprise that way gives the
measure, the worth of the find is a matter for pure
conjecture; and the man in the street can conjecture
just as well as the broker or dealer in the Beehive.
Nevertheless, good has come out of the racket, inas-
much as the sleepy crowds in the Victoria Consols
and Hercules and Energetic are getting ready to re-
sume active work on the slabs of ground they possess
on the Sheepshead line.
* * * *
Ajax, Daylesford (Vic.), has shown its
heels once again. Puffs were spread on the market,
because in No. 8 level some nice specimens could be
got out of the face. While the country rock was as
tight as a door-knocker, the lode was more or less
formation; though the word passed round was that
the formation was the most consistent seen for two
years. Still, this wasn’t much in the way of paint-
ing the lily, because Ajax for two years has been
within measurable distance of the end, failing an
addition to reserves. What the market won’t grasp
about Daylesford is that the flat markets are the
jewel pots, not the vertical lodes. Until a decent
flat make figures in the Ajax armory, its position
remains that of a wasting property.

Kalgoorlie Chamber of Mines is backing
up the Victorian institution of that name in its re-
quest to the Westralian State Government to inves-
tigate the circumstances connected with the acquisi-
tion and subsequent flotation of the Chaffinch. It is
pointed out to the Government that the police court
proceedings against Bonwick, Dyason and Greenway
were inconclusive, and, in the “interests of mining
morality,” the appointment of a Commission is
urged. As a set-off to the statement that Magistrate
Roe affirmed that the mine was not salted, it is worth
mentioning that Roe did so without hearing the de-
fence, which relied mainly on evidence of salting.
* * <■ *
Another glaring case of everything being
said to be all right when it is all wrong. The
other day an official circular was issued in London
stating that there was nothing to justify the* drop in
Oroya Links. Nonsense! There are several reasons
for the fall, and it is hard to believe that the direc-
tors aren’t acquainted with some, if not the lot. The
turning down of the Gimlet option at Ora Banda is
one; the derelict state of the co.’s holdings other
than the Eclipse lease is another; the poor quality
of the ore at present exposed in the Eclipse property
itself is still another ; and the inability of the man-
agement to make ends meet, through a variety of
circumstances over which it has no control, is still
another. Small profits may be earned when the
Eclipse ore is opened up at greater depth, but the
outlook for dividends isn’t too hopeful. Fortu-
nately the co. is well off financially, its liquid re-
sources now amounting to £55,000 —equal to nearly
Is. per issued share.
* * * *
At the end of 1910 Chaffers (W.A.) had
cash resources amounting to £22,859, of which
£13,000 was invested in Australian Government se-
curities. The year’s bullion and sundry receipts
totalled £73,165 ; and, after allowing for everything,
a credit balance of £3136 remained. Nowadays the
co. manages to make a little money by treating ore
from its own property and the adjoining Boulder
mine, where stone of a little over 30s. grade is being
stoped out of Lane’s workings, the gold recovered
being divided on a basis satisfactory to both par-
ties. But the present system of working leaves no-
thing for shareholders, and, as a last resource, the
management has asked the London directors to sanc-
tion the expenditure of £3OOO in special develop-
ment on the off-chance that the ore reserves will be
more than doubled. This might enable the co., by
treating a bigger tonnage, to pay dividends —for a
time.
* * -X- *
Discussing the co.’s possessions at the
Lake View meeting in London, Chairman F. A.
Goveft said that, as the chief asset was a five-eighths
interest in the Lake View-Star, it was comforting to
know that the mines worked by this concern were
now yielding a surplus at the rate of £30,000 a year,
with the probability of that amount being increased
to £40,000 as time rolled on. As regards the Zinc
Corporation-South Blocks amalgamation, he thought
the value of Lake View’s holding in the latter co.
would be enhanced by the combine. About the
Burma mines he was a bit dubious; the oil interests
were “about the same” ; and a host of sundries, in-
cluding the Amalgamated Zinc, which showed a profit
of £lO,OOO, were “doing well.”
Official reasons given for the Lake View
reorganisation, which was agreed to by shareholders
in London on May 22: “That now the mine has been
amalgamated with other properties in another cor-
poration in which the co. holds a controlling in-
terest, its basis as a mining concern will have tech-
nically disappeared as soon as the work of re-
treating the remaining residue dumps is finished;
that the directors can only continue the other busi-
ness of the co. at their own risk, and that it, there-
fore, is necessary to reconstruct the co. on proper
lines in order to remove this difficulty; that this
can only be done by the formation of a new co. and
transfer of the assets to such new co.” The value
of the assets, which largely consist of shares, is esti-
mated at £350,000.
Associated Northern (W.A.) now derives
a revenue of about £6OO a month from public crush-
ing, and the amount is increasing. It also rakes in
£250 a month, or thereabouts, from the treatment of
its own ore; but some people who ought to know
what Uiey are talking about say that the co. could
do much better than this if it let tributors work the
whole mine. Seeing that reserves have now almost
petered out, it is probable that this will be done be-
fore very long.
Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) got a raking
in the Kalgoorlie police court the other day for em-
ploying three men underground who couldn’t speak
English. The management admitted that two of the
men hadn’t any knowledge of the language, but
reckoned the other foreigner knew a little. The Court
found that neither knew any of Shakspeare’s tongue,
and, after emphasising the danger of employing
such men, imposed a fine of £lO and costs in each
case. There are any number of “Jack Smiths” and
“Bill Browns” working underground in the West
who hail from Dagoland, and who don’t know a word
of English. In fact, some of the out-back mines are
occasionally full of them.
* * * *
Several Meekatharra shows have been
priced during the past month by agents of Melbourne
and Sydney capital; though, as leaseholders want too
much, no business has so far resulted. Meantime
a lot of good stone is being sent by claimholders
to the Fingall mine for treatment; but until suffi-
cient inducement is held out to capital and its pals
to come along, the lower-grade stone, of which there
are considerable quantities, must remain neglected,
as the present cost of transit and treatment is pro-
hibitive in the case of stone below jjoz. value.
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : “R. 8.” and his fol-
lowing (if any) are entreated to come to
the rescue of Bullfinch. Having found the
railway from Southern Cross to be a dead
loss, the precipitate Government has effected
drastic alterations in the service. Now a train con-
sisting of an engine and a combined carriage and
van runs twice a week, and stays an hour at Bull-
finch. Other indignities lately heaped on this fraud-
ulent centre include the wholesale abandonment of
leases and the withdrawal of salaried correspondents
by the dailies. The wooded landscape is dotted with
mullocky tombstones on the graves of local and
foreign capital, and a healthy rivalry exists between
the local undertaker and the local auctioneer in the
despatch to the outside newspapers of the news of
what is going on —which isn’t much.
“R. 8.”: “Oofbird” wants to know why
I wrote something he alleges I did in B. 12/l/’ll,
when I knew “some time after the middle of De-
cember” that the Bull Ant wasn’t a mine. There
are such things as time for mail carriage and delay
of publication; but even if there weren’t, was I to
imitate “Spoofbird’s” marv.ellous wisdom after the
event, and damn a thing utterly because Grut’s
values hadn’t persisted? The point to be settled as
to whether I am “a fool or the other thing” is al-
ready settled. My friends know that I am neither,
and the others don’t matter. But there’s no point
to be settled about “Spoofbird.” Judging him by
his own insinuations, the kind of fowl he is is a
lying cuckoo. Meantime, Mr. Grut’s sampling still
sticks out through the whitewash.
* * * *
Fremantle Trading Go. (W.A.) has fast-
ened on to an old lead mine at Northampton as a
means of earning its living. A crushing and dress-
ing plant has been erected, and, by-and-bye, when a
promised railway extension comes along, the leady
concentrates are to be trucked and sent to smelting
works by the seaside. Like its insolvent ancestors,
this co. hasn’t done very much to justify its exist-
ence, and the announcement that it has got hold of
over 1000 acres of freehold mining property, with
several lead mines included, may help to cheer share-
holders to stand by it if any monetary trouble
should arise. It has other irons in the fire, too.
Some time ago the co. bought a sandy heap at Point
Moore, near Geraldton; and as the Government has
selected a site for a new railway station in the
vicinity, the co. has decided to divide this area into
building allotments, and try to get rid of them.
* * * *
Queensland maintains the gross value of
its mineral output. The total last year was
£3,710,222. That is better than the output recorded
10 years since; but profits are nothing like what
they were. The three largest producers—Mount Mor-
gan, Gympie, and Charters Towers —have all suffered
in that way. Practically one-half (£1,874,555) of
the Queensland mineral output is now obtained from
auriferous copper deposits, and these are always
costly to treat. At the moment the State is
struggling with difficult metallurgical problems, ac-
centuated by high costs and inadequate transport
facilities. The change over from gold-bearing
quartz veins, worked at shallow levels, to refractory
mineral, cannot be described as easy.
Great Fitzroy (Q.) is still in a bad way,
mainly because the assays of crude stuff and the re-
covery by flotation are below expectations. A loss
of practically half the gold and 30 p.c. of the copper
made the low-grade silicious stuff (assaying in bulk
slightly over 2 p.c. copper and 2dwt. gold) unprofit-
able ; so milling had to be suspended. The blast
furnace has been smelting ore assaying over 3 p.c.
copper, and on this grade, with a few shillings’ worth
of gold and silver to help, it is probable that a profit
can be made. Getting an improved furnace ex-
traction even at the expense of picking, appears the
only hope unless copper takes a rise. As a matter
of fact, some of the big American mines pay divi-
dends on li p.c. ore; but Fitzroy costs are heavy.
The Chillagoe Co. is erecting a small
sampling mill on the Etheridge. The idea is to
sample all ore, weigh, and pay the owner on the
spot, before the stuff goes to the smelters. The
small owner, to whom prompt pay means much, is
expected to be thankful in a practical way.
* * * *
The district in the vicinity of Hampden,
Elliott, etc., on the outskirts of Cloncurry, is going
a head —with sheep. Devencourt, which was practic-
ally idle for years, is being fenced in, and water is
being got all over the place; it looks as if it will
shortly become one of the big sheep runs of the
North. Other stations are going to belt in on the
same lines. , „
Mount Oxide (Q.) was under offer to an
English crowd at £lOO,OOO and a tenth paid-up
share interest, but was turned down. The crowd
that secures Oxide will have to spend a fortune to
get material in and products out, and it takes a
good mine to return £lOO,OOO, plus the £200,000
which would be required to place it m a paying
condition. Mount Oxide is a wonderfully rich forma-
tion ; but an outlay of at least a quarter of a mil-
lion makes even J. Bull think hard.
Mount Mulligan coalfield, which forms
part of the old Hodgkinson goldfield (N.Q.), is at-
tracting a good deal of attention, especially from the
Chillagoe people. Three Chillagoe directors, includ-
ing Chairman Reid, went the other day to have a
look round; and a coal expert has told them that the
coal may be expected to spread itself over 30 square
miles of country. Two seams exist, and the country
is not faulted. Though the mineral so far unearthed
is not of the highest quality, it is said to be suitable
for coke-making and steam-raising. A trial survey
of a 30-mile line is being made; this line would
junction with the Chillagoe Co.’s metals at Dim-
bulah Station. Material for making cement exists
on the Chillagoe line,' and cheap coal may launch a
new industry in those parts. But apart from that side-
line, cheap coal, anti especially coke for the smelters,
would be a big thing for that section of North
Queensland. At present a ton of smelting coke runs
to over 60s.
The Bluff Colliery people, in Central
Queensland, are making a fresh start with bores on
their Mammoth leases. A 100-ton sample was ordered
from the Mammoth shaft for the Admiralty. Part
of it gave good results, and then floods came, and the
rest of the parcel never left the mine. Now a bore
site above flood-level has been selected. The Bluff
might be a happier concern; its rival, the Blair
Athol, goes along much more smoothly, with an in-
creasing output.
* * * *
vVolfram Camp (N.Q.) is feeling better,
having hopes that two Frenchmen, representing the
Society of Rare Metals, will erect a plant to treat
low-grade material containing bismuth, wolfram and
molybdenite. The outfit they talk about will cost
£20,000, and be capable of treating eight tons per
hour.
Chillagoe ran its copper furnace 16 days
and its lead furnace eight days last month ; 2455
tons ore were smelted for 171 tons copper, 108 tons
lead, 16,8410 z. silver and 2280 z. gold. The extrac-
tion averaged between £4 and £5 per ton of ore;
but costs, with such a limited tonnage, are neces-
sarily heavy. Most of the ore is coming from the
Einasleigh, but the co.’s own mine at Zillmanton,
when it dries itself, is a likely producer. The flow
of water has been exceptionally * heavy, because the
Zillmanton is on the contact with the limestone. A
traction-engine is hauling some high-grade ore from
the Klondyke district, a few miles from Mungana.
* * * *
No. 3 North Columbia, Gympie, has
shelled out its second dividend —4d. a share. The
mine seems likely to turn out large quantities of
medium-grade crushing stuff from levels at 1656 ft.
vertical, driven 262 ft. north and 116 ft. south. No.
3 has a mill of its own, so costs are kept down.
Values are uncertain, depending on patchy makes of
specimen stone.
* * * *
No. 2 S. Great Eastern, Gympie, has sunk
its shaft to 1926 ft., so it is getting down to a depth
that corresponds with the Scottish Gympie work-
ings. The southern stopes on the Power reef con-
tinue to supply payable crushing stuff, but of lower
quality.
* * * *
Mount Lyell, for the first 32 weeks of the
current financial year, treated 79,693 tons ore for
2061 tons copper, 129,4920 z. silver and 22240 z. of
gold. The average is 2.6 p.c. copper, which would
give a fair profit irrespective of precious metals;
and the latter add from £lO to £ll to the value of
blister. At the North Lyell a large tonnage of
high-grade ore is being won at the 1100 ft. level;
but ventilation has been defective, and will not im-
prove until the rise holes through to the big stope
that has been operated at the 1000 ft. level. There
is an intermediate level at 1050 ft., originally put
in because of the extreme difficulty of tracing the
downward continuation of the ore body. Mount
Lyell’s development in its deepest level on both
sides of the crosscut is proof enough that the deeps
below 1200 ft. hold many secrets. Hugging the con-
glomerate, the schistose cupriferous ore body may
go down for thousands of feet.
* * * *
North Mount Farrell, one of the little
things which peg away in the remoteness of the Tas-
manian West Coast, hasn’t pegged with much suc-
cess lately. The half-year showed a working profit
of £664 on net receipts of £10,830, but that trifle
was more than wiped out on account of deprecia-
tion. There is a year and a-half’s ore in sight, and
with a new level opened up —the work is well on
the way—that quantity should be considerably added
to. But the co. owes close up to £5OOO in excess of
liquid assets; and with a mine of this size, and lead
and silver markets that are rather weak and not
likely to improve much yet a while, £5OOO is an
uncomfortable dead horse to have on the premises.
* * * *
New Ruby Flat Tin, Branxholme (Tas.),
still flourishes under the watchful eyes of Alf. Hughes
and Andrew Stenhouse. Wet weather spells good
profits ; dry weather no work and few drinks. Three
big faces are worked, and as the summer was moist
enough for an ordinary winter, the co., instead of
going on to the shelf for the time being, cleaned up
a little lot of tin which put it on to the div. list
more promptly than usual. Over 42,000 cubic yards
of drift last half-year averaged lRb. oxide, and with
the new face widening and showing good wash, and
with tin high, no loose scrip flutters in the market
place. The co., besides alluvial, has a big decom-
posed formation, said to be speckled with tin; but
lode tin is as treacherous as a Jap, so the co. had
better regard that formation as a hobby rather than
as a source of income.
* * * *
The Hercules (Tas.) directors have got
authority to amalgamate with the German smelting
people who own the works at Zeehan ; or, rather, to
sell to or otherwise traffic with those people. As
explained recently, the German smelters’ idea was
to get a leg in for the complicated Oker process;
but, though that plan may be adopted so far as the
zincy slags are concerned, the metallurgical advisers
of the Hercules are not likely to agree to the adop-
tion of that process exclusively. _ Sooner or later a
dressing process will have to be installed.
W.P.F.: That’s the position. Munroe
and Munroe failed; and the man who pushed off
Marconi (Canadian) shares on their account in Aus-
tralia couldn’t deliver. A few shares did come to
hand through the Union Bank ; and if anybody here
now knows anything about the business, that bank
does. .. . Komata: You don’t barrack for the non-
amalgamation of the two N.S.W. Savings Banks in
this paper. As to the ridiculous assertion that the
Government wants amalgamation in order to collar
the poor little reserve fund of the Barrack-street
Bank, how much is that reserve worth compared
with the guarantee of the whole State? And that is
the guarantee that the depositors will have if they
come under the State’s roof; and they can have no
more claim to it if they don’t come under that roof
than the depositors with the just-gone-bung Birkbeck
Bank had on the British Government. Further, the
varn re collaring the wretched little reserve fund is
plain rubbish. The Government may have the use
of that money; but it will have to pay for it, just
as it pays for any other loan. There is, however,
no undertaking whatever that the Government will
have even the use of the money; it certainly won t
if the funds can be invested to better advantage.
“Gouger” (from Lightning Ridge, N.S.W.) :
Opal-gouging isn’t a bad game —if you get on to
opal. One mate of mine has just come back from
a trip round the world, thanks to luck and a good
claim. He to'ok a parcel with him, and made his
expenses out of the sale. Another mate did the
same thing, going through Canada, and visiting New
York, London, and Paris, etc. Both are “buying
now, and gougers pray for their success. It might
help to stir up other buyers, some of whom wait
until a fellow is hard up before they will touch his
stuff Gouging is an uncertain business. The old
hand, who thinks he knows all about indications,
may belt away without seeing a color for months;
but let a pair of brand-new emigrants or a couple
of foreign sailors wander this way* and within a day
or two the word is liable to go round that “the
chums” are “on it.” The beggars seem only to
have to climb down some deserted shaft, and they
can scratch out a stone or two straight away. A
couple of Welshmen have just done this old trick,
the twelfth pair to my knowledge. Well, good luck
to them, anyway. #

TEA
Being a waitress in an hotel
isn’t bad work the different
people that come and go keep
you interested. Some of the
customers you like, and look
forward to their coming again—
but others, well, you simply hate
to see them come near the
dining-room. The only part that
isn’t too nice is when some of
the gentlemen take a drop too
much, but in the morning when
they come begging for a cup
of tea you can’t help pitying
the poor wretches, and how they
do swallow it, to be sure—just as
if their throats were “brick dry.”
Talking about tea, I’ve had
experience with a good many
kinds, but for giving general
satisfaction it’s my opinion
there’s none to beat Robur —
I’ve often been asked “what tea’s
this!” —and when I’ve told them
“Robur No. 1 Grade,” they’ve
looked surprised, as if they ex-
pected me to say it was some
very costly sort of tea —perhaps
the careful way 1 make it has
something to do with its tasting
extra nice—you see 1 don’t wet
the tea till the water is boiling,
and then 1 take care it doesn’t
stand too long. For your home
I should say you’d find the No. 1
Grade a very satisfactory tea
to use, and a little extra care
in the making will more than
repay you.
The Waitress.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, July 3, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —-
The Zealandia, bound for Vancouver, had
a full passenger list, which included various
persons in the public eye. Amongst them
were Lady Dudley (William Humble Ward
travels by another route) ; her old friend and
relative, Adeline of Bedford ; and Private Sec.
Walter Callan, who injected a brisk journal-
istic snap into his official letters, and was
credited with putting the point on her Ex.’s
public remarks. Katherine Grey, the Rule
Taylor triplet, Mr. and Mrs. Gerald Westma-
cott, and Mr. F. de Vere Lamb were also in
the exodus. Others in flight were the Rip-
ley trippers to this side. Sir Henry Ripley,
Baronet, is a landowner in two English coun-
ties, and varied this arduous career by tak-
ing a hand in the S’African squabble. His
usual recreations are hunting, shooting, fish-
ing and golf, though quoits and bridge are
liable to be added when he is threatened with
brain fag.
Tbe polo pony in a foam of latber, with
an ugly red stain trickling down one side,
was the gamest feature of last week’s polo
carnival. On Saturday afternoon her Ex.,
in a dark gown, with the ermine Coat of
Beauty, handed over the Countess of Dudley
Cup to the Muswellbrook winning team.
Fastened on to the muff that went with her
Ex.’s white, snow-drift fur wrap, was a
parti-colored rosette with a flare of red in
its ribbons. This was Lady Dudley’s last
public appearance here, and the (more or
less) Elect are now praying hard for the
coming of the Denman Excellencies, and a
more expensive wine-list at Government
House.
Lieut.-Gov. Cullen’s wife opened the Syd-
ney Club of Domestic Arts the other day in
the King’s Hall. The Victorian Ladies’
Work Association, or some similar vehicle
for the removal of the home-made stitch to
the back of the buyer, has been loudly ac-
claimed as an example for the new Sydney
sprint; yet it sticks in my mind, like hot
sealing-wax or wet putty, that the L.W.A.
consistently lost money for some years. Mr.
Macarthur-Onslow, M.L.A., who is a Lieut. -
Colonel of some frill in a volunteer corps,
handed over a few halting words of approval
—after much hunting for them in his
pockets and other likely places—to the jam
and sewing corroboree. The warrior is a
kindly soul, who can generally be depended
on by woman to lead a forlorn bope for her.
The Admirable King-Hall, who accused his
little daughter of being responsible for his
presence, also fired some tokens of his ap-
preciation.
Historic old Duntroon homestead has just
had its first official drink as a military col-
lege for Australian boys. Blinks, wbo has
an inquiring mind, asks : “Will Colonel Fos-
ter, . R.E., who emerged from the retired
British half-pay list to teach modern mili-
tary science at Sydney ’Varsity, occasionally
explode at Yassberra on his pet hobby, ‘The
Siege of Jerusalem’?”
A lady globe-trotter, who is gathering cats-
paw impressions of this desirable wool and
wheat patch, has been raking the Sydney
Sun, fore and aft, with a cross-fire of rude
remarks on the Australian Woman. It is
a magnificent ad. for the book this flitter
says she’s going to give to a world which
isn t shrieking for its publication. For the
rest, it is offensive froth, apparently whipped
together in penny trams and aerated bun
shops. Abnormal busts, flat hips, thick
ankles and boorish manners are the offences
that hurt the feelings of the unknown Miss
Plough of Despond when she casts her
weeping eye on Foster Fraser’s “sprightly,
sun-kissed Australians.” In case she isn’t
a myth (for she seems uncommonly shy of
breaking cover), I’m jumping on her ad. with
the large, flat feet she mentions as an Aus-
tralian woman’s perquisite.
H. H. Share, R.N., habitually Private
Sec. to Governors, who had a first look-in
here on the staff of Admiral Beaumont, and
a final wash-up in Gov.-General Northcote’s
retinue, had a long and extensive oppor-
tunity of gazing on the Australian female
face. He had been as a lad on the Royal
yacht, and made his first official bow (of
the right gradation to a hair-line) under the
august eye of Queen Victoria. His pro-
found knowledge of what the Vale of De-
spond lady calls “Good Society” may there-
fore be accepted without further apologies.
Mr. Share’s last impromptu comment on the
Australian girl occurred at a farewell party
to the popular Northcote lady. “I’ve seen
women in most parts of the world,” said he,
“but I think Australian women come first
for beauty. It is unwise, perhaps, to draw
comparisons, but I can’t recall any other
city I’ve been in that contains so many
strikingly pretty girls as Sydney.”
Federal politics and matrimony seem to go
together like almonds and raisins. Minister
O’Malley succumbed not long ago. Senator
Findley abandoned sewing on his own shirt-
buttons —or whatever insignia of liberty the
unattached male flaunts in the face of mere
spinsters—and went cheerfully to the altar
Acting-Prime Minister Hughes wedged in a
honeymoon, punctuated by a motor skid, and
married Miss Mary Campbell, of Burren-
dong _ (N.S.W.), before Parliament House
bad time to breathe its blessing. That makes
at least three Federal Ministers who have
paid our sex the compliment of marrying a
second time. Hughes, Tudor and Thomas
■were all left young widowers; and each in
due time remarried.
Though Acting-Prime Minister Hughes
and Acting-State Premier Holman are poor
friends politically, Holman expressed his
good wishes very neatly. His first speech,
after the wedding was announced, Holman
made at a Chamber of Commerce dinner.
Hughes had been invited, but sent excusing
himself on the ground of ill-health. “I was
sorry,” said Holman afterwards, “to hear
that my old friend Hughes is ill. I trust
that the complaint that is keeping him from
us will be with him for very many happy
years.”
Prof. David’s lecture on Antarctica and
the coming Mawson expedition, was rather
frost-bitten in its attendance. Four shil-
lings for reserved seats hit the general pub-
lic in some place that didn’t answer back.
The cheapest seats were filled with a com-
fortable crowd, but otherwise the chilly
Town Hall, with ice-packs on the lantern-
screen, was full of draughts and Austrian
chairs in all their naked cane. The newly-
knighted Allen Taylor delivered a brief pre-
liminary ode to the Mawson enterprise in
general, and Prof. David in particular.
The Lieut.-Gov. Cullens, who are slogging
steadily at the social top-dressing of their
present billet, came in to Prof. David’s lec-
ture with Captain Verney on their heels.
There was a mild effusion of bare heads and
warm evening wraps in the front seats. The
person who probably excited most envy was
the Shackleton expedition’s artist, depicted
on the screen in a snug sleeping bag, with a
candle perched on his temple and a book in
his hand. The arrival of the Cullens was
indexed by the feeble notes of a hidden
piano, which made an uncertain attack—ap-
parently on “Advance Australia Fair,” or a
cough in chords —and then limped through
“Gor’ Save.”
The Habitually Late stumble with a louder
thud than usual over the Early Worm at
Her Majesty’s Theatre. The Irving “Ham-
let” revival bites the evening air rather soon
after dinner, and the loiterers get no help
from the stage, which is draped in inky night
to swallow up the Ghost’s legs. He is a
most imposing Ghost. Henry Yibart, who
plays the part, haunts the Danish con-
science with a pale-green complexion and a
voice like a tolling bell.
Denmark must have been sadly down at
the heel when Claudius was King, for his
robe of State is like an up-country patch-
work quilt after it has been chasing a cer-
tificate of merit at local shows for 20 years
or so. Frank Tyars, who is Claudius, is
obviously one of the “sound” stock actors of
the older school, who never take liberties
with Shakspeare. He makes-up like King
John before the Great Wash, with a Magna
Charta frown of deep-dipped stage villainy.
And Queen Gertrude (Miss Helen Rous)
is as shabby as her lord. There was some-
thing so rotten in the state of Denmark,
when Claudius was sitting on its best garden
seat, that Gertrude’s tire-women —who are
not looking for work —were too limp to
mangle their royal lady’s head-drape, which
floats down her back in a dejected, wrinkled
wisp. Helen Rous has a musical voice, with
an emotional thrill in its back notes, and
gets a good deal of maternal thunder out of
her arras-curtain lecture to son Hamlet.
H. B. Irving, whose raven hair matches
the historic inky cloak—with a magenta lin-
ing—has completely captured the Sydney
playgoer, in spite of his legs and his father’s
chuckle. I’ve seen the elder Irving—though
not in “Hamlet” —and the parent’s manner-
isms are copied faithfully. H. B. carries the
present production on his shoulders as easily
as a boy carries a toy cannon. He is a great
Hamlet, and the booking is prodigious. You’d
find it hard to stab the applause with a hair-
pin between the gallery boys, so closely are
they packed.
. “The Gay Grisette,” at the Criterion,
gives Florence Imeson a good excuse to
wear gauze wings and an abbreviated pink
drape; this is as Cupid in the second act.
She’s a pretty, graceful creature, and pirou-
ettes on shapely limbs, chiefly clad in micro-
scopic pink-ribbon bandages. Cupid’s classic
bow and arrows are going to the scrap-heap,
like the Pah’f’l and the ’bus-horse; so
Florence was armed with a toy gun which
doubled forward with a Grecian bend in its
waist when it aimed at the front stalls. Polo
men dropped in on the first night of the
piece, and the stalls have seldom offered a
larger acreage of susceptible white shirt-
front as targets for ballet toes. Two boxes
were full. Miss Von Arnheim, in one,
showed a graceful scarf of shimmer and chif-
fon over a good-looking white frock.
The Norland Ball broke the first chill of
a wintry night by a grand march round the
Town Hall one evening last week. Mrs.
Greig-Smith and Lieut.-Col. Jack Antill were
chiefly responsible for this walking-out con-
tingent, which ducked its head or its knee —
as most convenient —to the Mayor and
Mayoress. The gorgeous Coronation set,
arranged by Mrs. Norman, bristled with
duchesses —Miss Martyn and Miss Bentley,
for instance—and had a handsome trim-
ming of the marchioness species. Count-
esses made an elegant tail to the dazzle, and
were squired by comparatively common per-
sons like fancy-dress viscounts and barons,
with a mere duke or two thrown in for luck.
Miss Olga Roth, who belongs to this winter’s
crop of debutantes, was one of the nicest-
looking damsels at the dance.
Whites and Wills-Allens loomed large dur-
ing Polo week at Kensington. The flower-
edaed polo dinner given by Mr. and Mrs. E.
White at the Australia the other evening is
alleged to have been the most sparkling as-
sembly of the Well-Fed for quite 48 hours.
F. E. Winchcombe, M.L.C., presides over
the Wild Life Preservation Society in this
State. The society met the other evening
and had a thrilling story of osprey fatalities
to tell in connection with this season’s mil-
linery. Lieut.-Gov. Cullen took the chair,
and A. S. Le Souef, David G. Stead, and the
learned W. W. Froggatt—who knows the
family history of ants, fleas and frivolous
ladybirds—made lantern-slide remarks. Sud-
den deaths in egret settlements are likely
to have the brake applied, if the Wild Life
Preserving Co. goes on harrowing the feel-
ings of Woman with its tragic pictures of
orphan bundles of white fluff writhing on
an empty stomach.
Mr. Herbert Allnutt, who recently mar-
ried Miss Lucy Cobham, of Mosman, is a
son of Parson Allnutt, of Cobbitty, where
they only change their shepherds of souls
about three times in a century. The Allnutt
family has kept open house to the country-
side for nearly 30 years, in a handsome old
stone rectory with gabled roof, and heavy green
whiskers of ficus, or some other close-fitting
herbage that climbs over its face. Before
Parson Allnutt’s day, Canon Pain, who’s now
another kind of ecclesiastical gun in Gipps-
land, put in about 25 years as Anglican
rounder-up of psalms and hymn tunes in the
Cobbitty paddocks. He followed, I think,
the Hassall autocracy, which lasted 40 years
or so. Ancient inhabitants prefer the mild
sway of the present parson, who is a good
sport, and encourages local cricket in the
rectory paddocks. Here, under the shade of
great wild apple trees, I saw a mighty com-
bat for supremacy last summer, when Brown-
low Hill estate (the Downes property) tried
to wipe the eye of Cobbitty with a cricket
ball. The Allnutt cow which grazed amongst
the players’ legs took reasonable care to
avoid swallowing the ball.
Yarraford, a usually peaceful village about
six miles from Glen Innes, has recently had
a violent eruption of Royal portraits. Big
and genial H. C. Holmes, a local identity in
wool, was moved last year to get rid of some
spare cash, and presented Yarraford with
the “King Edward Memorial Hall,” Miss
Brace, an English agricultural lady, who put
some of her capital into a snug holding in
those parts, tripped back to Fogland in 1910
with her friend, Miss Violet Alcock. Their
return to Yarraford was the occasion of a
school-children’s corroboree to welcome hun-
dreds of library books impounded by the
Brace-Alcock duet on the other side. Violet,
by the way, was secretary of the London Ly-
ceum Club for about seven years, and knows
the ropes. With Gov. Chelmsford to give
a tug at this end, she engineered the presenta-
tion of signed portraits of George and Mary
to the “King Edward Memorial Hall.” F.
J. Thomas, M.L.A. for the district, tore down
the drapery that shrouded the pictures in
the presence of the local nobility and gentry.
Yarraford is at present bearing its honors un-
der a hard frost, and is suspected of giving it-
self Coronating airs which may incite other
New England settlements to take to drink.
Off to try the snow on skis on Kosci-
usko’s hoary head —Mr. and Mrs. David
Maughan (she is a daughter of Edmund Bar-
ton), the Misses Watt (from Moree), Mr.
and Mrs. H. W. Allen, Dr. Gordon Moodie
(of Candelo), Mrs. Mackenzie (who is at
home when she is in Jondaryan, Q.), Mrs.
Forbes (another inter-State ski-er from Vic-
toria), Mrs. J. Strang (who’s come from
Maoriland), and an Adelaide man, Dr.
Shorney.
Who is the Cavendish-Bentinck the Van-
couver World says is following the Shef-
field Choir round the map? The Duke of
Portland, head of the Cavendish-Bentinck
clan, has a large circle of relatives of every
age and weight. The alleged love-lorn mem-
ber of the family is probably a half-nephew
or some other fractional part of the straw-
berry patch.
Lord Henry Cavendish-Bentinck, half-
brother of the present Duke, married a lady
with the ear-splitting prefix of Baroness
Mary C. de Heeckeren-Wassenaer, and has
two sons. One of these would be about the
age, 30 or thereabouts, of the Portland sprig
who is said to have laid down his share of
the family diamonds at the feet of Miss
Edith George, of the Sheffield Voices. She
is a Yorkshire lass, with an elder sister who
acts as watch-dog. The voyage from Fog-
land to St. John’s, N. 8., is quoted as the
scene of the Bentinck’s first proposal. Later
he is said to have chased the girl of his heart
across the All-red route, and to have booked
for these parts by the Zealandia.
A minor wail has just come from Thurs-
day Island. “Milk,” writes an indignant
housemother, “is Is. a quart, and meat 9d. a
pound all round—and generally it’s all
gristle. Children in every shade, from bis-
cuit to ebony, are as plentiful as peas in
pods, and tin houses scintillate in the vivid
sunshine in dazzling, irregular patches. The
surrounding sea is dotted with picturesque
islets, _ and the tropical vegetation is hand-
some in high-colored tones; but we’re more
interested in the sea, which contains pearls,
sharks, divers and plenty of oysters and
fish. Goats are as numerous as the varie-
gated children, and they all play happily
together.”
The British chaperon with “high” connec-
tions, Court influence, and no cash, has been
epidemic during the Coronating splash in
London. She has been stretching out a
hawk-like claw for Australian gold, with
large promises of introducing girls from this
side into the inner circle of the Best Set—
at bridge and other lotteries. The rich
American girl has either grown wary of this
type of elderly siren, or takes charge of her
own charms in Cold-land Society; hence
the ogre’s hunt after possible Australian
“clients.” It would be interesting to hear
whether any have fallen into her hands, and
how they fared afterwards.
