Anthony Hope’s new book, “Mrs. Maxon
Protests” (Roycroft Library), is a medi-
ocre love-story which follows afar off with
discreet silences —the mental earthquakes of
Wells’s “New Machiavelli.” This time it is
the lady who experiments, with a net result
of considerably less wreckage than she’d be
likely to achieve in a plain old world which
keeps Mrs. Grundy as perpetual referee. The
principal love affair of Mrs. Maxon, who is
young, pretty and emotional, doesn’t meet
with the approval of the cook-housemaid, but
otherwise seems to be fairly clear of fem-
inine scratches. Anthony Hope gives Winnie
Maxon £l5O a year of her own, stately seal-
skin coats and other desirable clothes, a
legacy of £l5OO, and, finally, a husband with
a new country house and old-fashioned
ideas. Hope’s book is an incentive to any
woman who’s ruminating over the “incom-
patibility of temper” crease in her matri-
monial affairs to try the Mrs. Maxon recipe.
Results ax - e likely to be mixed.
Yours with a broad nib.

“Out Back” is an unknown country to thousands of Australians, and on
that account possesses a charm that is all its own. Very few of our writers
have given faithful accounts of things as they are out there ; some are all gloom,
others all caricature: but in Sumner Locke we have an author who strikes a
happy medium, with the happiest results.. “Mum Dawson” is a pen picture
worthy of Dickons \ drawn, seemingly, without effort, so natural is it, «md
illumined with bright splashes of humor which are inimitable. It is safe to
predict that this new author will have a vogue that will rival, if not outrival,
any of the present-day Australian writers.

When the Dashwoods visit the city the fun begins, for Old John
and Young Peter seem incapable of keeping out of mischief, and
manage somehow to entangle the whole family in their ludicrous
escapades until mother and the girls begin to long for home. For

In the whole history of Australian bushranging no gang was more notorious than that captained
by Ned Kelly. Various accounts of the doings of this gang have appeared, but none bears tfte nan-
mark of truth so pointedly as these leaves from a dead man’s diary, as edited by Mr. 1 ratt, wno nas

“GAMBLER'S GOLD,"
By ARTHUR WRIGHT, Author of “A Rogue’s Luck,” etc.
WITH FOUR FULL-PAGE ILLUSTRATIONS.
PRICE, Is. POSTAGE, Id.
Gamblers of all kinds have a place in this book —the card-player at the club, the punter at the
racecourse, and the larrikin at the two-up school. The scenes at the various places are depicted with
this author’s usual power and picturesqueness, the characters being splendidly drawn and the incidents
thrilling in the extreme. There is a description of a fight worthy of Conan Doyle; a callous murder
for which an innocent man is blamed; a night at a two-up school as vividly portrayed as on a cine-
matograph screen; and a welshing incident at the racecourse. Side by side with all this there is a
dramatic love story which ends happily.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 4, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Lady Dudley, who is now on the high
seas, gave a carefully trimmed-down party
before she left here. There were only about
oO guests, and to them tenor Kirby, the pet
of duchesses, sang several songs in his best
manner. The day was cold, and the Little
Countess, in the most informal way, begged
the company to “come near the fire.” The
hostess wore a narrow’-skirted white cloth
dress that had, instead of the fashionable
fish-tail of the moment, a generous train,
ihe G.-G. strolled in with an affable air and
said “How d’ye do?” all round ; then grace-
fully retreated to the less-crowded fire in his
den. Governor Fuller and his lady came
late from the football match. His sub.-Ex.
inarched in with muddy boots and trousers
turned up above his spats. Mrs. Alfred
Miller struck the finest frocking note with a
beautifully-cut dress of black velvet, and hat
to match. Mrs. Reggie Power was in pale-
green ninon, with a wide edging of silver
lace and pale-grey squirrel fur. Mrs. Hughes,
the John. Grices and Mrs. Deakin smiled in
the firelit circle. John Madden’s lady,
like Mrs. Miller, was in black velvet. Tea
was a comfortable, sit-down affair, and was
served in the dining-room. Later, the
guests strolled back, and Kirby sang some
more.
The' Grand National, at Flemington on
Saturday, attracted a fine field of specta-
tors. It was a day of iced sunshine, and
the crowd kept moving briskly all the time.
Lady. Fuller ventured out in purple cloth
tailorings and beaver furs. Miss Phipps, in
a twisted-up velvet hat and turned-down
collar, moved about with a “sporting” air,
while his Ex. and an aide or two haunted
the paddock. Vera Clarke went into the
vice-regal box and chatted with the invalid
lady for some time. She wore squirrel
trimmings. to her grey velvet dress and a
waratah pinned in her squirrel muff.
Mrs. Warrnambool Airlie, with some up-
holstery borrowed from the squirrel family,
aroused admiration. There were two rows of
fur edging on her gown, with cape and tur-
ban to match. The squirrels would have
been delighted if they could have heard the
exclamations of the beholders. Mrs. Reggie
Power also flaunted squirrel furnishings on
the edge of her silver-green frock. She
passed the time of day on the lawn inside a
real Paisley coat and a squash hat that dis-
guised the wearer effectively. An exquisite
rose mnon dress was partly visible beneath
the Paisley. One of the smart-setters, also
hidden beneath an extinguisher, had on a
tight-fitting dress of mole velvet. A black
velvet dress of the same clingsomeness ac-
companied her.
Alfred Deakin escorted his tall young
daughter about, and theatrical stars twinkled
everywhere. Big Hugh Buckler, who is al-
ways a fashion-plater, had his pretty fair-
haired wife with him in a smart frock.
Governor Fuller seems to have sworn off
travelling in homeward-bound race specials
since he was left standing on Flemington
platform by a train which didn’t know
who he was. On Saturday the racegoers
who rushed for the first train discovered
only a gorgeous special car, with its guard’s
box and engine. Into this brief caravan
the Government House party stepped and
steamed away, while Croker of the Waist
and other stewards bowed their adieux.
State Government House parts company
on Sunday mornings, the Fullers going off
to St. Paul’s Cathedral, where the bells
deafen passers-by on the only quiet morning
of the week, while the Linlithgow pair go up
Collins-street hill to Scots’ Church, and “sit
under” Dr. Marshall, who christened brother
Charles Melbourne in the not so long ago.
The Fuller lady, after several hard laps
on Sassiety’s saucer track, had to retire
temporarily last week. She arrived from
England with a very young baby, and
straightway had to start shaking hands
at Parliament House. A rush of minor func-
tions and the Coronation flurry followed;
but she kept on till a race to Ballarat was
the next item on the programme. Then she
had to admit she was tired, and a doctor’s
“rest and quiet” decree closed her Ex.’s en-
gagement book. So it came to pass that
Governor Fuller went north to the City of
South-street alone. The hatpin sex there-
abouts was bitterly disappointed, but it had
to be. At the Art Association’s ball, the
new sub-Ex. danced with President Mrs.
Brind, and tried to smile for two, but Bal-
larat could not forgive him.
The Glassy Harem chose a bad day for its
reopening. Sassiety was mostly booked for
the Grand National on Saturday; so a lot
of people who would ordinarily have been on
the ice were on the turf. The floor, never-
theless, was fairly well covered. The ice
dressing this season is to be black velvet,
judging by the number of frocks present.
The warm, durable stuff shows up well on
the gleaming surface. Marie Hall, the girl
who will inherit a bank vault of the Ben-
digo Watson gold on her 21st birthday, was
garbed in the sleek material. Nancy Madden,
daughter of the newly-knighted, was another
in sable attire, only she added a Fauntleroy
trimming of lace collar and cuffs.
The Chinese Consul, his wife and his secre-
tary resolutely refused to dance at the Gaud
Mayoral Ball. The trio are Wesleyans—
the sect which regards leaping or gyrating
to music as the favorite pastime of the Lost.
Governor Fuller is darkly suspected of being
a Wowser, _ because, when he danced at the
same function, he seemed to jump with both
feet.
So numerous were the functions of Coron-
ation time that my pen could not race round
to all. The official ball given by Mayor
Hughes, of St. Kilda, must, however, be
awarded belated mention. It was complete
in every respect, including supper and prettv
girls, and it rolled along furiously for half
a dozen delightful hours or more. The
amber-tinted lights offered every complexion
a good chance, and if there were any people
who didn’t give three cheers as they left
they haven’t been heard of.
A horrid man writes:—
I don’t see a word in our Johanna’s letter about
the lovely revelation which happened at the Gaud
Mayoral ball when one of the plumpest and most dis-
tinguished of the dames was dancing a two-step. She
had a vice-regal aide for a partner, and in her en-
thusiasm for the mazy whirl she raised her left arm—
the train-bearing arm—higher than prudence would
have suggested. The result was a choice display of
exquisitely rounded limb in white silk stocking. It
was the penalty that plumpness pays for dispensing
with underskirts. Women seated near about, in-
cluding Lady Dudley and the Puller lady, blushed
audibly behind their fans, whilst downlookers in the
balcony enjoyed their confusion. But seeing that the
star performer was quite unconscious of her leg-
show, the G.-G. Countess gave the cue for blushing
to cease. She whispered to the other vice-regal
matron, and they broke into a forgiving smile.
An informal meeting at State Government
House last week discussed the subject of the
City Newsboys’ ball. Lady Fuller is taking
the usual vice-regal motherly interest in the
newsboys, and she made some very practical
suggestions for extracting cash from the
pockets of two-steppers. The tickets, in the
interests of sweet Charity, are usually high-
priced, and some too-frugal souls apparently
resent it. A soprano voice piped up at the
meeting and asked if there would be family
tickets —as if it were an excursion to the
seaside! Lady Fuller sweetly remarked :
“We are not organising this dance for plea-
sure, but to make every penny we can.”
Mrs. Professor Peterson and her choir of
women killed two birds with one song last
week, a newly-elected president being hon-
ored, and Bandmaster Sousa farewelled at
one and the same time. The conductress
made a warm-hearted speech of welcome to
Mrs. Harper, who has taken the vacant chair
of Lady Carmichael, departed; and Mrs.
Harper, with her arms full of presentation
vegetables, replied gracefully, and said that
she wanted to be an active head and lift up
her voice in the choruses. The white-robed,
hatless choir listened approvingly to the hon-
eyed remarks, to which Sousa in his turn
contributed. Then it uprose and sang
various melodies, including a quaint abori-
ginal chorus in the kindergarten manner.
Pianist Leonard Borwick, who has been
spell-binding audiences in the Town Hall, is
an unassuming man with pale blue eyes
and hair cropped close in the basin fashion.
He creeps on to the. platform like a mouse,
and_ starts to play brilliantly without am'
preliminary canters up and down the key-
board. On the first night his interpretations
so roused some of the musical people that
they could only express themselves by stand-
ing on their chairs and waving hankerchiefs.
The average critic reverently hands him
sugarplums of praise for breakfast, and the
general opinion is that he earns them all.
At his recitals, the seats under the gallery
hold the intensely musical section, who wait
for tedious hours, after a successful early-
door rush, armed with nothing but a block
of chocolate and a sheaf of the old masters’
notes. In the front seats, as usual, the
motored take their classics in comfort, with
a judicial after-dinner air, and wonder if the
star can be captured for high tea on Sunday
evening. The Thursday afternoon recital
drew a mostly fur-muffled audience, in which
the stage was well represented.
Nellie Veitch was at home on Saturday
night to a hundred or so friends. The guest-
in-chief was Ethel Irving, who brought with
her Mr. E. Irving Porteous and Mr. Leading-
man Ewart. The visiting star and the hostess
are mutual friends of Genevieve Ward. At
supper Gaud Mayor Davey toasted them both
and each replied for herself. The party
filled two comfortable, fire-lit rooms, and
tenor Kirby, with the order presented to
him by Queen Margharita of Italy pinned on
his coat lapel, sang a few trifles from
Puccini’s operas, to the accompaniments of
Mrs. Cooke. Miss Winkelmann, baritone
Collier, and Amadio of the flute also con-
tributed. The only amateur performer was
Dr. McAdam’s pretty daughter Beryl, who
drew a capable bow across her violin. Mrs.
MeAdam and Mrs. Major Patterson helped
in the hostessing. Miss Irving’s dress was
royal purple, with a fichu of ninon, and a
purple fillet in her hair. Nellie Veitch wore
white silk and turquoises.
“Why, I haven’t seen you for ages !” This
sentence was repeated 35,487 times at the
first reunion of the Loreto Past Students’
Association, at the Austral Salon, last
week. It was a searching but good-
humored female stocktaking, and a
bureau of information regarding mar-
riages, engagements and so forth. Rita
Hope, Roy Cook and Violet Somerset con-
tributed to the programme, and Unie Russell,
besides reciting from Dickens’s Christmas
Carol, stage-managed some effective tableaux,
in which Bertha Collin, Lyle Christian,
Eileen Deegan, Norman Mayes and several
infants of tender years posed as the Cratchit’s
family party.
At a suburban Bachelors’ Ball of much
tonnage the other night, none of the dancing
belles attracted so much attention as the lady
who sat around in a large coat, and was in-
troduced as the wife of an otherwise married
man. Everybody talks of this startling
social incident as one of the “warmest”
things that ever happened in a suburb where
the influence of Mrs. Grundy seems to have
fallen to vanishing point.
The Art Students are preparing for their
Persian Ball, to be held on Friday, July 7.
Many strange garments are being built, and
the shade of Omar is expected to be present
—if it doesn’t drop in at a wine-cellar on
the way.
One night last week the supping owls of
a Collins-street foodery had some excitement
with their coffee and cigarettes. A lordly
youth, who drives the biggest motor and
smokes the fattest cigar in Melbourne, was
inside with the crowd ; his out-size in cars
reposed unattended by the kerb, as the owner
is his own engineer. All was right enough
until a well-known man strolled out of the
place and collided with two strange females.
They smiled radiantly on his apologies, and
he mischievously invited them to go for a
ride in his juggernaut. They readily ac-
cepted the offer. He assisted them in, and
then he excused himself while he collected
his friend. In a few minutes out came the
rightful owner with his party. The party
recoiled horror-stricken from the two fe-
males, while the owner fiercely ordered them
out. But they refused resolutely to budge.
The argument proceeded and a sizable crowd
had gathered round, when a policeman ap-
peared in the distance. That settled it. The
damsels fled.
Melbourne just now is hot on the subject
of paper-bag cookery. Every woman who
cooks with enthusiasm has a regard for paper
coverings laid flat either above or below
special confections; and the paper-bag idea
is an extension of the protective principle.
The idea is to cook, not isolated dainty
dishes, but complete dinners in specially pre-
pared bags. Like most new things, it is
quite old, and various city chefs who have
been interviewed amidst the steam admit
they. occasionally cook en papillotes with
delicious results. Experimenters will pro-
bably brew disaster if they commence with a
stew and use as covering the cast-off en-
velope of the last two pounds of sugar sent
from the grocer's. A brisk demand for the
special sort of bags required has set in, and
a local firm of paper manufacturers has set
out to supply it; so every woman will soon
be able to attack the problem fully equipped.
The great Maryannless section are rejoicing,
as the bag system, if successfully worked,
means no greasy pots and pans.
R. S. Smythe is out of hospital after un-
dergoing ah operation. It was hard luck
for a man who had reached four-score
without any illness beyond the mumps
and measles of childhood to be delivered into
the surgeon’s hands at last. After the ordeal,
the Much Travelled received an invitation
to a smoke night. He sent in reply this gay
little message of regret: “I could come, and
I would, only that your other guests might
fancy I had been playing football against
Carlton.
The sad death of Edith Muntz will be
mourned by the many Melbournians who knew
the clever girl who did so well at the Uni-
versity. She was the daughter of the late
Thomas Muntz, civil engineer, and had just
returned from a round-the-world trip with
her artist-sister, Mrs. Muntz-Adams. The
l° n S journey was undertaken after a severe
nervous breakdown caused by overstudy. She
appeared to be well on the road to health
when she arrived home the other day; her
death, therefore, came as a severe shock to
her friends, who had thought that the time
tor anxiety was past.
Australia has just lost its other Tom
Price. The two —one a Premier, the other
a Colonel—were as unlike as men well can
be; and the gentler one died first. Colonel
lorn—the “Fire low and lay ’em out” of old
strike days—had long been very sick, and
his name had dropped right out of late.
_ Times are changing. Nowadays even a
bishop may be suspected of not being a Right
Thinking person. Accordingly a private
word comes across the Strait from Tasmania
that, though everybody expected Bishop Mer-
cer to be first chairman of the little island’s
first Wages Board—in the boot trade —some
of the influential employers mutter their mis-
givings; and, as the Bishop thinks there
should be unanimity, the chances are he will
not allow himself to be nominated.
Mrs. Essie Perrin-Kcrouse and her hus-
band have returned from snow-capped Buf-
falo, and solemnly declare that they found
it colder than Switzerland. One of their
meal-time diversions was chipping off bits of
frozen salads. One day the 14st. husband
tried to walk across the frozen lake without
skates. When he was some little way from
shore, the ice-film caved suddenly, and the
tail fair bride on the shore thought she
would have to order mourning. Fortun-
ately, however, the water was not a hun-
dred fathoms deep, and the 14st. bridegroom
was able to swim or walk out.
A notable drinking fountain was formally
conferred upon Melbourne last Saturday,
and a string of flags waving across the inter-
section of Bourke and Russell streets pro-
claimed the event. This time the fountain
was not another Boer War memorial, but a
monument to the glory of one Mr. Ferguson,
who appears to have been—if he is not still
—a hard-working sec. of the Total Abstin-
ence Society. The memorial tablet leaves
one in doubt as to whether Mr. F.’s services
to the Cause in the past are being continued
m the present; but the monument itself is a
tall, unquestionable fact —a brown sandstone
pillar on a substantial grey granite pedestal,
rather _ obstructive to traffic, but less un-
sesthetic than the cabmen’s shelter standing-
further down Russell-street.
One supposes that Mr. Keast, M.L.A.,
voted his very best vote against the new
Victorian land tax, but clearly he doesn’t
suspect the Federal tax of ruinous effects
upon the country at large. And, being in
the real estate business, he ought to know.
One of his latest transactions is the pur-
chase of the Newmarket Station, of 22,000
acres, near Hay (N.S.W.), where son
Stanley will have scope for growing large
crops of sheep and other vegetables. A
frontage to the Murrumbidgee promises a
guarantee against drought, though that
river, to be sure, is apt to become rather
like a ditch in dry seasons.
From “Rosna,” who has been in London
helping George It. choose a hat:—
Musical Australians are conspicuous in London at
present. Last week small Alma Moodie, the Queens-
land fiddler-child, gave a recital, with a committee
and patron list a mile long, beginning with Princess
Louise, and including no end of Viscountesses and
Marchionesses. The new G.-G. Denmans lent their
town house and shouted tea. Katherine Goodson,
Hugh Peyton and Lady Tree helped, so no wonder the
prodigy scored a big attendance. The critics agreed
about her extraordinary talent and surprising confi-
dence.
Mrs. Doubleday is in London, and daughter Leila
arrives next week from Vienna to make her London
debut in a recital with orchestra at the Queen’s
Hall on June 9.
The Titian-haired Adelaide girl, Daisy Kennedy is
one of the seven favored fiddling pupils of Sevcik,
and her exalted maestro thinks so well of her that
he brings the bud to burst in London in July. One
also hears a good deal about the Victorian pianist
William Murdoch. Clytie Hine the pretty Adelaide
soprano, has secured another scholarship at the Royal
College of Music, and is already coming in for occa-
sional engagements. The change in her appearance
is wonderful. The frail girl has grown into a bright-
faced maiden, full of rose-bloom and energy.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
For months past we have heard of the pending
removal of Government House to Torrens Park •
present Government House was to be handed over
to the University, which is sorely in need of more
elbow-room. Now everything is off. Premier Ver-
ran said so in the House on Wednesday. lie gave
two reasons-d) the price for the new place was too
stiff, and (2) the Torrens Park premises were un-
suitable, anyway. So we are saved the agonv of
pursuing our dances and dinners into a remote fast-
•i,Talking of Government House, Lady Bosanquet is
ill, and will be unable to fulfil any official engage-
ments for 10 days or so. Presumably she is suffer-
ing the effects of the recent season of gaiety.

Speaker Jenkin Coles is also ill, so ill that, for the
first time m 21 years, his seat in Parliament knows
hun not. Indeed, the week as a whole has been a
drab one. John Downer (eldest son of the Downer
Knight) was to have been married before this, and
his friends were shocked to hear of his death on
i hursday. It was known that he was ill, but there
were hopes he was on the mend—so much so that Mrs
Edmonds (his cousin and his fiancee’s sister) gave a
dance only last week. And then there was Mr. John
Chnstison, who died suddenly at Clare. Since his
breakdown in 1907 Surveyor-General Strawbridge
had been more or less of an invalid, and his death
on Sunday came as no surprise. Mr. E. M. Smith,
who is to be appointed in his place, has for years
been a pillar and warden of St. Peter’s (Anglican)
Cathedral. A wide circle of friends was shocked, too,
by news of the tragically-sudden death of Mrs. Geo’
Dempster, the young wife of a popular Stock Ex-
change man. She was one of the north-country
pastoral Gebhardts, and had been married only just a
year. A baby girl died with her,
A small girl who had the bad luck to be born on
the Orsova last trip, goes into the world saddled
thus—Orsova Mina Wilson Ingram Staunton Hodgson!
The ship, nurse, doctor, purser and captain have
been honored in the christening.
Tattersall’s Cup Meeting on Monday (the first time
the race has been cut away from the Easter meeting)
had the luck of a perfect day, and the committee
reaped a corresponding harvest. Femininity ran to
tweeds and tailor-mades. The more one sees of the
half-yard skirt the more fearsome an invention it
seems, and if the would-be smart dresser could see
herself as others see her walking up the grandstand
steps, she’d mend her ways, and her modes.
The cottage at Dingley Dell, in the sou’-east,
where poet Adam Lindsay Gordon used to live, is
still in existence; and the Poetry Recital Society
has begun an agitation to get the Government to
buy and preserve it. I can’t say that the society
has received any furious encouragement so far.
The city garden scheme advances apace. Till now
the long narrow strip at the eastern end of North
Terrace has been Government property, and has
barely justified its existence. Now the city corpora-
tion is to take the ground in hand, and has an ex-
tensive scheme of landscape gardening in view. The
approach of the Angas memorial and Edward VII.
statue calls for some re-arrangement of existing lay
figures; so Venus will be removed from her prox-
imity to the national memorial and Government
House gates, and placed nearer Bobby Burns —-who
has not been heard to complain.
We’re the proud possessor of the lady who sampled
the first chloroform. The lady in question is a niece
of Sir James Young Simpson. In the days when he
discovered the ancesthetic, she (then aged' about 10)
and the rest of the family used to look upon the
sniffing of chloroform as a kind of game. Miss
Simpson’s niece is, in her tarn, a nurse, so evi-
dently a desire to heal the sick runs in the family.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

A RHEUMATIC
CRIPPLE.
Tells How He Banished
Suffering and Pain.
Could just drag along —Cured by
Dr. Williams' Pink Pills.
■ -%•
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are recommended
to Rheumatic sufferers with confidence, be-
cause they have cured hundreds of cases of
Rheumatism. That is the greatest test any
medicine can have —-the ability to cure. Mr.
C. Shanahan, bootmaker, of 67 Buckingham
Street, North Richmond, Melbourne, was
so badly attacked by Rheumatism that often
he could hardly drag one foot after the
other, and the weight of the bedclothes was
sometimes agonising to his aching muscles
and joints. Mr. Shanahan’s story, given in
his own words, will be of interest to anyone
suffering with Rheumatism.
“I could not make it out at first,” he
said, “but I noticed severe muscular pains
coming into my legs from the feet to the
knees. I got worse attacks as time went
on. The attacks might go away for a week
or so, or appear for a few days, but once I
was laid up for five weeks, in bed most of
the time, and unable to go out, and then I
was attacked in arms and shoulders as well.
I was always freer from the pain in winter,
but I dreaded the summer, for I always suf-
fered then. The perspiration would run off
me with the pain. I felt every change in
the weather. There was no sign externally,
but the muscles of the legs would feel as if
they were being all drawn up and twisted.
1 was always having the parts rubbed with
liniments, but I could not find any relief. I
could not bear any heavy bedclothes, the parts
were so sore and tender, and the warmth
always made me feel worse. I would lie
awake afraid to move. 1 was afraid for
anyone to come near me. When the attacks
were on me I dreaded the least touch. My
legs were so stiffened that walking was most
painful. I would just drag my feet after
me, and I dare not put them on any cold
surface. I might feel quite well one moment,
and perhaps the slightest change of weather
would bring on an attack. I would have to
get up and walk about if I had been resting
awhile, and, again, if I had been walking
home very tired I would have to go and lie
down. I am glad to say that a course of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills took the complaint
right away. lam only too glad to give this
testimonial to help any other sufferer. This
remedy cured me, and I have not had any
sign of the Rheumatism since. My limbs are
as supple as could be wished.”
Remember, only the genuine Dr. Williams'
Pink Pills cure. Don’t be gulled into tak-
ing something said to be just as good. Of
all dealers, at 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes
15s. 3d., or from the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Co. of Australasia Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
HIS TASTE—POLITICAL.
THE BARMAN : “I see by the papers that the
majority of the Labor party are teetotalers.”
P. E WTER: "Meself, I favor sh (hie) Liberal
measure sh. ”
Provision made on the business paper of
the “Liberal” conference, held at Sydney:
“That all Labor seats should be contested by
staunch Liberal candidates at next elec-
tions.” No doubt a misprint for “paunch.”
Those croakers who averred that the Com-
monwealth could never man a nay, and
that the Australian didn’t take to the sea,
have got a backhander in the fact that the
3000 naval cadets whom the Defence author-
ities asked for promptly offered themselves;
also the offers came not from one or two dis-
tricts, but. from all round the Common-
wealth, and the requirements were filled
three times over. Obviously the lads trust
the administration to provide decent treat-
ment, decent pay, fair chances to rise, and,
of course, absence of yellow, brown or black
competition. When the mercantile marine
guarantees its lower-class employees as
much, it also will probably get all the native-
born sailormen it needs.
W. A. Trenwith has intimated to the Vic.
Liberal caucus that he wants to contest a
seat against a Labor candidate. In Tren-
with’s case, the cobbler hasn’t stuck to the
last. He has, instead, attached himself to
the Lost.
Jenkin Coles, Speaker of the S. Aus. As-
sembly, had last week to cease piling up his
world’s record any higher. Elected Speaker
a trifle over 21 years ago, he had never
missed a sitting of the House —till Wednes-
day, when Parliament was opened with him
in bed, and the doctors resolutely blocking
his desire to be present and chance the con
sequences.
“Con. I). O’Bolin,” from the North Coast:
In connection with the N.S.W. North Coast rail-
way, far-sighted officers saw the day afar off when the
traffic could not be manipulated on a single line, so
they provided that the tunnels should be wide enough
for a double set of rails. Good; but the contract
for the first section stipulated that the Wallarobba
tunnel, between Maitland and Dungog, should be
only wide enough for one line. All the other tun-
nels right to Grafton will be double. The leaving of
one narrow hole amid a number of wide ones, is, no
doubt, capable of explanation ; but at present, in the
absence of the explanation, it strikes the North
Coast man merely as a good joke.
“Norroy” sets the fireworks right:—
Numerous illuminations made a grievous mistake
with their “Long may they reign!” Queen Mary
does not reign. She is only Princess Consort, as
Albert was Prince Consort. Though called Queen,
she is not Mary 111. There is Mary 1., daughter of Henry
VIII., and there is Mary 11., who had the help of
William ; but though it was “William and Mary,” it
is not “George and Mary.” By the way, there is an-
other Mary, a German Princess, who is called Mary
111. by the handful of Jacobites, descendant of the
Stuarts. Our present Mary is, certainly, an heir to
the Throne but far removed. Strange to add, an-
other heir, ; till farther removed, is a German Prince
descended from Jerome, King of Wurtemberg, Na-
poleon’s brother.
A note from the Gallery:—
They’re still at it down below in the N.S.W. As-
sembly, and if a young and inexperienced policeman
had the right of entry, I’m doubtful if there would
be a quorum after he had finished serving sum-
monses for “language.” I’ve had a good few years
in this Gallery, and I’m getting’ used to it now —far
better than I was in the week of my first appear-
ance, when an hon. member looked up at me and
informed me in a loud voice that he would “pull
my windpipe out!” He mistook me for some-
body who had allegedly misreported him. Since
then I have heard members say things that would
probably get a man in the street a serving of hard
labor—things that even the Watchman wouldn’t
print. Young men, old men—they’re mostly all the
same in their standard of politeness. Even old
“wire whiskers” Lonsdale has taken to calling out
“liar” and “cur,” and looking for fight. “You are
the filthiest fellow in the House,” said one gentle-
man to another a few nights ago. Maybe; I duhno.
The smell of decayed language is too general.
The “confiscation” gag so persistently
used in connection with taxes on unimproved
land values is about the drollest thing in
Tory politics. All Government levies on pri-
vate possessions are confiscation. Income
tax and probate duty are
confiscation. Jewellery
valued at £5OOO may
change hands by testa-
mentary bequest 10 times
in as many years, and
the death of each succes-
sive, owner of the gew-
gaws entails payment of
probate duty on his lega-
tee. Yet the taxing of
those values is never
called “confiscation.” The
wearisome old gag is
confined to the woes of
landowners; and they
needn’t be taxed if they
don’t want to be. No-
body compels them to
own the earth. They can
sell it and bury the money
and live on £2 a week,
exempt from all taxation
whatsoever, if they wish it.
The more or less strenuous people who
want to advance Willie Watt to the Vic-
torian Premiership offer him this text: “Be
not Weary in well-doing.”
Opposition-leader Peake, of S. Aus., is
trying a No-Confidence motion just to make
sure where each member is. There are 21
Labor members in a House of 40, so there is
not much doubt as to the result.
Allen Taylor, having achieved his title,
has given notice that he will not accept re-
election as Gaud Mayor of Sydney next
year. The Labor vote in the City Council
is now almost strong enough to decide the
mayoral election, and after the next general
poll the withdrawal of Allen Taylor will pro-
bably mean that Sydney will have its first
Labor Mayor, as well as its first Labor
Premier.
It was given out at a recent Melbourne
meeting of the Federated Iron Workers’ As-
sociation that local employers were bringing
to Victoria a noted American strike-breaker.
The mission of the incoming wrecker is to
make the Free Workers’ Society an effective
weapon in the hands of the employers. The
manager of a Melbourne bank is the accepted
grand panjandrum of the Free Workers’
Society—which makes it plainer that the
society is just as “free” as any other ass that
tows a capitalistic apple-cart.
In S. Aus. the metropolitan seats in the
Assembly are all held bv Labor; and the
disgusted Register has started to try to
pull one or two out of the fire. It wants to
“revolutionise the electoral machinery by
the application of science,” so as to “en-
sure the full representation of the people.”
What, of course, the Register really wants
is the representation of the metropolitan
Tory minority, so that, added to the country
Tory vote, the minority of the whole people
may have a majority in the House.
With more than usual violence and ver-
bosity, Melbourne Argus accuses the Labor
Government of disloyalty, or something of
the sort, because of the “shabby and mean
display” it made in the way of fireworks the
other evening. But if “loyalty” is to be
gauged by the number of colored lights a
person hangs out, just where does the Argus
itself come in? On Coronation night (he
Age office was brilliant, and the Heralp
building was ablaze ; but the Argus’s dis-
play, carried out on a scale of lavish inex-
pensiveness, consisted of a small “G.R.” and
a thrippenny crown.
The land hunger is still very keen in S.
Australia. Recently 130 blocks near Tail-
em Bend were thrown open, and there were
786 applications for them. Tailem Bend is
on the fringe of “the desert,” down near the
Murray mouth ; but that apparently is the
best S. Australia can do for its hungry
young men, who can’t pay the appalling
prices which Fat demands for his fertile
sheep walks within a hundred miles of
Adelaide.
HELPING HIM TO A DECISION.
The Daily Telegraph hints at a Liberal-Labor
coalition in the N.S.W. Parliament. On the other
hand, the S. M. Herald predicts a slump in coali-
tions as far as Liberals are concerned.
THE HUNTER-STREET LADY: “By the way,
dearie, I 'opes you ’avent forgot how the
last of them 'mixed marriages' ended.”
Minister of Lands M'Kenzie (Vic.), join-
ing in a demonstration over the arrival of a
batch of Irish damsels who are to make
shirts in Melbourne, told the colleens: “You
will earn enough here to be independent of
matrimony.” The colleens did not seem to
rejoice at the prospect. A healthy girl has
to be earning more than 255. a week before
she loses the desire to have somebody else to
earn it for her. Anyhow, the sentiment is
wholly unsound, and implies that singlets
are of more consequence than triplets.
The policy of the Verran Government
for the new session of the S. Aus. Parlia-
ment includes Adult Suffrage for the Coun-
cil, Workmen’s Compensation, Early Clos-
ing, Land Values Taxation, a Bill to better
secure the purity of food and drugs—
mostly measures passed in previous sessions
by the Labor majority in the Assembly, and
either thrown out or mutilated by Toryfat
in the property Chamber. In addition to
making bricks, the Labor Government has
an idea of organising a fuel business, on
account of the rapacious clawing of a pri-
vate combine.
“O.”: The other week, N.S.W. Minister
for Public Instruction Beeby increased the
pay of 3A assistant men-teachers who were
married. I wasn’t married. But £l2 is an
immense amount of money to a 3A assist-
ant. So I persuaded a lady to come along
and help me get it. I don’t know whj
Beeby did what he did, because I don’t work
any better than I did before I got the extra
£l2; so I can only put it down to the Labor
party’s well-known prejudice against the
marriage tie and its notorious determination
to break up the home.
Asked in the N.S.W. Legislative Council
if the Federal Parliament had not given a
vote to inmates of State charitable institu-
tions, ex-Solicitor-General Garland snorted:
“Judging from the result, probably they
did.” The snarl comes ill from a man who
has had Jock Garland’s luck. After being
biffed by three Parliamentary electorates, he
was pitchforked into the nominee Chamber,
which is the Parliamentary charitable insti-
tution, and presented, not with a mere vote,
but with the fat billet of Solicitor-General,
at a handsome salary, contributed by the
same taxpayers who help to support the less
fortunate “failures” who have been pushed
into the non-Parliamentary almshouse.
The N.S.W. Labor Government managed
to squeeze its Industrial Arbitration Bill
through its second Assembly reading without
the help of the Storey “independents.” The
chief opposition to the measure is on account
of the inclusion of rural workers in the sched-
ule. The “Liberals” lay down the princi-
ple that the “great primary industries” can-
not afford the benefits of industrial legisla-
tion. Nevertheless the Liberal party in-
cluded the shearing industry in the Act
passed last Parliament. There is no logical
reason why the man who drags milk out of
a cow r should be denied the benefits afforded
to the man who drags wool off a sheep. But
it happened that the organised shearing in-
dustry is strong enough to obtain protec-
tion, while the rural workers are not. So
the -‘Liberal” party naturally only offered
protection to the strong, and let the weak
slide.
H. D. Morton, putting in a word in the
N.S.W. Assembly for tiie dreadful person
who drives the little slaves of the Cow, said
that the- Scotch farming commissioners only
made a hurried examination. That is true;
if they had had more time they could have
found out more horrors. They might, for
instance, have heard this wail of a Williams
River cow-king: “I can’t carry on if the agi-
tators get these high wages. I’ll give up
dairying, for I’d never pay 30s. a week to
any man for milking. I have a hard
enough struggle to make things pay now.”
And then they might have found out at the
factory that that grumbler's cream cheque
ran to £6O a month, that he had three State-
school boys doing the milking and separat-
ing, and that one received a munificent ss.
per week and keep, while he was actually
receiving £1 a week from an open-hearted
Department for the maintenance and edu-
cation of the other two. It is scandalous to
expect men like that to pay wages at all.
The S. M. Herald mourned the other dav
over another foreign land-investment co*
which was appalled to find that it hadn’t
earned as much as the land tax came to.
Well? If John Fairfax and Sons had a
printing machine that didn’t do one-fifth of
what it ought to do, wouldn’t John Fairfax
and Sons take steps to force it to either do
a fair thing or give place to some other
machine that would?
Labor-leader Bath, of Westralia, is nosing
out what smells like a pretty bad scandal.
He alleges that it is almost as hard for a
legitimate selector to get a prompt selec-
tion from the local State Government as it
is for him to crawl through a camel’s eye.
The result is that the would-be selector is
encouraged, and in many cases forced, to go
to some blood-sucking middleman, who, hav-
ing large areas in his maw, disgorges a trifle
at a price that means long, up-hill drudgery
for the newcomer.
The Sydney daily press has shoved the re-
port of the Decentralisation Commission,
“which didn’t appear to consider Sydney,"
into the darkest drawer of the editor’s table
after a very brief and passing attention. But
the soul-stirring matter of Government
House is still good for columns. It is a
grim illustration of the ideas of value on the
part of the drapery-shops’ organs: (1) Self,
(2) Sydney, (3) the Ma State, and, if no :
thing else is doing, (4) Australia. Generally
something else is doing.
N.S.W. Works Minister Griffith is on a
good wicket in the Bill he proposes for stan-
dardising land-valuations throughout the
State. A valuing department is to be estab-
lished, to consist of independent experts who
will work on a scientific and harmonious
plan, and the results of their labors will be
available for such purposes as Federal land
taxation, municipal and other rating, crown
land reappraisements, etc. This will knock
on the head the self-valuation of squatters'
lands : —achieved by the simple process of first
electing nominees to shire councils, and then
inciting the nominees to elect their nominee
as town clerk. A low valuation in such
cases means a high endowment; so they rake
it in with both hands. If Griffith’s law is
passed, it will cut this business out by the
roots, and the public will begin to get a
square deal.
IN THE CRYPT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In keeping with the permanent organisation of
the party, the N.S.W. Liberal Association now has a
permanent home more worthy of its objects. The
rooms in Pitt-street have been remodelled more on
the lines of a club, to Which Liberals may betake
themselves for information, recreation and social
intercourse. Members will soon have a comfortable,
well-stocked library, and a full set, etc. r |k. etc. —
Solemn News Item.
“This way! This way!” the old man cried;
My sleeve he tightly gripped,
And from Life’s busy, roaring tide
Dragged me to see the crypt.
Between two massive plates of brass
I glimpsed a stair of stone.
“Down here,” I mused, “the dead folk pass,
And mourners sigh and groan ! ”
He like a spider dragged me in
That chilly place of tears ;
The city’s deep, incessant din
Came faintly to my ears.
When half-way down the dusty stair
I saw a slab. “Alas!
What wight unnamed is buried there?”
He said “It is the gas!”
Amazed, I let him drag me down
The second shadowed flight;
No longer could I hear the town
And there was little light.
“Come in! Come in!” he whispered low.
Some bowed, white-bearded souls
Were sitting in a silent row
Perusing printed scrolls.
Sad, rat-like rustlings filled the tomb,
As hopeless leaves were turned ;
Like lions’ eyes within the gloom
The yellow grave-lights burned.
“Whose vault is this?” I asked my guide.
“Don’t make that loud hubbub!”
He pleaded faintly at my side;
“This is the Lib’ral Club!”
I broke from him and up the stair
To Life and sunshine sped,
For I am young and cannot bear
Communing with the dead.
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Handicaps for Melbourne and Caulfield
Cups suggest that the 12 top-weights in either race
are likely to include the winner. The three-year-olds,
headed by Jacamar (brother to Poseidon) at 7st.
111 b., are at present an uncertain quantity; but the
claims of Trafalgar, Comedy King, Prince Foote and
Styria (if she be sound) are undeniable —in the two-
mile event, at any rate. For the Caulfield Cup,
there can be no questioning the pretensions of
Broadsword (Bst. 51b.). A horse that could run
second for the Futurity Stakes (7 furlongs) under
a heavy burden, and third for the Newmarket, pre-
vious to winning the Doncaster mile at Kandwick,
must be dangerous in the Caulfield Cup with Bst. 51b.
up, if he can stay the extra distance. And why not?
Thus far the form of Broadsword in Australia favors
the idea that he can at least go further than a
mile, and is as much of a stayer as Flavinius proved
himself in capturing the Caulfield prize last year.
* * * *
The G. N. Hurdle Race, for the 13th time
in succession, was captured by a nag carrying less
than list. During those 13 years only two of the
winners have carried more than lOst. And now, for
the eighth consecutive time, the winner had a com-
paratively light age, as well as a compara-
tively light weight, in his favor. Since 1903 no
“aged” animal has been fast enough. Those that
might have had pace enough have been stopped by a
crushing weight, or have been killed on some previous
occasion. Under present conditions of hurdle racing
there seems to be no hope for the good old stagers
in the biggest events. With steeplechasing it is dif-
ferent. The high-weighted veterans are still worth
considering where log fences and stone walls are
concerned ; nevertheless, a wise punter doesn’t want his
fancv horse to be too veteranish. For instance, Bribery,
a winner of both Grand Nationals, is now a 10-year-
old ; and, although the old hero has a youthful spirit
and paws the ground in his readiness to be off and
doing, he is probably a bit too far gone in the
tooth to carry 12st. 81b. to the fore next Saturday.
His illustrious brethren, Grafnax and Ripon, are two
years younger, and may for that reason only hold
Bribery safe. But this is another story.
Arquebus, from S.A., was whispered last
week as being “another York” for the Hurdle Race,
and, with only 9st. to tackle, the carefully-prepared
stranger ran up to the whisper. A trifle on the leggy
side, Arquebus is bred to stay, and the three miles
were not too far for him. He won practically from end
to end. With the light-weight out of the way the
result would have been according to the betting, for,
in spite of the warning of the Calendar, punters took
short prices about Sparkle (list. 91b.) and Obi (list.
51b.), who filled the places, whilst the other favorite,
Battery, finished fourth. Sparkle’s effort to overhaul
the leader was most meritorious, and Obi also de-
served a bit of credit for beating 17 members of the
field. Each of the placed horses was a 5-year-old.
* * * *
The S’Australian contingent at Fleming-
ton gave free expression to their feelings re the bet-
ting. They had to take as little as 14 to 1 at the
start about the “good thing” which had given no
hint of its goodness in public; and many of them
backed the gallant Sparkle at 5 to' 1, or less, for a
saver. With a “1 —2” tote in operation, they would
undoubtedly have got better odds about the winner,
whilst the thousands of backers of Sparkle would
have drawn a small but satisfactory divvy for the
second horse. Extraordinary that the public, who
provide the capital for the business of horseracing,
don’t form themselves into an Employers’ Union,
and wait upon the Government to protest against the
wickedness of being compelled to pay enormous wages
to bloated bookmakers for work which could be so
much more cheaply, honestly and quietly conducted
by a philanthropic machine.
It was a pleasure last Saturday to see
Dyed Garments, an old Sydney Cup winner, assert his
class distinction by running away with the Maiden
Hurdles. Another conspicuously good thing was
Grafton King, the tall, long-striding gentleman that
was let into the Caulfield Cup at 6st. 131 b., and looks
quite capable of giving 3st. and a beating to some
of the entrants.
* * * *
The G. N. Steeple happens at Flemington
next Saturday. At time of writing Grafnax is first
favorite in the non-published betting, and on form
he looks capable of carrying even 13st. 51b. to vic-
tory. Ripon also is a stable fancy, and he and the
Grafton horse are, as was previously remarked, two
vears less ancient than Bribery. Simple Simon and
Confide look dangerous in the lOst.-odd division, and
Squirm, Nimrod and Obelisk among the lighter
weights. If Nimrod can stay three miles over big .lumps,
his performance in the G. N. Hurdle gives him a good
chance. But, seeing that this paper has previously
mentioned Confide as a likely winner, it had better
stick to her. Her sex is against her, but the market
indications are favorable. #
Tattersall’s Club, a very strong social body
in Adelaide now, has given its new secretary, Jack
Goffage', his first chance at running a metropolitan
race meeting. With a holiday, and perfect weather,
the totalisator takings flew up to £l4,ooo—close on
double those for any previous meeting of this club.
George V., as a youngster, was identified
with a notable sporting coincidence. A gee-gee
named after him—George Frederick (dam Princess
of Wales) —won the English Derby on his birthday.
An Adelaide jockey—or, rather, boy-rider
.—has had a curious experience and an unpleasant
shock. Himself a “maiden,” and riding an outsider
in a small race with a big field, he pulled his mount,
or let it pull him, all over the course, got 100yds.
behind in a mile race, and then came with such a
rush that he landed second money and a £5 16s.
tote dividend. To his amazement, he found the
crowd receiving him with jeers, the trainer talking of
a horsewhip and the stewards severely carpeting him
for “gross incompetence.” It is a hard world.
The smug apology of the S. M. Herald
in describing the small ponies at the recent polo
tournament in Sydney: “Some of them showed ominous
dark red stains on their flanks, but they were the pick
of the polo ponies of the State, and are used to the
iab of the spur.” But if Bill the carter tried to ex-
plain to the police magistrate that the hard-working
horse which he had cruelly treated had been flogged
so regularly that it had become quite accustomed to
the lash or the boot, the indignation of the beak
would carry him to the extent of calling Bill “a
hardened ruffian” ; and if Bill got out of it with less
than seven days’ hard labor, he’d at least leave the
best part of a month’s wages behind him.
Delightful weather and two large-sized
surprises characterised League Rugby in Sydney on
Saturday. One of the unforeseen happenings occurred
at Wentworth Park, where, before the eyes of a de-
lirious crowd, Newtown, last year’s premier club,
was dealt with most severely by Annandale. It was
a great game, and fulfilled the prophecy in this
column that Annandale would be shortly kicking up
some dust. The ’Dalers have been practising com-
bination, and they unwound some lovely passing
rushes, which Newtown, unable to stop, could only
stand and gape at. Ray Norman raged like 40
heathen; he was here, he was there, he was every-
where, and his barrackers hung limply against the
fence with burst throats. Newtown crawled off,
beaten by 6 to 3. The other trouble arose at North
Sydney Oval, where the mighty were put down from
their seat, and North Sydney kicked the stuffing out
of the hitherto victorious Glebe. Perhaps it was the
sea voyage that upset the beefy ones; anyhow, at
the end of the first half, North led by 15 to 4. After
the spell Glebe woke up, and let loose all its mass
of brute strength. It was too late; and, although
they piled up points fast, the butchers could not pile
them up fast enough. Time ran out with the Hill-
men ahead by 20 to 17. The most dashing team of last
year, Eastern Suburbs, manages to get beaten now
with monotonous regularity, and Saturday proved no
exception. This particular dishing was served up by
South Sydney, before 10,000 barrackers, mostly
South’s jackals; scores 12 to 8. All the Eastern
points came from the toe of Messenger’s boot; and
had he been in his usual kicking humor, the result
might have been different. The possession of Mess-
enger has just about ruined East Sydney. The most
conspicuous thing about South Sydney appears to be
its propensity for off-side play. The referee, time
after time, had to penalise the crowd for this rather
mean dodge. Balmain has at last managed to lift
its nose from the mud and score its initial win.
Western Suburbs was the victim, and its defeat (18
to 10) gave great joy to the 3000 Balmainiac bar-
rackers who were present.
The smashing of Newtown and Glebe puts
South Sydney at the top of the competition list with
a margin of three points. The procession follows
with Glebe 10, Newtown 9, Eastern Suburbs 8, An-
nandale and North Sydney 5, and Western Suburbs
and Balmain 2 each.
Union Rugby’s following in Sydney on Sat-
urday centred its attention on the Inter-’Varsity
match at Camperdown, where the local students met a
team from Maoriland. The Maorilanders showed their
superiority at once, and scored the first try within a
few minutes. The home team pulled itself together
and let loose a series of rather fine passing charges.
But they failed to materialise, and the Maorilanders
followed up each repulse with a furious counter-
charge; so the game became spectacular. In the
second spell there was a business-like method about
the visitors’ attack that broke up the local defence,
and when the whistle blew the Maorilanders had
scored five unconverted tries to the two converted
tries of the Sydnev team, and thus won by 15 points
to 10.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby League last week rode over
one of the recalcitrant clubs which threatened to re-
quire an outsize in hats. South Sydney, which is
leading in the premiership, objected to splitting the
gate-money with the peds. in the professional blow-
out on the coming Saturday, and asked for an alter-
ation or —no game! The League shouldered its
spare gun and told South Sydney that failure to turn
up would mean disqualification of club and officers.
The club said: “Don’t shoot —I’ll come down!”
Maoriland Rugby Leaguers hopped off the
train at Newcastle to tackle what they regarded as
a “good thing.” Later on they left with a bare vic-
tory—2l points to 20—and a more respectful idea
of football among the coal-men.
Though the conditions, including the
“gate,” were ideal for hoofball, Melbourne’s chief
League struggle on Saturday, between South and
Carlton, panned out a good deal short of 24-carat.
Nevertheless, a furious, hard-fought contest raged,
and no man was sure of victory till almost on the
close. Both teams depend chiefly on the spasmodic,
irresistible dashes of their large, meaty men. These
plunged and charged around enthusiastically; and, as
neither side could get a controlling lead of the other,
the. 35,000 spectators indulged in a perfect wallow of
excitement. But in the last 10 minutes Skinner s
hirelings tightened their belts, and the two resultant
goals, with the 3-point lead they already held, gave
the answer they wanted. Scores: South, 80 points;
Carlton, 65. the ’Wood-’Roy riot chiefly served to
stress the myopia of the police, who are understood
to have orders to treat assaults on the field just as
they would in the street. When the bell rang, ’Roy,
with 72 points to ’Wood’s 61, was quite pleased with
' itself, but as the umpire talked of reporting several
’bailers, it may be less pleased later on. The erst-all-
conquering EsSendon narrowly escaped its third suc-
cessive defeat —at the feet of Richmond—winning by
only 10 points (57 to 47) ; and it had a lot of —er —
luck in getting even them. Melbourne kept a good
hold of Geelong, and put on an extra two goals at
the finish, landing with 67 points to Sleepy’s 55 ; and
St. Kilda swept the ground with the Students 101 to
41.
The Ass. games, the first of the series of “returns,”
proved merely replicas of the earlier ones, not
a loser being able to turn the tables .on its former
conqueror. Brunswick made a valiant effort to
get even with Port, and came within three points of
doing so —42 to 45—but the rest of the “hopeless
teams seem content to remain so. North, which was
bumping Preston about, had the cheerful experience
of learning at half-time that it was playing a man
extra. He was, of course, retired, and North s points
were wiped off the board. But that made little dif-
ference, as even then it finished with 69 to Preston s
48 • P’ran acquired 68 to Brighton’s 50; Essendon
100 to ’Cote’s 47, and ’Scray 62 to ’Town’s 35.
As matters sit, South and Essendon head the League
list with 81 games each; Carlton, ’Wood and ’Roy
follow with 7 each; Geelong owns 64, Melbourne 41,
Richmond 4, St. Kilda 2, and ’Varsity one lonely
unit, and none but the five first-named can now reach
the semi-finals. In the Ass. Essendon is unbeaten,
with in wins: P’ran has 8. North and Port 7 each,
and ’Wick 6, and four of these five must ultimately
argue the possession of the pennant, unless somebody
capsizes their logic with a heavy purse.
Through the week the South Melbourne beaks suoke
to certain unruly barrackers for Skinner’s hirelings,
desired them to hand His Majesty as much as 20
crowns apiece, and intimated that the next offender
would be ordered to assist George Rex personally with
a small hammer and a large heap of stones. #
The seven Adelaide district clubs have fin-
ished their first round; West Adelaide has not lost a
game, and South has not won one, and the others
range in a neat sequence between those extremes.
West and Port were both unbeaten till the close of
the round, when they met at last, and Port went
down badly. Norwood, which generally stirs those
two up, lost its first four matches, and has only just
started to pull up. # *
Since they formed a union, Adelaide press-
men have begun to grow a spine. On the local foot-
ball grounds no shelter or other accommodation is
provided for the men who report the matches; and
unless that state of things is altered promptly there
is going to be a blank where the enthusiasts expect
to find a thrilling account of how Brown scored 7$
off his own toe. #
During a football match at Toowoomba
(Q.), the other day, between the King’s School (Parra-
matta, N.S.W.) and the Toowoomba Grammar School,
a remarkable goal was kicked by Ross, the King s
School full-back. The ball struck the left upright
some distance above the cross-bar, rebounded to the
cross-bar, then struck the other upright, then fell
back again on the cross-bar, and finally dropped over.
Collin Bell (N.S.W.) versus Pat Doran
(Melb.) was an interesting recent fixture at Mel-
bourne Athletic Club’s Hall. The lads are in the
heavy brigade, Bell (14st. 31b.) having the ad-
vantage of Doran to the extent of 101 b. avoirdupois,
and he seemed strong enough to juggle with two
people like Patrick. The first round was devoted
to cautious inquiry; then, _ satisfied that he had
an easy grip, Bell smote his opponent in various
ways and places, and in the third Doran was down
inspecting the flooring boards for Bsec. This only
served to excite his curiosity respecting the timber,
and in round four he was in a recumbent attitude
most of the three minutes. Bell might have ended
the contention there and then, had he not flared like
an ignited vesta, losing his head in the process, and
flurrying in a demented way, with the result that
Doran began to play the saving game, and was there
after 20, having swiped Bell some nasty knocks m the
meantime. Bell, who was declared the winner on
points, will make a fair to medium fighter when he
wears a permanent ice-pack on his fevered brow.
* * * *
Pug Johnson, though said to be abso-
lutely broke, cut a dash at the Coronation, easily
eclipsing Jeffries. In fact, Jeffries is an even
worse Coronator than fighter, and his wife complains
that he did nothing but sleep in an obscure London
pub, while Johnson wore 16 suits a day and smiled
his way through the city in borrowed motor-cars.
Poor Mrs. Jeffries consequently had to see the sights
of the city with Pat Malone. So low is the negro’s
coin, that what is practically a charity match is
being arranged between him and Petty-officer Curran
at Dublin in August while the big horse-show is on.
* * * *
The booming success of the Mclnstoush
has roused the ambitions of others, and a co. has
been formed to run a stadium in the Sports Ground
at Sydney during the summer months. Its real snag
will be to procure fighters of standard quality or
international fame, if what we heard from London
lately is true. According to that report practically
every fighter worth seeing in England, America and
Prance has been tied up under agreement with the
Mclnstoush. Between a concern whose circuit takes
in Paris, London and Australia, and one whose sole
venue is the Sports Ground, Sydney, there can be no
serious competition for crack bruisers.
* * * *
Arnst and Pearce have now lodged all but
the final deposit of £2OO each, and that won’t fall
due till Friday, July 28, the day before the race.
Three ferry boats will follow the event, and, judging
by the interest already manifested, seats will be at a
premium. Followers of Pearce have awakened to
the fact that he has dished his opponents in eight
successive contests. No other Australian aspirant
for the world’s championship has, they declare, ever
done it. Therefore, he must be a world-beater. The
fact conveys nothing unless we knew by how much
Arnst could also have beaten them.
Victorian motor-boat men indulged in their
first speed championship on Saturday afternoon. The
course was from St. Kilda pier out towards the Gel-
librand, then back along th.e South Melbourne fore-
shore to St. Kilda. G. W. Whatmore and his Chal-
lenger, late of Sydney, and W. C. Oxley’s Viper were
the starters. The race was the most exciting ever
witnessed in the south. Viper got the best of the
start, but the Challenger recovered the loss, and the
two raced neck and neck nearly all the way. Near
home the Viper went out, and won by a mere three
seconds. Viper is built from an American design,
and is anything but a beauty, but with the aid of a
20-h.p. engine just managed to get there. There
are rumors of some new racers next year, and Vic-
toria has hopes of being able to challenge Sydney’s
supremacy. #
E. V. Schneider, the Austral amateur
track champion, made a second appearance at the
Festival of Empire at the Crystal Palace, London.
He showed good form, excellent judgment, and every-
thing that a cyclist could wish to show but the
ability to win some of the plated ware. Still, he
downed Leo Meredith himself in a qualifying heat,
and that’s something to write home about. Schnei-
der is a promising rider.
The Goulburn-to-Sydney cycle race, annu-
ally promoted by the Dunlop Rubber Co., for the
purpose of selecting a N.S.W. representative for the
“Warrnambool,” will take place on September 23.
As usual, it will be under the supervision of the
League of N.S.W. Wheelmen. The Saturday follow-
ing will see the Australian Cycle Traders’ 100-miles
race, and two weeks after that the “Warrnambool”
itself will happen. # # *
Another record goes by the board; an
English motor-bikist, A. E. Cutt, has cut out 2557
miles on the road in six consecutive days, in the face
of a lot of wet weather. His first day’s spin saw
461 miles reeled off, 412 the second, 428 the third,
407 the fourth, 423 the fifth, and 426 the sixth.
Considering that it is 657 miles better than the pre-
vious best, the chances are that it is not too beastly
slow.
* * * *
F. D. Walcott, one of N.S.W.’s crack road-
riders, announces his intention of making an early
attempt on that £2O and £5 ss. medal which the
Dunlop Co. is offering to the cyclist who can beat
Leon Meredith’s out and home unpaced record. To
do better than that slippery 4hr. 52min. 51 4-ssec., a
man wants all the conditions favorable, and Walcott
isn’t likely to find them so at present.
■* * A' *
N.S.W. Lawn Tennis Association failed
for the third time, last week, to get the three to
one majority necessary in order to open the Double
Bay courts for practice on Sundays; a minority of
102 squelched the 203 who voted in favor of altering
the Constitution. Later in the week the debenture-
holders dumped a demand on the door-mat for in-
terest ; and as the demand cannot be met, there is
only the alternative to burst up the co. When that
is done a better Constitution may arise out of the
ashes. #
The billiard match between Fred Lindrum
(Aus.) and Tom Reece (Eng.), 16,000 up, dragged
itself to an end on Saturday night. The Australian
pipped Reece on the post, and won by a narrow 861,
as against his Melbourne win of 5960. When Lin-
drum ran out, Reece’s little lot was 15,139, and at
no other time in the match were the two so far apart.
Up to the last session they were so close as to raise
the suspicion that they were nursing one another. Lin-
drum let Sydney off lightly with his breaks off the
red, his biggest being 438, made with 146 consecutive
losing hazards. #
“Jobber” : The game’s to a bubble. Reece,
the billiardist, is our latest visitor, and he is
going to settle in Australia. Just like Tahmmy
Burns and a dozen others. They make me tired,
these sporty blokes. Do they take us for sand-
wiches, or melons, or what?
Sydney baseball on Saturday didn’t alter
the position of clubs on the premiership list, al-
though there were many close finishes. Lane Cove
just defeated Mosman-N. Sydney by 9 runs to 8;
Waverley crawled out ahead of Paddington by 6 runs
to 5; Leichhardt accounted for Burwood by 10 to
6; and University walloped Petersham by 10 runs to
3. The dust-up leaves Lane Cove at the head of the
premiership with 12 points, Waverley and Leich-
hardt next with 10 points, University with 8, and
the balance very much in the soup. #
After much yabbering, Christchurch
(M.L.) has been selected as the spot on which Aus-
tralasia will defend its right to the Davis Cup
against the winner of the America-England prelim-
inary tie, towards the end of the year. Champion
A F. Wilding, who hails from this speck of the
Rickety Isles, will, therefore, get a big cheer when
he gives another Australian a hand in the third at-
tempt to keep the utensil out of the foreign pantry.

Australia has been harboring still another
baronet unawares, though it might have read
his name and style in the Directory for years
past. He was Sir Charles Edward Gordon,
seventh baronet; the title dates from 1706.
There doesn’t appear to have been any rush
of Gordons to secure the barren title, for
the old gentleman who has held it lately,
but died in North Sydney last week, was no
more than the second cousin of the sixth
baronet. The new baronet is the old gentle-
man’s son; and he has a son upon whom
the glory of being Baronet of Earlston may
fall some time or other.
The murder of a European stewardess on
the P. and O. Co.’s China by a Lascar is the
latest score to be chalked up against the
cheap Asiatic seadog. Next time the victim
may be a first-class passenger. A couple of
weeks ago, the steamer Kerries called in at
Fremantle (W.A.), and the officers ex-
plained how they had to put back 800 miles
to Mauritius to get rid of the mutinous
Chinese crew. If the colored person keeps
on at this rate, shipowners will be compelled
to experiment with trained baboons. From
the cheap shipowner’s standpoint, almost
anything is preferable to employing white
men at white men’s wages.

The World’s Standard
Valveless Engine.
THE life history of a FERRO ENGINE wouill be
vary interesting were we to follow it from its
embryonic stage on the blue prints of the
designers, through the various phases of infantile
life, till It emerges from the factory to be transported
to some particular spot on old mother earth, to fulfil
its destiny as the WILLING SLAVE OF INDUSTRY.
From icy pole to torrid zone, from far-off Alaska

Special Important Notice to Owners of Motor Cars.
We desire to intimate to all users of MOTOR-OAR RUBBER TYRES, that,
acting on behalf of one of the leading English Amalgamated Companies, who
require a considerable quantity of Old Car Covers and Tubes, we have been
instructed to make a Liberal Allowance for all kinds of USED MOTOR-CAR
COVERS and TUBES. , , .
Our principals, owing to certain prior arrangements, have instructed us tor the
present to confine this privilege to purchasers of CONTINENTAL TYRES.
All CONTINENTAL TYRES will be supplied at Current List Rates, and
coupled with reduction in force from 26th June. This consideration we can offer
now will show a considerable saving in your Tyre Bill. . .
Our instructions are to supply CONTINENTAL TYRES OR TUBES, and to
make Allowance for your OLD TYRES OF ANY MAKE. All Tyres supplied are
guaranteed by BENNETT AND WOOD, LTD., as New Stock, and carry the usual
war ran tv and guarantee of the local Continental Tyre Co.
COUNTRY MOTORISTS. Our Local Depots and Traders throughout
N.S.W. have been instructed to offer same facilities to Country Motorists. If send-
ing direct to us Prepay Freight on Old Tyres.
EVERYTHING FOR THE MOTOR. All Makes of Tyres at List Prices.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany. •

Slogging in from six to six,
No wonder that I feel so sick;
Shelter none, no air to breathe, the sun so hot,
It’s no wonder that men go off their pot.
I have worked ten hours for 4s. 2d. pay
And cockie says it’s only one day ;
But wait a while, we will teach him to say
“Five bob and keep, with eight hours a day.”

SCENE IN LITTLE BOURKE ST.
In their lottery shops in narrow Little Bourke
The Orientals carried on a roaring trade,
When to their dismay one night
While their lamps were burning Bright
The officers of Justice made a raid ;
The spectators round the dens enjoyed the fun ;
There was confusion and a jabbering Chinese Din,
But the hardened men in blue
Did their duty firm and true,
They dumped ’em into cabs and run ’em in.
Wild excitement prevailed in Little Bourke,
When ticket markers endeavored to escape;
When the vast crowd did disperse
Hidden Chows did rave and curse,
And firmly barred their doors when all was safe.
....H.W. (V.): Not sufficient interest... .J.J.J.:
Sorry no points about it.. . .P. S. Alfred: Not a very
bright rhyme.... Texas: Dishing-up hasn’t been skil-
fully done... .A.E.V.: Postal experiences are fairly
harrowing, but we haven’t the space. Try another
paper.... Jimmie C.: It conveys a clear impression
of a lonely life, and we hereby add it to our series of
Plain Language Poems: —
PIONEERING.
There’s a ploughman down south in W.A.
Who has a lonely out-back job ;
His camp is curbed by “boodies” ;*
For his tent is on a knob.
He has a chat just once a week
When his boss brings his scran,
But the rest of the time he is on his pat,
And he never sees a man.
He starts to work at daybreak
To steer his plough all day, ' ,
And cooks his supper at 8 p.m.
So I guess he has earnt his pay;
He feeds his team at half-past ten,
Once more at half-past four;
And he is hard at work at sunrise
For a lonesome day once more.
*The “boodies” referred to are the W.A. “boodie
rat” or kangaroo rat of N.S.W C.J.O.: No snao.
... G.E.S.: Might be of value to some comedian. Not

Yes. very well I love her,
I’ll ask her to be mine,

When I call round at half-past nine.

Yes, very well I love her,
I tell her as I lean
In ecstasy above her
Each summer night at nine-eighteen.

James Gordon, Adelaide’s police-magis-
trate, and brother of the literary Judge, re-
cently asked an accused person if he had any
defence. “I’m charged with larceny, y’r
Worship,” said the thief, “but it was simply
a case of kleptomania.” “Well,” said Gor-
don, “that’s within my jurisdiction. One
month.”

Remember. Utterly Intolerable.
Seemed Beyond Medical Power to

by Cuticura Soap and Ointment.”
“I take great pleasure in praising the
wonderful Cuticura Cures. Ever since I can
remember I was a terrible sufferer of eczema,
and other irritating skin diseases. I would
lie awake all night, and my suffering was
intolerable. A scaly humor settled on my
back, and being but a child, X naturally
scratched it. It was a burning, itching sensa-
tion, and utterly intolerable, in fact, it was
so that I could not possibly forget about it.
It did not take long before it spread to my
shoulders and arms, and I was almost covered
with a mass of raw flesh on account of my
scratching it. I was in such a condition that
my hands were tied.
“A number of physicians were called, but
it seemed beyond their medical power and
knowledge io cure me. Xlaving tried numer-
ous treatments without deriving any benefit
from them, I had given myself up to the
mercy of my dreadful malady, but I thought
1 would take the Cuticura treatment as a last
resort. Words cannot express my gratitude
to the one who created ‘The Cuticura Mira-
cles,’ as I have named them, for thank
heaven there was such a miracle as the Cuti-
cura Remedies sent to the suffering world,
and now I feel as if I never suffered from
even a pimple. My disease was routed by
Cuticura Soap and Ointment, and I shall
never cease praising the wonderful merits
they contain. I will never be without them,
in fact, I can almost dare any skin diseases
to attack me so long as I have Cuticura
Remedies in the house. I hope that this
letter will give other sufferers an idea of how
I suffered.” (Signed) C. L. Green, 929 Chest-
nut St., Philadelphia, U. S. A., Aug. 29, 1910.

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W V H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resprt. The Crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors;
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office—or your own residence—direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Caves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Castlereagh Street.
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

834
Business, Robbery, etc.
An odd but important case has been tried in Ade-
laide police court, a driver for a big carrying firm
being charged under the Masters and Servants Act
with simply refusing —not rudely, but firmly and
absolutely^—to handle “scab” fruit (picked and sent
down river by non-union men) from Renmark.
There was no ill-feeling between the man and the
boss, but the Drivers’ Union had ordered that the
stuff should not be helped towards a sale, and the
man was loyal to the order. The S.M. said “con-
scientious grounds” would not go in that court. So
it was 10s. and costs—£4 3s. in all.
* * * *
The scheme to go in for whale-hunting on a large
scale in M.L.waters, which was recently announced by
a Norwegian co., is meeting with some protest—-which
appears to have presented itself too late'. Already
the Marine Department has granted concessions to
the co. to make use of ports in the Auckland Islands,
Stewart Island, the Chathams, and the Bay of Islands,
and whaling stations have been mapped out. The
objection that has been raised is that the whaling
industry has disastrous effects upon the ordinary
fishing" industry, the theory being that the whales
are marine sheep-dogs which drive the mobs of
aquatic jumbucks shorewards. But, as there is no
considerable fishing industry to be injured in Maori-
land, and as the Norwegian whalers will fly the
M.L. flag, the co. is generally being wished “good
hunting.”
