“Gallati”: Da soft dreenk shop it ees no good.
It is alaright taka the day, but taka the night it ees
over da odd. Saturday night da beeg bloke roosh
into da shop and yell like da steam wheesle, “Bringa
da sod-da-brand queek.” “Notta da hope,” I say;
“I hava da sod straight, but da brand 1 have none.
I have da meelk-and-sod.” Then the beeg bloke
rolla da eye lika da sheep at sea, and fall heel over
head lika da dead barracoot. I roosh over to da
pub quieka da lively for da brand. By Orees! I
poura da bob’s worth down da beeg bloke’s goot.
lie risa up from da dead and say, “It is alaright.
The meelk-and-sod it is no gooda to Gund,” and he
go to hoppa da skip. I maka da noise for da bob.
“Alaright,” he say, “thees is da sly-grog shop.”
By Crees! I let him a-go queek. By-’n’-bye I maka
da find he robba da teel, when 1 roosh for da brand,
so I put upa da shut. It ees no gooda da soft dreenk
shop.
* * * *
“The Snake”: Of course there are no combines in
Australasia, and the recent collapse of the Whole-
sale Club, Ltd., of Christchurch (M.L.), proves it.
The business was started as a co-operative venture,
in the interests of the public. Goods were sold on
a very small margin of profit for about a fortnight
and the outlook was promising, when the ware-
houses took a hand and refused supplies even for hard
cash. So the directors of the boycotted firm bought
a warehouse as a going concern, and more capital
, was sought from the public on a published pros-
pectus. Just there a newspaper got busy, and the
shares, which had been rushed on the first day,
went begging on the second. Nevertheless, the
directors subscribed the money themselves, and re-
moved into larger premises. Everything was serene
until the first shipment of goods from the manufac-
turers came to hand, when the banks squatted on
top of them and refused to get off. The rest of
the story was told by the managing director of the
club after the towel had'been thrown in: “To com-
bat these tactics Mr. Alcorn and I took upon our-
selves, in consideration of the interests of all, to
guarantee the club to the extent of £6OOO or more.
We have put in our money to the last penny, and
we have lost practically everything. The whole
thing has been a short but strenuous fight between
the organised trade of the city and the Wholesale
Club.” That’s the case for the club, and when the
mail left the other side hadn’t even mentioned its
address.
# * * *
“A. Kito”: A Bulletin par. (May 25) gave rates
of pay of Irish linen workers. Under the influence
of a strong greed, developed through intercourse
with a Tory uncle who has an importing warehouse
and a hatred of local manufactures, I resolved to
get some handkerchiefs at the wholesale price. I
bought two dozen, and paid Is. each for them.
Twenty-four shillings for wipes that would not exceed
an area covered by four Bulletin pages! The linen
worker got about 2d., and worked a day on them.
Retail, the handkerchiefs would be at least a couple
of shillings. There is a leakage somewhere. I
think it is the blood of the Irish.
ACCOUNTICIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 used to hate the sight of Shears,
And hurry past his shop ;
I feared he might, with bitter tears,
Hush out and bid me stop.
I hated him with all my soul;
It almost made me ill
To think —it is a reason droll —
I owed the brute a bill.
But I am frightened when I owe;
For Credit means to me
The well-soaped road down which you go
To blank Insolvency.
But Shears, whene’er by fortune ill
We met where traffic flows,
Used never whine about his bill,
But talk of further clothes.
“Well, what about another suit?”
The smiling beast would say;
It caused me agony acute—
I used to edge away.
“Come round, if you have time to spare,
And see the latest stuff.”
(I longed to catch him by the hair,
And loudly shout “Enough I”)
I never did. Upon his head
No vengeance did I wreak,
But muttered as 1 turned and fled,
“Too busy now—next week!”
Then Shears would gladly go his way,
And all the talk forget,
Leaving to me a sunless day
With dismal thoughts of debt.
Last week, by all the- gods! a horse
I’d backed for many quids
Rushed safely home, and so, of course,
I skinned the laying Yids.
Straight into Shears’ I rushed and paid
That nightmare of a bill;
Shears grumbled something as he laid
The banknotes in his till.
He lingered over the receipt;
His pen he had to pull
When writing at the column’s feet
The legend, “Paid in full.”
He did not heed my chatter bright,
And scowled when he had done;
My singing heart felt very light—
My freedom I had won.
But as he slammed his Doomsday Book,
My heart in twain was torn,
To see upon his face a look
Of loathing, mixed with scorn.
Then through my intellect immense,
This thought went creeping slow:
A tailor loses confidence
If you should cease to owe.
He honors most the well-dressed men
Who gracefully contrive
To pay a little now and then
And keep the bill alive.
The solvent wretch who goes and pays
The whole of the. amount,
Shears looks upon as one who slays
The tender young account.
N.s.w.
THANK HEAVEN THAT’S PAID.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientific de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no mat-
ter how old you are. There is no mystery
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully and
explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and
in the same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an- aid to weak hearing. But,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Ear-Drums are invisible and comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the
strain off them—-the strain of trying to hear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
■ So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it lies in the ear. You will
notice that The Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
o\<y
t?<9
W
o -
A <0
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to the natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
and tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may
wear the Ear-Drums day and night, and take
them out whenever you wish. Children can
wear them (we have special sizes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bought you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do not send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
FOR the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”

BANE, TOWNSVILLE, & TOOWOOMBA.
IN BANANA LAND
A king is crowned and a king is dead.
The dead one, aged 100 or so, was Jerry
Straightfinger, king of the Burnett blacks.
He was the bravest nigger of them all.
Years ago, he was giving an exhibition of
spear and boomerang throwing before a
crowd of spectators, fie skied a boomerang,
which, instead of returning, circled and
made straight for the fear-stricken mob.
Jerry rushed forward, shot out his hand and
grabbed it. He carried to his grave as me-
mentoes of that occasion a sobriquet and a
broken finger.
“Rob”: When Mount Morgan began to use
its Many Peak fluxes, people in the line of
prevailing winds got a sniff of fumes such as
the Oldest Inhab. couldn’t remember. For
miles along the ranges great forest giants have
smelt the same smell and given up the ghost
rather than smell any more. One morning’s
fumes will settle your flower garden and your
pot plants. And the water—it is like poison
in the tanks, undrinkable after one hour’s
fumigating from the big stack. Make tea of
it, and the milk will curdle and hang in clots
near the surface, like submerged rafts.
From a man who has been in the busi-
ness :
Home Secretary Appel is introducing various com-
forts into the cells of prisoners guilty of extra-good
conduct in St. Helena. Tables, chairs, books, pic-
tures and vases of flowers are to be supplied with a
view “to refining and elevating,” etc. Without de-
tracting from Minister Appel’s admirable intentions,
long observation shows that such perquisites, unfortu-
nately, nearly always fall to the cunning, exper-
ienced gaol bird, who lays himself out to win every
possible privilege by every possible hypocrisy. The
best behaved men in prison are seldom the novices
in crime. On the contrary, the first-sentence men
are often sullen and rebellious. But the downy old
veterans are alert and cunning as lynxes. By the
way, Harry Thaw, who shot Stanford White in
America, had a suite of cells. Perhaps Bananaland
may yet go in for the Thaw apartment system—
suites to the sweet.
Rockhampton seems to breed people with
an eye for cattle-flesh. In the usual weight-
guessing competition inseparable from local
shows, 19 persons correctly estimated the
weight of the exhibition bullock (8901 b.), 12
more guessed to within lib., and 50 more to
within 51b. of steak.
Governor Macgregor, at the opening of the
Salv’army’s People Palace in Brisbane, said
the Army’s self-denial fund for 1910 was
£70,000. It was simply miraculous. The
Army succeeded by its “sincerity and practi-
cal aspect, more especially on account of its
religious foundations.” Presumably this is
medical Scotch for “unblushing cadge.”
“Quan”: Some time ago The Bulletin
remarked that the sectarian devil has a hard
row to hoe in western Bananaland. He
has. Among those at a reception given at
Cunnamulla to Roman Catholic Bishop
Duhig, of Rockhampton, were the Anglican
and Methodist parsons. Twelve months
among the mulga and the saltbush would
make a different man of Dill Macky.
HE WAS A VEGETARIAN.
PHILANTHROPIST: “ Er—I sent a poor starving devil down to yon with a note this morn-
ing to tell you to give him a meal. What’s the bill?”
BUNG: “ Eighteen pence."
PHILANTHROPIST: “ What are the items?”
BUNG: “ Four beers and two cigars.”
The Denham party has decided, after all,
to nominate W. D. Armstrong, who was
Deputy Speaker during deceased “Joey”
Bell’s illness last session, as Speaker of the
Bananaland Assembly. So the hopes of a
prominent independent are shattered.
Robert Philp re his proposed retire-
ment :
He would never accept office again, and it would
be unfair to Townsville to offer himself again, as
he might deprive the city of the opportunity of
Ministerial representation.
Philp’s objection is not to office in itself,
but to office under Denham, the loyal Philp-
ite who knifed his leader in 1903. At the
same time, the ex-Premier knows perfectly
well that his political cake is dough in
Townsville. He has already a Labor col-
league, and the recent Federal referendum
vote showed Labor to possess a 300 majority
in the Philpian constituency. The time for
retirement has plainly come.
Mrs. Nash, wife of James Nash, the dis-
coverer of Gympie, was a witness in a
Gympie will case heard in the Brisbane
Supreme Court the other day. Nash dis-
covered gold at Gympie on October 20, 1867.
The place was then called Jimpy, or Gympy;
but for some time the township was known
as Nashville —just as at one time it _ was
proposed to call Broken Hill Mullinsville.
John Graeme Balsillie, who has just been
appointed first Commonwealth wireless ex-
pert, is a young Queenslander who has con-
trived to cram quite a deal of wandering
into his 27 years of existence. A Brisbane
Normal and Grammar School boy, he early
made the discovery that the surest way to
get appreciated in Australia is to live
abroad. So he drifted first to the Cold Land
and then to Russia and China, mostly on
wireless work, until, after patenting a sys-
tem of his own, Andrew Fisher thought him
good enough to have the chance of earning
a living at home.
Died at Gympie other day, Lieut.-Col.
Patterson, one of the first officers of the
Queensland Mounted Infantry—the crowd
that fell into sad disrepute during the strike
that was nearly a revolution.
Pathetic patch from the Walsh and Tin-
aroo Miner :
Permit me to draw attention to the following
facts. There is only one cart plying for hire here,
and it is used as a hearse, for carting refuse, in-
cluding dead dogs and goats, from the town. It is
also used for conveying meat from Irvinebank to the
butcher’s shop. I ask you for decency’s sake to
give this disgraceful matter publicity.
Truly a comprehensive vehicle! Dead dogs
and goats, dead bullocks and dead humans
—it refuses none of them.
“Bromide,” on cane-cutting records: —
“Stan” (B. 15/6/’ll) nominates Charlie Steve, of
Ingham, as a champion because he cut 16 tons of
trashed cane in nine hours. Supposing Steve did it,
there is nothing to write home about if the crop
was a favorable one. I’ll guarantee to produce
scores of cutters who will, in a crop of 25 to 35 tons
per acre of trashed cane, do as much and think no-
thing of it. Mere figures in such a case are not worth
their ink. What counts is the condition of the crop.
What tonnage per acre? Was it straight cane?
Short and heavy, or long whipstick cane? “Trashed
cane” means that all the dead leaves, shoots, etc.,
have been cleared away, and it is the presence of
these things in “green cane” that puts the brake
on the cutter. I suppose 95 per cent, of the crops
are cut in the “green” ; it is only when there is too
much “trash” that the cocky will stand running a
fire through it to clear it out. By all means give the
champion sheep-barber a rest, and let us hear a bit
about the white slaves of the sugar fields; but let it
be about cutting and loading green cane, and let us
know the tonnage to the acre, whether straight or
twisted, long or short, and all the other things which
have to be considered when pricing cane. As the
day’s work of the cutter includes both cutting and
loading, no entries for the championship should be
accepted from men who have merely cut and not
loaded. And, more important than all, work must
be limited to eight hours; jackass to jackass cutters
should be strictly barred.
The Man of Beezness.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Friends, Romans, countrymen, lend me your ears—
I come to blame not Judas, but to praise him:
A good, keen beezness man, of habits steady;
A calculator keen of odds and chances;
A trusted, trusty carl to carry bags,
And meet the dues that fell on poor disciples
Was this same Judas!
’Tis true he sinned somewhat in one transaction;
’Tis true they tell a tale of Thirty Pieces
He gained by one unhappy, so-called bargain.
But then remark his Aim !
He knew the power of Him, the Mighty Master,
Could stem the hostile tide and dodge disaster,
And so the thing was safe —as safe as houses—
And would, moreover, as he noted clearly,
Pour coin, good coin, coin that was badly needed,
Into the bags of Judas.
Why deal in blame unduly? Was he not
A fair forerunner of the bands of Beezness?
Did he not wisely buy when things were low
And sell when things were high ?
Did he not offer in the open market
The goods most in demand?
And if it pleased the High Omniscient
That One should perish on the Cross untimely,
Who then was Judas that he should oppose
And thwart the plan eternal?
And if the Thirty Pieces in'his palm
Burned like the fabled sixpences that lie
Hot on the hobs of Hades,
’Twas but a transient weakness of the flesh
Demanding Christian pity!
Why then damn Judas?
There lie in ev’ry Gentile cemetery
Scores upon scores of one-time portly brethren
Who cornered Egypt’s grain and robbed her fleshpots.
Who sold the weak and stole the bread of children,
Who flayed the needy, ground the poor to powder,
Whose doings daily crucified the Master;
And these repose ’neath many a massy tombstone
Scored with mendacious mem’ries of the virtues
The dead men never knew.
One blemish only hath this tale of Judas,
Precursor of our modern Bag and Bourse men —
He died, his neck within a noose; but now in
Beezness
Ye lay the noose on necks of other people
But never on your own; in which respect
He fajled his tribe, this much abused Judas,
And died, I fear, recanting. Of your goodness
Pray for his soul, ye brethren in the Beezness,
And for his final frailty forgive him.
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER FLAM.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 1, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
About 3500 of the Naicest and sub-Naicest
called in last Saturday to the Mayoral Did-
dams s reception, given in grateful celebra-
tion of the bonneting of George. The
Mayoress received in a becoming grey frock,
set off with bright-edged smiles, in a bower
of palms. The Exes, from Government
Mouse were among those who helped to wear
the lady’s glove to shreds. The affair was
the biggest and likewise the most successful
ever held here, and all the “proper” people
showed up—which isn’t the rule at Brisbane
Mayoral shows.
Mrs. Jessie Lambert Compigne, who died
in Brisbane the other day, walked hand in
hand with a lot of Queensland history during
her 76 years of life. As a child she ar-
rived from N. S. Wales with her father
(Captain Collins) and mother, and resided at
Telemon Station, Upper Logan, in the days
when black brother was a most infuriated
person, for sufficiently good reason. Never-
theless, the then young girl-pioneer fre-
quently rode the 50 miles to Brisbane and
back without an escort. She married the
late A. W- Compigne, one of our original
M’s.L.C., in ’53, and was present when Sir
George Bowen read the proclamation mak-
us a separate colony. Later, the bluff old
Gov. was her guest at Nindooimbah Station.
She remembered Brisbane when it had no
bridge, and when convicts worked in the
streets. A kindly, cheerful soul, her friends
were many, and her enemies none at all.
Another case of leprosy is reported from
that jumping-off place of the aged and halt,
Dunwich. This time it is the young of
an official, and he makes the fifth victim to
date. No explanation of the persistency of
the horror thereabouts is forthcoming, but I
am not singular in the belief that the adja-
cent leper station in Moreton Bay should be
removed with great speed and determination.
Perhaps the festive mosquito is acting as a
special experimenter in inoculation, and if
that is so, the winged agent has only
miles to go to deliver the dreadful goods.
Minister for Customs Tudor, with M.L.A.
George Barber, rushed through Brisbane last
Thursday to the North, to try to dissolve the
sugar difficulties. But at time of writing
the fatman was still sitting sullenly on the
bag, and declaring that it is impossible for
him to pay fair wages and enormous divi-
dends at the same time. And no reasonable
person can deny it.
The annual frivol of the Ninth A.I.R. was
marked by a number of antediluvian dances
that figured upon the ball programme on the
night Belgium’s capital had gathered there
her beauty and her aldermen. The officers of
the Ninth A.I.R. had a bad time manoeuvring
through the ancient figures, and I did hear
that the P.M.O had to be piloted by an ac-
tive section of the ambulance corps. One
verra Scawtch importation knew every
turn; but, then, Scotland is a place where
any old thing is carefully hoarded up for
possible use.
The consular Carters served tea and the
usual etceteras to a large throng at the
Nunnington mansion during the week, and
those who came to “scoff” remained all day.
Once it used to be the thing to sip a thimble-
cup of tea, nibble a cookie, and then flutter
to the carriage; but in these times the
bidden toy with six cups of tea (not small),
four scones, three cream cakes, four or six
sandwiches and a couple of fruit salads.
The clan Unmack was responsible for an-
other tea-party, and those who were not
satisfied were not there.
Our new Commandant and Mrs. Parnell
will trip from the mail train this evening.
A wail reaches me from Colombo, where
a timid young engagee, who flitted thither to
meet “him,” nearly had her life’s romance
shattered by a small local half-caste, who
persistently trotted beside the double rick-
shaw in which the couple were taking the
air, and claimed the gentleman as a long-lost
father. She declares that for ever Ceylon
will be associated in her mind with five
things : Tangled relationships, 1; odors of
pitch, cinnamon, kerosene, and hot nigger, a
dead-heat.
The Bulletin the other day said it
couldn’t get a roof-garden meal in Australia,
but Brisbane can at least supply it with a
mid-day lunch 80ft. above the pavement, in
very charming surroundings. The new
Finney-Isles’ “garden” is the favorite lun-
cheon resort of the moment; the only trouble
is that it is hermetically sealed, most days of
the week, at 5.30. But we are hoping to get
the 5 changed to a 9 in the future; and then
we’ll dine as well as lunch on the roof.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
There is yet hope for the North. That
famous old industry, the manufacture of
Mackay rum, is on the verge of a glorious
resurrection, and the tens of thousands of
gallons of molasses now running to waste
mav soon be converted into lovely tipple,
gorgeous visions, and serpents innumerable.
The census shows that it has taken
Queensland 10 years to incx-ease its popula-
tion by 100,000. At this rate of increase, it
will take 30 years for Bananaland to attain
its first million. Will Japan, a country
that supports nearly 50,000,000 souls on less
than one-fourth of the area of Queensland,
please be so kind as to wait a century or
two until the northern State has enough
men to make a decent resistance? Or will
vai’ious local people quit their present anti-
immigration attitude?

A CIGAR IS THE BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
CHI
aum.
ALL over Australasia the only places you can
buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities. In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better. He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when they ve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting “fu’ the noo.” Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some “back-yarder"—real “Flor de Cabbage”
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them—made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-falutin' name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story—l’ll tell you about it
another time.)
The storekeeper or tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker’s profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge 9d. for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be small; and
good Cigars have to be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they'll go off.
T 1 jse are just some of
the reasons why you can’t
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of Australia,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or
6 of them, and, if they’re
not as good as any 6d.
Cigar you’ve ever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That’s the way I’m in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. I know Cigar-
making from Ato Z. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigars of
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
rious smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you'll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, Java leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d Cigar, sold in the cities by high-class
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer—
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen’s, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge you 22/6 the box
of 100 DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.
Mv Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN

The buffering arid Pain Endured by Many Women
js Almost Beyond Relief.
THERE are thousands of Australian Women whose duties call tfieiji
out in all weathers, often to sit with vyet feet oj stand all day, thus
undermining their health. Many women, however, spend their
lives at home, and these are ambitious that their homes should be kept
neat and clean, their children well dressed and tidy, and they often do
their own house-work, and sewing for the entire family, Truly the work
of such women “is never done,” and is it any wonder that she breaks
down at the end of a few years —the back aches, there is a general
depression, and the struggle to continue her duties is pitiful ? The reason
is that the kidneys are not doing their duty. If they were, the blood
would be kept and would nourish the whole body ; but if the kidneys
are inactive the blood carries pain and disease throughout the system.
Dr. Sheldon’s Gin Pills are the great corrective for all Kidney ills, and in

Reverie
(On Norman Lindsay’s Women).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CtURVES that almost rift the veil, \vhose
i still enchantments seal
' Coldly-linear fairy maids from human
lovers’ 'zeal:
to the' hungry eyes of three-dimen-
sioned Miys, *•' '
Picture-cabined Aphrodite straining through
the bars!
Curves eaducean, that know how fond Pyg-
malion bare
Throbbing Galatea from marmoreal despair!
Curves the Creative compasses through
Chaos conquering swept!
(Yet surely hither, through that leer, salaci-
ous Anarchs crept!)
Succulence half waking from her winter to
refresh,
On the prairie of a page, the bisons of the
flesh !
Strokes whose swooning dances shape the
temptresses that fire
Sluggish-hearted Anthonies, yea, Buddhas to
desire,
Calling back our votive feet from cold
Diana’s trysts,
Heaping our ascetic boards with rosy Eu-
charists !
Gospels of form, to rescue man from solitary
sin!
(But what interpolating imp dehumanised
that grin?)
Pale our loves these dying days, and rare
the men they lead
Troy-ward, or to Joyous Garde or wan In-
ferno’s mead ;
Lilliputian marionettes assuage our tepid
throes,
Sex hath shrunken to a sin, and Beauty to a
pose !
Gross we call the bosoms of the super-
maidens, men
(Through a ruddy mist we know not) fought
and died for then !
Ah, Lindsay, you reveal them to the eunuchs
of the South!
(Yet, Oh! for God’s sake, shoo that fiend
from young Australia’s mouth !)
Nectar sipped from amaranth, where god-
desses recline,
Bee-distilled for human lips and hived within
a line !
Shapes that are a trumpet-call and groups
that are a choir,
Pulsing through ascetic souls, evangels of
Desire!
Hill-crest bosoms sloping ripe to girdling
lava-dales,
Limbs with all temptations taut that splen-
did Beauty veils!
The Golden Age, the Joy of Life, the gods of
Love are near!
(Yet would a healthy Pagan through those
tarnished pig-eyes peer?)
Lines iconoclastic, stripping weeds and frozen
flowers,
Monks had swathed her in so long, from
Aphrodite ours;
Hammering pale idols to their idiot mother
stones,
Smelting bogey thunderbolts and wrecking
tinsel thrones;
Rousing in our wilderness satyr, elf and
faun,
Sleeping dim and frozen there, unpastured
since the dawn;
Bacchantic lines a-calling to the rapture of
the Wild !
(But why the sneer of ages on the features
of a child?)
Necromancy stirring, in primordial deeps of
man.
Behemoth long-memoried, unchained Levia-
than ;
Loosing waters, crimson-lotused, brimming
passions old —
Helen rapt, Europa’s ride, the fertile shower
of gold,
Vestal Rhea, Sabine courtship, Viking booty
fain,
Leda by the genial pond, Cassandra’s struggle
vain !
Ah, she ! Then woe, dark woe the Vision
Beautiful may smear,
And Love, the sunny banyan tree, be rooted
in a leer!
VICTORIA.
A FAUN OF THRACE.
Silence is Golden.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THEY took out a warrant for the arrest of
Dido Connelly, as soon as they heard
from Willalong how the job had been
done. The safe had obviously been cracked
by two men, and of these Dido was as ob-
viously one. The detectives knew his touch.
He and Piggy Blamyre had been seen to-
gether in Melbourne three days before the
robbery at Willalong post office, and so, on
general principles, a warrant was taken out
for Piggy as well. At the time this war-
rant was issued Piggy was ivell on his way
to San Francisco. Dido, not so wise, was
snapped up by one of the plain-clothes police
in Bourke-street, two weeks after the rob-
bery.
Herein did Plain-clothes Constable Smith-
wick err. Instead of taking Dido to the
Detective Office, where the experts could
“put the acid” on him nicely and quietly,
Smithwick took him to the City Watchhouse,
jjtnd there put the formal charge against
iiipi. It is true that on the way up from
Bourke-street he attempted to pour a few
drops of acid himself, but he did it very
clumsily—shook the bottle, as it were—and
the net result of his questioning was that
Dido had never seen him before, did not san-
guinary well ever want to see him again, did
not know who, or where, Piggy was, and did
not scarlet well care. Dido reiterated the
information that he did not know who Piggy
W o S by the name he ought to be a rufous
plain-clothes John- In answer to further
ouestions he invited Constable Smithwick to
depart to a red and improper Gehenna.
Dido was very annoyed about his arrest.
There was somebody else, however* still
more annoyed about it than Dido When
the star detective of the Criminal Investiga-
tion Branch heard that Dido had been locked
on and the charge formally preferred, his
remarks to Constable Smithwick were so in-
sulting that had he beep a civilian he wopld
probably have been locked up too. But this
\yas the great McMasters, and Smithwick
was discreetly humble.. .
‘‘■What the hell, sir,” asked McMasters,
“did you arrest the man for? Do you think
you are paid Bs. a day foy spoiling our
dases?'”
“But” ^ — Ipegan Smithwick-
“Bah, you ass!”' shouted the fletectiye,
and the conversation languished.
Now, to those "who understand the rules
which govern the innermost workings of pie
c. 1., it plainly appears that Detective
McMasters did well" to be angry. is not
generally known how far upon the way to
the “Third Degree” our Australian detec-
tives may go with a suspect. They may,
and they do, take him into the “Gazette
Room” at the office, and there question him
from dawn to dusk, and sometimes back
again till dawn. They bully him, they coax
him, they frighten him; they tell him that
the other man has “coughed it all up,” that
they know all about it, that he had better
tell the truth, that the telling of the truth
is always placed by the Judge on the credit
side of the ledger, when the punishment
comes to be reckoned. By these arts, and
by cross-examination, if the suspect be a
fool who will answer their questions, they
trap him into obvious misstatements, and
not infrequently into a full admission. The
ethics of this method do not seem unim-
peachable, but when one hears talk about
“British fair-play” he will do well to remem-
ber the “fair-play” of the gang of buck-
larrikins who hit their victim on the head
with bottles, the sporting spirit of the sneak-
thief who picks a poor woman’s pocket or
steals a child’s money-box. As these sow,
surely should they reap, and some of them
certainly do reap their small crop in the
Gazette Room. But —and this is why De-
tective McMasters was angry—it is only a
suspect who may be treated so. Once a man
has been formally charged at the watch-
house counter he becomes “the accused,” and
is beyond the reach of their questions.
Dido, be it known, was a man upon whom
they wished very particularly to “put the
acid.” Though they knew that he had
done the Willalong post office safe robbery,
they wanted evidence. They wanted to find
out how he had managed to get from Mel-
bourne to Willalong, a distance of nearly 200
miles, in three days, without going by train.
They wanted to obtain a “statement” from
Dido which they could confute by investiga-
tion. Unless they could do this, their case
had broken down at the very outset. A
jury will not send a man to gaol for seven
years or so because the detectives tell it
that they know this man is guilty of the
offence alleged against him. A jury, unrea-
sonably enough, requires proof—strong
proof. And for these reasons Smithwick bad
to endure McMasters’s contumely. He had
taken from the detective the chance of con-
victing Dido out of Dido’s own mouth.
McMasters got across to the watchhouse
in time to see Dido signing the “property-
sheet” at the counter, the contents of his
pockets turned out before him by his captor.
As soon as the “crook” saw the detective he
scowled, and put his hand over his mouth.
“Hullo, Dido!” said McMasters. “What’s
the trouble -this time?”
Dido kept his hand over his, mouth, but
his little black eyes snapped viciously, full
of unuttered bad language.
“Come on, Dido, you know who I am,”
said McMasters.
“No, I don’t,” said Dido. “I s’pose you’re
a scarlet John Hop. Well, Mr. Hop, you
gets notning from me. I’m oyster.”
“I don’t want to ask you anything, my
lad,” replied the detective. “You’re under
arrest. A man of your experience ought to
know what we can do.”
“I know bloomin’ well what you can do,
Mr. bloomin’ McMasters. An’ I know you
don’t not me this d—n time.”
“Thought you didn’t know me,” said the
detective, smiling.
Dido put his hand over his mouth again.
Not another word would he speak, even to
the detective’s sneers. At length, “Take him
away,” said McMasters, and both left the
counter in thoroughly bad tempers, Dido for
his cell, and the detective for the office.
On the steps of the watchhouse, however, he
met Daley, the roundsman of The Clarion.
They went down into the city together, and,
sitting in the parlor of an hotel, McMasters
spat out all the bitterness in his soul con-
cerning the blundering Smithwick.
“Anything good in his pockets?” inquired
Daley, when Smithwick had been finally dis-
posed of.
“Haven’t looked at them yet,” replied the
detective, emptying Dido’s property on the
table, where both men examined it.
“Hullo!” cried the reporter. He handed
over Dido’s pocket-book, open at a page on
which a rough plan had been drawn.
“Why,” said McMasters, “d —d if this isn’t
the plan of Willalong post office. This ought
to settle Dido. I must go and show this
to the boss at once.”
The two parted at the hotel door, Daley
to write up the poor story of Dido’s arrest.
“Say nothing about this plan,” said McMas-
ters, and the reporter nodded.
The Superintendent of the C.l. Branch did
not take the plan in Dido’s pocket-book at
all in the manner expected by his subordin-
ate.
“Take it away! Take the d —d thing
away and burn it, my boy,” he said. “I’ll
have nothing to do with it. I’ll never have
anything to do with this sort of thing. Take
it away! Good God!”
“D’ you mean to say,” snorted McMasters,
“that you think I put the blasted thing
there?”
The Superintendent chuckled. “Didn’t
you, my boy? Well, well! Now, don’t get
hot under the collar. I like your cheek,
Mac. But remember the row when Dick
Murdock put the watch in Turn Jewell’s
room, and got Turn three years for it. No,
no, it’s far too risky.”
“So help me, God” began McMasters,
but Superintendent Turnbull waved his
hands blandly.
“I’ll have nothing to do with it, Mac,” he
said. “That ends it.”
The detective met Daley again late that
night. “Turnbull thinks I put that plan
there,” he said.
Daley laughed. “Didn’t you?” he asked.
“You chaps do such funny things some-
times.”
“I’ll swear I didn’t. But it knocks the
police case in. Unless I can get the brute
to admit before witnesses that he drew the
thing, I can’t show it to a jury. You know

He remembered, of course, that tell-tale plan

Dido’s eyes glistened. He was about to

As if by chance he came upon the page
whex’e Dido’s plan was displayed. Dido

“What’s that troublin’ you?” he asked.
, “Nothing! Nothing!” replied the detec-

Dido came across and stood behind his
chair. “Oh that!” he said, laughing.
“I’m a bill-poster by trade, and that’s a plan
of one of my boardings.”
“Get out!” said McMasters, “You never
drew that.”
“Yes, I did,” replied Dido. “That’s a
hoarding dowq Footscray I pasted last
year.”
The detective closed the book suddenly.
“ ’Fraid I can’t let you have this book, Dido,”
he said, and Dido was taken back to his
cell in a very bad temper. When he had
been safely locked in, the sergeant’s curiosity
was satisfied. -
The detective laughed, and smacked his
leg.
“That plan, Jim, is the plan of Willalong
post office. Look at it. The doors, the
front room, the office, the counter, and the
safe, there in the corner. I had to get
from Dido’s own mouth that he had drawn
it, with someone for a witness. Dido
wouldn’t open his mouth. So I didn’t
father I reckoned I could scare him, and
I did. Speech, Jim, is silver, they say, and
silence is golden. And it’s silence that scares
your crook worse than bounce or bluff, or
question. It’s silence that’s going to con-
vict Mr. Dido Connelly and put him where
he belongs for five or more quiet years.”

KODAK.
New Moon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A DRONE of voices, and a drone
Of organ music rising to
A glad triumphant pealing tone—
And I am married, dear, to you !
_ So strange it seems to be so true,
I vaguely fear it may not last..
But here’s the train. We’re moving. .
Phew !
Thank Heaven, that ordeal’s past!
Ah. now I hold you close, and know
The strange sweet tiling is really true.
With gratitude I burn and glow ;
I own all ecstasy—that’s you.
These trains are rotten slow, but who
Knows? —we may get somewhere next week.
Ive got the snookiest room, dear. . .
Pooh !
We’re married now. Why can't I speak?
It’s just the maid, kid, with the tea.
What’s that? Don’t like? Good Lord!
who cares?
You blush, you know, bewitchingly.
I’ll get the tray. Our shoes (two pairs)
Are winking slyly at the stairs,
f hat maid smiled roguishly, the goose;
She ~etter far think of her prayers
Ilian let her thoughts go on the loose.
I never saw you look so well—
It suits you, all that lacy stuff.
Great Scot! there goes the breakfast-bell,
les, we’ll miss breakfast, sure enough
Your hair’s delicious, when it’s rough-
And-tumble round your shoulders so.
Life may be one colossal bluff;
But you're the genuine thing, you know.
Your mouth —novv, hang if if I could
■ But say the things these fellows write!—
Your mouth would melt a man of wood,
Your eyes are heavy with delight.
You’re glad. You said so, dear, last night,
With little sobs and sighs of love,
Things unmistakable though slight
That Love best knows the meaning of.
And I’m content. Content!—l’d like
To shout my joy from sea to sea,
And give the other chaps the spike;
But that would he unkind of me.
T ve known a thousand girls, maybe
But you’re the best of all the bunch,
You’re absolute felicity...
D’ you think we should get up for lunch?
"MAORJXAND.
FRANK MORTON.
